The First Proof: Essential Impossibility Cannot Change
First proof from essential impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that the world cannot have been essentially impossible at any time, because essences do not change their intrinsic properties.

Core Idea
If something is essentially impossible, it can never become possible; therefore the world was never essentially impossible.

After the reductio argument, the author now provides a direct proof based on the nature of essences. This proof is foundational for the kalam argument for the world's creation, as it establishes that the world's essence was always possible, thus requiring an external cause to bring it into existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the passage starting from 'الذي يدل عليه وجوه' to the end. Notice that it is presented as the first proof.
The proof is concise and relies on the immutability of essences.
2
Understand the key principle
The proof states that the requisite of an essence (its intrinsic property) does not change. If an essence requires impossibility, it will always do so; if it requires possibility, it will always do so.
This is a metaphysical principle about essences.
3
Apply to the world
If the world were ever essentially impossible, it would remain impossible forever. But we know the world exists, so it was never essentially impossible. Hence its essence was always possible.
This implies that possibility preceded existence, but not in a temporal sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الخامسة في أن هذا العالم. هل له أول أم لا؟ القائلون بأنه يمتنع كون العالم قديماً ، ويجب كونه محدثاً : اضطربوا في إمكان وجود العالم . هل له أول ، أم لا ؟ فمنهم من قال : إنه لا أول لهذا الإمكان . ومنهم من قال : له أول · أما الذاهبون إلى القول الأول فقالوا : لو كذب قولنا : لا أول ، لإمكان العالم ، لصدق نقيضه ، وهو أنه لا بد لذلك الإمكان من أول . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، لزم أن يقال : إن قبل ذلك الأول، كان الإمكان مفقوداً، كان الحاصل، أما الوجوب بالذات أو الامتناع بالذات فإن كان الأول، كان القول بالقدم لازم ، وإن كان الثاني لزم أن يقال : العالم كان ممتنع الوجود لذاته [ ثم انقلب ممكن الوجود لذاته ] وهذا باطل . والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى : وهو أن كل ما كان ممتنع الوجود لعينه ولذاته امتنع أن يقبل الوجود البتة ، لأن مقتضى الماهية لا يتبدل ولا يتغير ، فإن كانت الماهية مقتضية لعدم قبول الوجود ، وجب أن تكون أبداً كذلك ، وإن كانت مقتضية لقبول الوجود ، وجب أن تكون أبداً كذلك . فيثبت : أن العالم لو صدق عليه في بعض الأوقات [ أنه ممتنع الوجود لذاته ] لصدق عليه الحكم في كل الأوقات، ولما كان التالي كاذباً، كان المقدم أيضاً كاذبا.
The Fifth Introduction: On whether this world has a beginning or not. Those who say that it is impossible for the world to be eternal and that it must be created disagreed concerning the possibility of the world's existence: Does it have a beginning or not? And if that is so, then it would necessitate saying: Before that beginning, the possibility was non-existent; what existed was either necessity by essence or impossibility by essence. So if the essence requires non-acceptance of existence, it must be always so; and if it requires acceptance of existence, it must be always so. Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth introductory discussion of whether the world has a beginning. It first lays out the disagreement among those who affirm that the world is created: some say the possibility of the world has no beginning (i.e., it was always possible), others say it does have a beginning. Thus the world's essence was never essentially impossible, meaning possibility preceded its existence.

?

The Dilemma of Modal Change
A second proof against modal change via a logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that disproves a change in the world's modal status from impossible to possible.

Core Idea
A change in modality is impossible; it leads either to absurdity or to denying the Creator.

This second argument tackles the possibility of a temporal change in the world's modal status. The author presents a powerful dilemma: if the world's essence could change from impossible to possible, either that change occurs contingently or necessarily. Both options lead to absurdities, ultimately undermining the proof for a Creator. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: a scenario followed by two conditional branches.
The argument is dense; focus on the flow of the dilemma.
2
Understand the initial scenario
The author assumes for argument's sake that the world was impossible at time T1 and then became possible at T2. This sets up the problem.
3
Examine the first horn: contingent change
If the new possibility occurs contingently (could have not occurred), then the possibility of the world's occurrence precedes the occurrence of this new possibility, meaning the world was possible before it became possible – a contradiction.
This horn is dismissed as absurd.
4
Examine the second horn: necessary change
If the possibility occurs necessarily, then times are similar and there is no reason for the change to happen exactly at T2. This is unintelligible. Moreover, if accepted, it allows that the world's existence could also be necessary, eliminating the need for a Creator.
This horn leads to denial of the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إنه لو كان العالم ممتنعاً لذاته في الوقت الأول ، ثم انقلب ممكناً لذاته في الوقت الثاني ، فذلك الإمكان إما أن يحدث مع جواز أن لا يحدث ، أو يحدث مع وجوب أن يحدث. فإن كان الأول كان إمكان حدوث هذا المكان سابقاً على حدوث هذا الإمكان يقتضي حصول الإمكان فقد كان الشيء ممكناً قبل كونه ممكناً وذلك محال ، وإن كان الثاني وهو أنه حدث مع وجوب أن يحدث ، فنقول : إن هذا غير معقول ، وبتقدير كونه معقولاً ، فإنه يقتضي نفي الصانع . أما أنه غير معقول ، فلأن الأوقات متشابهة متساوية ، فإن بتقدير أن يحدث قبل ذلك الوقت بتقدير يوم واحد ، لا يصير أزلياً . وإذا كانت الأوقات متشابهة متساوية ، كان القول بأنه ممتنع الحدوث قبل ذلك الوقت بتقدير يوم واحد ، وواجب الحدوث في ذلك الوقت بعينه : قول خارج عن العقل . وأما أن بتقدير صحته ، فإنه يلزم نفي الصانع وذلك محال لأنه لو جاز أن يقال : إنه حدث ذلك الإمكان في ذلك الوقت بعينه ، حدوثاً لا على سبيل الوجوب الذاتي ، فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يقال : إن وجود العالم حدث في ذلك الوقت بعينه حدوثاً على سبيل الوجوب الذاتي ؟ وحينئذ لا يمكن الإستدلال بحدوث المحدثات على افتقارها إلى الصانع ، وذلك يوجب نفي الصانع . فثبت بهذا أن هذا القول باطل.
The second proof: If the world were impossible by its essence in the first time, then it turned into possible by its essence in the second time, then that possibility either occurs with the possibility that it does not occur, or occurs with the necessity that it occurs. If it is the first, then the possibility of the occurrence of this 'makan' (place/world?) precedes the occurrence of this possibility necessitates obtaining the possibility, so the thing was possible before it was possible, and that is absurd. And if it is the second, meaning that it occurs with the necessity that it occurs, we say: This is unintelligible, and assuming it is intelligible, it necessitates the denial of the Creator. As for its being unintelligible, because times are similar and equal, for assuming that it occurs before that time by one day, it does not become eternal. And if times are similar and equal, the statement that it is impossible to occur before that time by one day, and necessary to occur at that exact time: is a statement outside of reason. And as for assuming its validity, it entails the denial of the Creator and that is absurd, because if it were permissible to say: That possibility occurred at that exact time, occurring not on the basis of essential necessity, why is it not also permissible to say: The existence of the world occurred at that exact time, occurring on the basis of essential necessity? At that point, it is not possible to argue from the occurrence of originated things for their dependence on the Creator, and that necessitates the denial of the Creator. So it is established by this that this statement is false.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a kalam proof (ḥujja) that the world's impossibility cannot turn into possibility. The author presents a dilemma. If the world were impossible at time T1 and then possible at T2, then the new possibility either occurs contingently (could have not occurred) or necessarily (must have occurred).

If contingent: then the possibility of the world's occurrence precedes the occurrence of this new possibility, meaning the world was possible before it became possible – a logical absurdity.

If necessary: Then the necessity rules out any alternative. But this is unintelligible because times are essentially similar and equal. There is no reason for the change to occur exactly at T2 rather than earlier or later. If it were necessary, it would apply at all times, making the world eternal or the change arbitrary. Moreover, if we accept that this possibility occurred necessarily, then we could just as well say the world's existence itself occurred necessarily at that time, thereby eliminating the need for a Creator and undermining the argument from contingency. Hence the claim is false.

Key Terms
إمكان
وجوب
أزلي
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a necessary change in possibility is a valid explanation for why the world became possible at a specific time.
Correction: The author shows that times are similar and equal, so there is no rational basis for a necessary change at one time over another; furthermore, it would allow the world's existence to be necessary, denying the Creator.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim would follow if the possibility occurs necessarily?
A) The world becomes eternal
B) Denial of the Creator
C) The world becomes impossible
D) The world becomes contingent

Key Takeaway
The world's modal status cannot change because any proposed change entails either a logical contradiction or the denial of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume that certain events in your life must happen a certain way (like a job or relationship), are you implicitly treating them as necessary by themselves? How does this argument challenge you to see them as contingent on Allah's will?
?
If you truly believed that the world's existence is entirely dependent on Allah at every moment, would you still worry about causes and effects as if they were independent? Where in your life do you act as if the world is self-sufficient?
?
The author argues that a change in possibility would make the need for a Creator dispensable. Have you ever considered that the regularity of nature might make God unnecessary in your practical life? What would change if you saw each event as a direct act of creation?

Objection to the Eternal Causality Argument and Initial Response
Introduces a two-fold objection to the eternal causality proof and begins the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-fold challenge presented to the eternal causality argument and the beginning of a direct response.

Core Idea
Objections to causal arguments come in two forms: answering them or countering them.

The previous proof for a necessary being relied on eternal causality. Here, an objector raises a twofold challenge: either the argument must be answered directly, or counter-arguments must be brought. The text begins to explore the first approach, though the full response is cut off.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: the objector is framing two possible lines of attack.
The passage ends mid-sentence.
2
Identify the two aspects
The objector distinguishes between giving a direct answer (jawab) and raising counter-arguments (mu'āraḍāt). This is a common dialectical method in kalam.
3
Understand the opening of the answer
The answer begins with 'Why may it not be said that...', suggesting the objector will propose an alternative interpretation that undermines the original proof.
The full answer is not here; we are only seeing the start.

Commentary
This passage introduces an objection to the previous proof. The objector states that the argument can be challenged in two ways: either by providing a direct answer (jawab) to the reasoning, or by raising counter-arguments (mu'āraḍāt). The objection suggests that perhaps the premises of the original argument are not necessary, and the answer begins with 'why may it not be said that...', indicating that the objector will propose an alternative interpretation of the conditions. Since the answer is incomplete, the full response is expected on the following pages.

Fifth Argument: Change from Impossibility to Possibility
Fifth argument: Change from impossibility to possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument that the world was impossible by itself before creation and then became possible, explaining why God's will did not attach earlier.

Core Idea
The world's state changed from impossibility to possibility by God's power alone.

This lesson presents a key argument from Islamic theology about why the world was not created earlier. The argument posits that before existence, the world was impossible in its own essence, and only through God's power did it become possible. This explains why divine will attached at the specific moment of creation, not before.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Notice that the passage is a single sentence with a logical sequence.
2
Identify the two states of the world
The passage describes the world before creation as 'impossible by its essence' and after as 'possible by its essence'. Spot these terms.
Key phrases: ممتنع الوجود (impossible of existence) and ممكن الوجود (possible of existence).
3
Connect to the divine will
The argument concludes that because the world was impossible, God's will did not attach to bringing it about earlier. Reflect on why will only attaches to possible things.
Will attaches only to possible objects.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider that this argument shows God's power to change the ontological status of things, defending why creation occurred when it did.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس في الجواب: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: العالم قبل أن حدث، كان ممتنع الوجود لعينه ولذاته، ثم انقلب ممكن الوجود لعينه ولذاته، فلهذا السبب لم تتعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإحداثه قبل الوقت الذي أحدثه فيه.
The fifth aspect of the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The world, before it came into existence, was impossible of existence by its essence and itself, then it turned into possible of existence by its essence and itself; for this reason the will of God, exalted, did not attach to bringing it about before the time in which He brought it about.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is the fifth argument, which posits that before creation, the world was impossible in itself (imtina' bi-dhatihi), and then it became possible (imkan bi-dhatihi). This change is not due to any external factor but by God's power. The will attaches only to possible things, so it could not attach when the world was impossible.

Key Terms
ممتنع الوجود
ممكن الوجود
لعينه ولذاته
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Things are either always possible or always impossible by their own essence.
Correction: The text argues that God can change a thing's ontological status from impossible to possible by His power, so possibility is not fixed.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, what was the state of the world before creation?
A) It was possible by itself.
B) It was impossible by itself.
C) It did not exist.
D) It was eternal.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument shows that God's will attaches only to possible things, and He changed the world from impossible to possible at the moment of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that some things in your life might be impossible not because of circumstances, but because of their very nature, and that only God can change that nature? How does that affect your prayers for the seemingly impossible?
?
This passage shows that God's will only attaches to what He first makes possible. Think of a blessing you are waiting for—could it be that God is preparing its possibility before granting it? Are you willing to trust His timing even when it seems delayed?
?
If God can change a thing from impossibility to possibility, what does that mean for your personal struggles with sin or weakness? Will you ask Him to transform your 'impossible' habits into possibilities for righteousness, knowing that His will only works on what He makes possible?

Seventh Argument: Presupposition of Pre-Eternal Time
Refutation by exposing presupposition of pre-eternal time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seventh argument refutes the objection about the timing of creation by exposing its hidden assumption of pre-eternal time.

Core Idea
The question 'why not before' only makes sense if time is eternal.

The seventh argument addresses a common objection: why did God create the world at a specific time and not earlier? The argument shows that this question itself assumes that time exists before creation, which is precisely the point of dispute between those who believe time is created and those who believe it is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...' and 'if not...then not...'.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage contrasts two views: one who affirms pre-eternity of time (qidam al-zaman) and one who believes time is created (zaman muhdath). Understand each stance.
3
Trace the logical move
The arguer shows that the questioner's question is valid only if time is pre-eternal. If time is created, there is no 'before' the first moment, so the question is impossible.
This is a refutation by exposing a presupposition.
4
Reflect on the refutation's power
This argument turns the question back on the questioner: the question itself assumes what the opponent denies. It does not prove time is created but shows the objection is invalid unless one already accepts pre-eternity.
5
Connect to the broader discussion
Consider how this relates to the larger debate on divine creation and the nature of time. Why is it important to establish that time is created?
This argument is one of many in a series.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع في الجواب: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن قول القائل: لم تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت، ولم تتعلق بإحداثه في الوقت المتقدم على ذلك الوقت، إنما يصح لو حصل قبل ذلك الوقت وقت آخر. وهكذا قبل كل وقت وقت آخر لا إلى أول. وهذا الكلام إنما يصح لو ثبت القول بقدم الزمان، وهذا هو عين محل النزاع. فإن من اعتقد أن الزمان محدث، اعتقد أنه لم يحصل قبل ذلك الأول وقت آخر البتة، وعلى هذا التقدير امتنع أن يقال: ثم لم يحدث العالم قبل أن حدث؟ لأن هذا الكلام إنما يصح لو حصل قبل ذلك الوقت وقت آخر، فإذا لم يكن الأمر كذلك امتنع قول القائل: لم لم تتعلق إرادة الله بإحداث العالم قبل ذلك الوقت؟
The seventh aspect of the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The statement of the one who says, 'Why did God's will attach to bringing about the world at that time, and did not attach to bringing it about at the time prior to that time?' is only valid if there was another time before that time. And likewise, before every time, another time, ad infinitum. This statement is only valid if one affirms the pre-eternity of time, and that is precisely the point of dispute. For one who believes that time is created believes that there was no other time before that first time at all. On this estimation, it is impossible to say, 'Then why did He not bring about the world before it existed?' because this statement is only valid if there was another time before that time. Since the matter is not so, the statement of the one who says, 'Why did God's will not attach to bringing about the world before that time?' is impossible.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is the seventh argument, which refutes the question by pointing out that it presupposes the pre-eternity of time. If time is created and finite, there is no 'before' the first moment. Therefore, the question 'why not before?' is meaningless because there was no time before creation. This argument depends on the doctrine that time is created.

Key Terms
الزمان
قدم الزمان
زمان محدث
نزاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that time is an infinite continuum, so there is always a 'before' for any moment.
Correction: This argument shows that the question 'why not before' assumes time is pre-eternal. If time is created, there is no 'before' the first moment, so the question is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh argument, what assumption makes the question about a prior time valid?
A) The belief that time is created.
B) The belief in the pre-eternity of time.
C) The belief that God is eternal.
D) The belief that the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God could have created the world earlier only works if time exists prior to creation; if time is created, there is no 'earlier'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever questioned God's timing in your own life, assuming that an earlier moment would have been better? How does this argument challenge you to re-examine whether your question is based on assuming a framework that Allah denies?
?
If there is truly no 'before' the first moment of creation, what does that imply about God's independence from time? How should this affect your trust in His perfect wisdom for every occurrence in your life, even when you do not see the reason?
?
This passage shows that some questions are invalid because they rest on false assumptions. Are there any questions or criticisms you have about Allah's decree that might be rooted in assumptions you have not examined? Will you surrender those questions in humility before the Creator?

The Counterargument Against the Philosophers Begins
Al-Razi transitions to counterarguments: the same problem of temporal specification applies to the philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi shifts the argument about temporal specification to apply it against the philosophers.

Core Idea
The philosophers cannot escape the same objection they raised against the theologians.

Al-Razi now turns to counterarguments. He takes the core objection about specifying a time for creation and shows that it equally entangles the philosophers. This lesson sets up the structure of the second position, announcing that the same reasoning will be turned against the philosophers in the following discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the transition phrase 'وأما المقام الثاني' – this marks a new section. Notice how al-Razi summarizes the previous argument and then declares its applicability to the philosophers.
The text ends abruptly; the full counterarguments appear on the next page.
2
Identify the logical move
Al-Razi is using a tu quoque strategy: if the theologians must provide a preponderator for the specific time, then the philosophers face the same requirement for any temporal specification they propose.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why this is an effective counterargument. It shows consistency and challenges the philosophers' own assumptions about causality and time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني وهو ما يتعلق بالمعارضات : فنقول : قصاري الحجة المذكورة أن يقال : إن تخصيص إحداث العالم بوقت معين ، مع كون ذلك الوقت مساوياً لسائر الأوقات ، يوجب رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح ، فنقول : هذا أيضاً لازم على الفلاسفة وبيانه من وجوه :
As for the second position, which concerns counterarguments, we say: The utmost of the mentioned argument is to say that the specification of the creation of the world at a particular time, despite that time being equal to all other times, necessitates a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator. So we say: This is also necessary for the philosophers, and its explanation is through several aspects:
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage introduces the second part of the discussion (al-maqam al-thani), dealing with counterarguments (mu'aradat). It summarizes the objection that specifying a time for creation requires a preponderance without a cause, and then states that this same objection applies to the philosophers. The explanation will be given in several aspects, but the text on this page cuts off.

Key Terms
معارضات
تخصيص
فلاسفة
رجحان
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the temporal specification objection only applies to theologians who believe in a temporal creation.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the objection also applies to philosophers, because they too must account for any temporal specification in their system.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the second position (al-maqam al-thani)?
A) To refute the previous nine responses.
B) To show that the objection applies to philosophers as well.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi announces that the temporal specification objection applies to philosophers as well, setting up his counterarguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi turns the tables on the philosophers, showing that their own position suffers from the same problem they criticized. Is there an area in your life where you have been quick to judge others for a flaw while ignoring the same flaw in yourself? What would it take for you to honestly examine that double standard today?
?
The objection centers on having a sufficient reason for a preference. Think of a major decision you made recently. Did you have a clear reason, or was it arbitrary? How does the idea of a wise Creator who specifies all things with purpose challenge you to reflect on the purposefulness of your own choices?

The First Counter-Argument: The Problem of the Poles
Presents the first counter-argument using celestial poles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi turns the argument of preference without preponderator against the philosophers by pointing to the arbitrary designation of poles on a homogeneous celestial sphere.

Core Idea
The same problem of arbitrary preference applies to the philosophers' own cosmology.

Al-Razi introduces the second station of the discussion, where he presents counter-arguments against the philosophers. He begins by stating the original argument and then shows that it equally applies to the philosophers' own assumptions. The first example concerns the celestial sphere: if all points are similar, why are two specific points chosen as poles? This mirrors the alleged arbitrary choice of a time for creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the style: Al-Razi states the original argument, then claims it applies to the philosophers, and gives a specific example.
Pay attention to the parallel structure: 'We say: This is also binding upon the philosophers...'
2
Identify the logical structure
Break the passage into three parts: (1) restatement of the original argument, (2) assertion that it applies to the philosophers, (3) the first example concerning celestial poles.
The example is given as 'The first' – there will be more in subsequent passages.
3
Analyze the parallel
Consider why the problem of arbitrary pole designation is analogous to the arbitrary time of creation. Both involve selecting one possibility among equal alternatives without a reason.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Understand how this counter-argument turns the philosophers' own principles against them. It shows they cannot escape the problem they raise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني وهو ما يتعلق بالمعارضات: فنقول: قصاري الحجة المذكورة أن يقال: إن تخصيص إحداث العالم بوقت معين، كون ذلك الوقت مساوياً لسائر الأوقات، يوجب رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح، فنقول: هذا أيضاً لازم على الفلاسفة وبيانه من وجوه: الأول: إن جرم الفلك جرم بسيط عند القوم، فتكون جميع النقط فيه متشابهة، فاختصاص نقطتين معينتين من تلك النقط الغير المتناهية بالقطبية دون سائر النقط، يوجب رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح.
As for the second station, which concerns the counter-arguments, we say: The utmost of the mentioned argument is that it can be said: The specification of the creation of the world to a specific time, while that time is equal to all other times, necessitates the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderator. We say: This is also binding upon the philosophers, and its clarification is through several aspects: The first: The body of the celestial sphere is a simple body according to them, so all points in it are similar. Therefore, the specification of two specific points among those infinite points as poles to the exclusion of other points necessitates the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the second station of the discussion, which presents counter-arguments against the philosophers' position. The author states that the original argument (that specifying a time for creation involves an unjustified preference) applies equally to the philosophers' own cosmology. The first example is that the celestial sphere, being a simple body (homogeneous), has all points similar, yet two specific points are designated as poles. This arbitrary designation parallels the alleged arbitrary time of creation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The problem of 'preference without preponderator' only applies to theistic arguments about creation in time.
Correction: Al-Razi shows the same problem arises in the philosophers' own system, specifically in their explanation of celestial poles. This refutes the claim that only theism faces this issue.

Checkpoint
What is the first counter-argument?
A) The celestial sphere has infinite points
B) The specification of two poles among similar points is arbitrary
C) The celestial sphere is not simple
D) Time is not equal

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi begins his counter-arguments by demonstrating that the problem of arbitrary preference is not unique to theism but also appears in the philosophers' own cosmology, as shown by the arbitrary designation of celestial poles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume something is obviously necessary (like the Earth's poles being fixed), do you ever consider that your certainty might rest on an arbitrary convention? The philosophers' 'self-evident' poles were shown to be arbitrary. What in your own worldview might be similarly arbitrary but feels necessary? Describe one such assumption and what it would mean if it were not actually necessary.
?
Al-Razi used the philosophers' own principles to expose a blind spot in their reasoning. Think of a person you often disagree with (or a group you critique). Can you honestly identify a flaw in your own position that mirrors the flaw you see in theirs? Write down one such mirror flaw and commit to examining it with the same scrutiny you apply to others.
?
If you had to defend the consistency of your own beliefs using the same logical standards you demand of others, where might you stumble? Take one specific belief (about God, creation, or morality) and trace it to a foundational assumption. Is that assumption truly self-evident, or could it be a preference without a preponderator? Sit with that discomfort for a moment.

Second Counter-Argument: Arbitrariness of a Single Orbit
Second counter-argument: infinite equal orbits make specific motion arbitrary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second counter-argument against the philosophers: that the selection of one specific orbit among infinite equal orbits is rationally arbitrary without a preponderator.

Core Idea
If all orbits are equal, choosing one without reason is arbitrary.

This lesson presents the second counter-argument against the philosophers' view of celestial motion. It argues that the celestial sphere, being a simple body, is equally capable of motion in any direction or orbit. Yet we observe motion on one specific orbit, which would be a preference without a preponderator — a rational problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: premise, examples, conclusion.
2
Understand the premise
The celestial sphere is a simple body, so it is equally capable of motion in all directions (east-west, north-south, and infinite other orbits).
Equality of orbits is key.
3
Identify the problem
The occurrence of motion on one specific orbit, while all are equal, necessitates a preponderator. Without one, the choice is arbitrary.
This mirrors the earlier argument about the time of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إن جميع المدارات المفترضة في الفلك متساوية، لأن الفلك جسم بسيط، فكما يكون قابلا للحركة من المشرق إلى المغرب، فكذلك يكون قابلا للحركة من الشمال إلى الجنوب، وكذلك القول في سائر المدارات التي لا نهاية لها، فوقوع الحركة على مدار واحد بعينه مع كونه مساوياً لسائر المدارات التي لا نهاية لها، يوجب رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح.
The second: All the assumed orbits in the celestial sphere are equal, because the celestial sphere is a simple body. Just as it is capable of motion from east to west, so is it capable of motion from north to south, and likewise with regard to the rest of the infinite orbits. So the occurrence of motion on one specific orbit, while it is equal to all the other infinite orbits, necessitates the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the second counter-argument against the philosophers. It argues that if the celestial sphere is homogeneous, then all possible orbits (including those in different directions) are equally possible. Yet we observe motion on one particular orbit (e.g., east-west). This specific choice, like the specific time of creation, would be a preference without a preponderator, a problem for the philosophers.

Key Terms
المدارات
متساوية
قابلا للحركة
مشرق
مغرب
شمال
جنوب
مدار واحد
لا نهاية لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the motion of the celestial sphere is simple; here the author argues that there are many possible motions and the choice of one requires a reason.
Correction: The celestial sphere's simplicity does not restrict it to one orbit; many are equally possible, so the actual choice needs a preponderator.

Checkpoint
The second counter-argument criticizes the choice of which specific motion?
A) Motion from east to west
B) Motion on one specific orbit among infinite possibilities
C) Motion of the stars
D) Motion around the poles

Key Takeaway
The second counter-argument shows that the specific orbit of celestial motion, like the specific time of creation, would be arbitrary without a divine preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you made a decision between two seemingly equal paths without seeking a definitive reason? How does this lesson's logic expose the potential arbitrariness of that choice?
?
The philosophers assumed the east-west motion was natural and needed no cause. What assumptions in your life do you hold simply because they feel 'natural,' and how might the necessity of a preponderator apply to them?
?
If a simple, homogeneous sphere cannot determine its own motion without an external cause, what does that imply about your need for divine guidance? In what area of life are you acting without a sufficient reason from Allah's signs?

Arbitrary Position of a Star: The Third Counter-Argument
Presents the third counter-argument about the location of a star on a homogeneous sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third counter-argument against the philosophers' view, which highlights the problem of arbitrary placement of a celestial body on a homogeneous sphere.

Core Idea
A planet's specific location on a uniform sphere is arbitrary and lacks a sufficient reason.

This lesson presents the third counter-argument from the theologian's critique of natural causation. The argument focuses on the position of a planet within the celestial sphere. If the sphere is simple and all its parts are alike, why should a planet occupy one particular spot rather than any other? This challenges the notion of inherent causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise argument structure.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the premises
The argument has two premises: (1) the planet is fixed in a specific location, and (2) all parts of the sphere are similar because it is simple.
These are stated explicitly.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that this specific placement constitutes a preponderance of one possibility over another without a preponderator, i.e., without a cause.
This is the problematic point for the philosophers.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Why is this a problem? If the sphere is truly uniform, there is no reason for the planet to be in one place rather than another. This violates the principle of sufficient reason.
5
Address the common misunderstanding
Some might think positions are natural, but the argument shows they are arbitrary if the sphere is homogeneous.
This connects to the misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إنَّ الكوكب مركوز في موضع معين من أجزاء الفلك، وجميع الأجزاء المفترضة في الفلك متشابهة. لما بينا: أنه بسيط. فحصول النقرة التي المكان لذلك الكوكب في ذلك الجانب المعين من ذلك الفلك، دون سائر الجوانب، يكون ترجيحاً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح.
The third: The planet is fixed in a specific location among the parts of the celestial sphere, while all the assumed parts of the celestial sphere are similar, because we have shown that it is simple. So the occurrence of the hollow that is the place for that planet in that specific side of that celestial sphere, to the exclusion of other sides, constitutes a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
The third counter-argument focuses on the position of a planet within the celestial sphere. Since the sphere is simple and homogeneous, all parts are essentially similar. Yet we find that a planet occupies a specific spot (like a hollow) in a particular side. This specific location, rather than any other, is arbitrary and thus problematic for the philosophers' system.

Fourth Counter-Argument: Epicycle and Varying Thickness
Presents the fourth counter-argument against the epicycle model.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth counter-argument against the epicycle model: that the varying thickness of the complement body implies an arbitrary preponderance without a preponderator.

Core Idea
A simple body cannot have arbitrary thickness variation; that would require a preponderator.

The fourth counter-argument targets the epicycle model used by astronomers. It examines what happens when an epicycle is separated by an eccentric sphere, leaving two complements with uneven thickness. Since these complements are simple bodies, this unevenness is problematic—it suggests a preference without a cause. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. The argument is concise, so take your time to grasp each phrase.
Notice the structure: 'the fourth' introduces the objection, followed by the scenario and its implications.
2
Identify the components
Identify the epicyclic sphere, the eccentric sphere, and the two complements (inner and outer). Understand how they relate.
The terms 'falak mumaththil' (epicycle) and 'falk kharij al-markaz' (eccentric sphere) are key.
3
Understand the objection
Why is varying thickness a problem? The complement is a simple body with one nature. A single nature cannot produce two opposite qualities (thickness and thinness) in different parts without an external cause.
This connects to the principle of causality: any preponderance needs a preponderator.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
The argument challenges the astronomers' model by showing it implies an arbitrary preference, which is philosophically unacceptable.
This is part of the larger kalam criticism of Greek astronomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إن الفلك الممثل إذا انفصل عن ثخنه فلك خارج المركز، فإنه يبقى منه متممان: أحدهما: من داخل. والآخر: من خارج، والمتمم يكون مختلف الثخن لا محالة، وذلك المتمم في نفسه جسم بسيط، والبسيط له طبيعة واحدة، ثم إن تلك الطبيعة اقتضت في أحد جانب ذلك المتمم ثخناً عظيماً، وفي الجانب الآخر منه رقة عظيمة وذلك يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح.
The fourth: When the epicyclic sphere is separated from its thickness by the eccentric sphere, there remain from it two complements: one from the inside, and the other from the outside. The complement is necessarily of varying thickness. That complement is in itself a simple body, and the simple has one nature. Then that nature required in one side of that complement a great thickness, and in the other side a great thinness. And that necessitates a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
The fourth counter-argument deals with the epicycle model used by astronomers. The author describes a scenario: when the epicyclic sphere is separated from its thickness by the eccentric sphere, two complements remain (inner and outer). These complements, though simple bodies, have varying thickness (one side thick, the other thin). This variation is a form of preference without a preponderator, which is problematic.

Correction: This argument shows that the model implies an arbitrary distribution of thickness, violating the principle that every preponderance requires a preponderator.

The Fifth Counter-Argument: Homogeneous Sphere with Two Surface Types
Begins the fifth counter-argument: homogeneous sphere with differing surfaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author begins to challenge natural causality by pointing out that a homogeneous sphere has both convex and concave surfaces, which are different.

Core Idea
A homogeneous sphere producing two opposite surface curvatures is rationally puzzling.

The fifth counter-argument starts a new line of attack against the idea that natures alone determine all properties. Even if we accept that the celestial sphere has a single homogeneous nature, that nature somehow gives rise to two opposite surface shapes — convex outside and concave inside. This distinction seems arbitrary and raises a difficulty for strict natural causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation. Note that the passage is incomplete; the argument breaks off mid-sentence.
The Arabic ends with 'في أمور:' indicating a list that is not given here.
2
Identify the premises
The passage contains two premises: (1) The celestial sphere is homogeneous in nature. (2) That nature requires a convex surface on the outside and a concave surface on the inside.
The conclusion — that these surfaces differ — is introduced but not elaborated.
3
Reflect on the conceptual difficulty
Think about why a homogeneous nature would produce two different types of surface curvature. This is the point of the counter-argument: it seems to involve an arbitrary preference.
The author uses this to cast doubt on whether natures are sufficient to explain all features of the cosmos.

The Argument from Natural Instinct for Infinite Space
Argues that natural instinct about direction implies infinite voids and a need for a preferrer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the natural instinct's judgment about spatial direction leads to the necessity of infinite voids outside the world and requires a divine preferrer.

Core Idea
Natural instinct's judgments are true; north-south distinction forces infinite space and a preferrer.

In this passage, the author tackles skepticism head-on: if we dismiss our natural convictions as mere imagination, we fall into sophistry. He then establishes a key principle—the original natural instinct (الفطرة الأصلية) is reliable. Using the simple example of north and south being distinct, he builds an argument that necessarily leads to the existence of infinite voids beyond the world, and ultimately to the need for a voluntary agent who placed the world in a specific location.

Guided Path
1
Refute the skeptic's claim
Read the opening lines where the author rejects the claim that our convictions come from false imagination. Understand why he says this leads to sophistry.
This sets the stage for a first principle: the reliability of natural instinct.
2
Grasp the principle of original natural instinct
Identify the statement that everything judged by the original natural instinct is true and correct. This is the foundation of the argument.
The term 'original natural instinct' refers to primitive, untutored intuition.
3
Analyze the directional example
Consider the example: the side next to the North Pole is distinct from the side next to the South Pole. See how this judgment implies the existence of infinite spaces outside the world.
The distinction requires a framework of infinite voids to make sense.
4
Understand the conclusion about preference
Follow the final step: if the world is placed in one part of that void rather than another, that would be a preference without a preferrer, which is impossible. Therefore, a preferrer (Allah) must exist.
This is a classic kalam argument for a voluntary agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلمنا : أن قول القائل : إن هذا الجزم مقتضى الوهم الكاذب ، لا مقتضى العقل الصادق : يوجب السفسطة . وذلك باطل . فثبت : أن كل ما جزمت به الفطرة الأصلية ، كان حقاً وصدقاً . لكن الفطرة الأصلية جزمت بأن الجانب الذي يلي القطب الشمالي ، متميز عن الجانب الذي يلي القطب الجنوبي ، وهذا يوجب القطع بصحة أحياز لا نهاية لها خارج العالم . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : وقوع كرة العالم في بعض جوانب ذلك الخلاء دون البعض ، يكون رجحاناً لا لمرجح .
We know: that the statement of the one who says: 'This conviction is a requirement of false imagination, not of true reason' leads to sophistry. That is false. So it is established: that everything that the original natural instinct has judged is true and correct. But the original natural instinct has judged that the side that is next to the North Pole is distinct from the side that is next to the South Pole, and this necessitates the certainty of the existence of infinite spaces outside the world. And when this is established, we say: the occurrence of the sphere of the world in some parts of that void rather than others would be a preference without a preferrer.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a proof against skeptical claims that our convictions might stem from false imagination. It begins by stating that such a claim leads to sophistry and is therefore false. It then asserts that every judgment of the original natural instinct (الفطرة الأصلية) is true and correct. As an example, the natural instinct judges a distinction between the north side and the south side. This judgment implies that there must be infinite spaces (أحياز لا نهاية لها) outside the world to account for the directional difference. If the world is placed in one part of that void rather than another, that would be a preference (رجحان) without a preferrer (مرجح), which is impossible. Thus, the argument shows the necessity of a voluntary agent who chooses the world's location.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Our convictions about direction might be from false imagination and thus unreliable.
Correction: The passage shows that dismissing natural instinct leads to sophistry, and the original natural instinct's judgments are true.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would change if you saw every natural preference as a divine invitation?

The Eighth Argument: Sperm and the Need for a Voluntary Agent
Two-case argument from sperm's nature to a voluntary agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of sperm development, whether simple or composite, demonstrates the necessity of a voluntary (conscious) agent.

Core Idea
A blind natural power cannot produce distinct organs; a conscious agent is needed.

This lesson presents the eighth argument from Al-Matalibul Aliyah, which uses the formation of organs from sperm to argue for a voluntary creator. The argument considers two possibilities for the nature of sperm—simple or composite—and shows that in both cases, the observed diversity of organs cannot be explained by an unconscious natural power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall structure. Notice that the argument has two conditional cases.
The passage is concise; each case is introduced with 'if' (إن).
2
Identify the first case: simple sperm
The first scenario assumes sperm is a simple body. The formative power (القوة المصورة) is blind, yet it produces different organs (heart, brain) in different parts. Why is this a problem? Because a blind power cannot preferentially direct different outcomes without a conscious guide.
Key term: الرجحان بلا مرجح (preference without a preferrer).
3
Identify the second case: composite sperm
The second scenario assumes sperm is composite of simple elements. Each simple element, when acted upon by a blind power, would produce a spherical shape. But we observe non-spherical organs (heart, brain). This again is a preference without a preferrer.
The spherical shape is the only natural outcome from a uniform cause in simple matter.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Both cases lead to the same conclusion: the observed diversity cannot be explained by an unconscious natural power. Therefore, a voluntary agent (God) must be responsible for directing the formation.
This argument is purely rational, not dependent on empirical biology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : النطفة . إما أن تكون جسماً بسيطاً أو لا تكون ، فإن كان الأول فنقول : القوة المصورة عند القوم قوة عديمة الشعور والإدراك ، فتأثيرها في حصول الصورة القلبية وبعض أجزاء تلك النطفة ، وحصول الصورة الدماغية في الجزء الآخر منها ، يكون رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن لا المرجح ، وإن كان الثاني وهو أن لا تكون النطفة جسماً بسيطاً ، فنقول : إذا لم يكن بسيطاً كان مركباً ، والمركب مركب عن البسائط ، فكل واحد من تلك البسائط إذا تكون عنه عضو ، وجب أن يكون ذلك العضو كرة ، لأن تأثير القوة الخالية عن الشعور والإدراك في المادة البسيطة ، لا يكون إلا تأثيراً واحداً ، وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، بل حصلت أشكال مختلفة ، ومخالفة لشكل الكرة ، فحينئذ يلزم منه رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر ، لا لمرجح
The eighth: the sperm. Either it is a simple body or not. If the first, we say: the formative power according to them is a power devoid of sensation and perception, so its effect in producing the heart-image in some parts of that sperm and producing the brain-image in the other part of it would be a preference of one of the two possible sides without a preferrer. And if the second, that is, the sperm is not a simple body, we say: if it is not simple, it is composite, and the composite is composed of simple [elements]. So each of those simple [elements], if an organ is formed from it, that organ must be spherical, because the effect of a power devoid of sensation and perception in simple matter is only one effect. But when the matter is not so, rather different shapes occur, contrary to the spherical shape, then this entails a preference of one of the two possible sides without a preferrer.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument, which uses the example of the sperm (النطفة) to argue for a voluntary agent. It considers two cases. First, if the sperm is a simple body, the formative power (القوة المصورة) is blind (devoid of sensation and perception). Yet it produces different organs (heart and brain) in different parts of the same simple substance, which is a preference (رجحان) without a preferrer (مرجح). Second, if the sperm is composite of simple elements, each simple element would, when formed into an organ, necessarily produce a spherical shape, because a blind power in simple matter can only produce a uniform effect. But we observe different shapes (e.g., heart, brain) that are not spherical. This again is a preference without a preferrer. Both cases show that a conscious agent is needed to direct the formation.

Key Terms
النطفة
القوة المصورة
الرجحان بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about biological development or empirical embryology.
Correction: The argument is purely rational: it uses the observed fact of diverse organs to demonstrate that a blind natural power cannot explain the diversity, so a conscious agent must be involved.

Checkpoint

A) It cannot develop at all.

C) It would not be able to form a brain.
D) The heart would disappear.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Ninth Argument: Refuting Necessary Concomitance
Proves that if a perfect cause necessarily entails its effect, the cause would be contingent, which is absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that a perfect cause necessarily entails its effect is refuted through a reductio argument showing it would make the cause contingent.

Core Idea
If perfect causes necessitated effects, even God would be contingent.

This lesson presents a sophisticated reductio argument against the necessitarian view of causality. The author shows that if a perfect cause always produced its effect immediately, the cause would have to be contingent—an impossibility for the Necessary Being. We'll walk through the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
The claim is that a perfect cause necessarily entails its effect immediately, with no possibility of separation. This is the view the argument aims to refute.
2
Observe the empirical premise
We observe that forms and accidents cease to exist. This is an undeniable fact that grounds the argument.
3
Trace the contrapositive reasoning
From the non-existence of effects, we infer the non-existence of their causes via the logical rule: if the consequent (effect) is absent, the antecedent (cause) must also be absent.
4
Examine the two cases for the cause
The cause must be either necessary in itself or possible in itself. The argument shows both lead to contradiction: necessary would become contingent; possible leads to infinite regress and absurdity.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Since both cases are impossible, the original claim is false: it is not necessary that the effect occurs with the perfect cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : لو لزم من حصول المؤثر التام حصول الأثر معه بحيث يمتنع انفكاك ذلك المؤثر عن ذلك الأثر لزم كون ذلك المؤثر ممكن الوجود وهذا محال فذلك محال . بيان الشرطية : إنا نشاهد أن هذه الصور والأعراض قد تبطل بعد وجودها . وإن كان كذلك فحينئذ يلزم من عدمها عدم لوازم تلك المؤثرات ويلزم من عدم اللازم عدم الملزوم . ينتج : أنه يلزم من عدم هذه الصور والأعراض عدم مؤثراتها . ثم ذلك المؤثر إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته أو كان ممكناً لذاته فإن كان واجباً لذاته فحينئذ يلزم من عدم هذه الصور والأعراض عدم الموجود الذي هو واجب الوجود لذاته لكن كل ما كان قابلاً للعدم لم يكن واجب الوجود لذاته أن الواجب لذاته ليس واجباً لذاته هذا خلف . وأما إن كان المؤثر في هذه الصور والأعراض الزائلة أشياء ليست واجبة الوجود لذواتها بل كانت ممكنة لذواتها فنقول : يلزم من عدم هذه الآثار والصفات عدم ما كان مؤثراً منها ثم عدمه لا بد وأن يكون أيضاً لعدم مؤثره ولا يزال يكون عدم كل معلول أسفل لعدم علته التي هي فوقه حتى ينتهي إلى العلة الأولى وحينئذ يلزم من عدم هذه الآثار عدم ذاته وذلك باطل . فيثبت : أنه لا يلزم من حصول المؤثر التام وجوب حصول الأثر معه .
The ninth: If from the occurrence of the perfect cause the occurrence of the effect necessarily followed such that it is impossible for that cause to be separated from that effect, then that cause would be contingent in existence, and this is absurd, so that is absurd. Explanation of the conditional: We observe that these forms and accidents cease after their existence. If that is so, then from their non-existence follows the non-existence of the concomitants of those causes, and from the non-existence of the consequent follows the non-existence of the antecedent. It results: that from the non-existence of these forms and accidents follows the non-existence of their causes. Then that cause is either necessary in itself or possible in itself. If it is necessary in itself, then from the non-existence of these forms and accidents follows the non-existence of an entity that is necessary in itself, but everything that is capable of non-existence is not necessary in itself. So the necessary in itself would not be necessary in itself—a contradiction. And if the cause of these perishable forms and accidents is something not necessary in itself but possible in itself, we say: from the non-existence of these effects and attributes follows the non-existence of their cause, and then its non-existence must also be due to the non-existence of its cause, and the non-existence of each effect is lower than the non-existence of its cause which is above it, until it reaches the first cause. Then from the non-existence of these effects follows the non-existence of that [first cause] itself, and that is false. So it is established that it is not necessary from the occurrence of the perfect cause that the occurrence of the effect be concomitant with it.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the necessitarian view that a perfect cause (المؤثر التام) necessarily entails its effect immediately. The argument begins with a conditional: if that were true, then the cause would be contingent (ممكن الوجود) because the effect can cease, and by the rule of contrapositive, the cause would also cease. This is absurd because a necessary being cannot cease. The author explains: we see forms and accidents cease, so their non-existence implies the non-existence of their causes. Then he considers two possibilities for the cause: necessary in itself (واجب لذاته) or possible in itself (ممكن لذاته). If necessary, its non-existence is impossible, but the effect's non-existence would imply its non-existence, contradiction. If possible, then its non-existence would require a further cause, leading to an infinite regress ending at the first cause, which would then also have to cease, absurd. Therefore, it is not necessary that the effect occurs with the perfect cause. This refutes the claim of necessary temporal concomitance.

Key Terms
المؤثر التام
الأثر
الواجب لذاته
الممكن لذاته
اللازم
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies causality altogether or that effects depend on causes.
Correction: The argument only denies that a perfect cause necessitates its effect immediately and inseparably; it does not deny that effects depend on causes.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the ninth argument reach about the relationship between the perfect cause and its effect?
A) The effect must occur immediately.
B) The cause must be necessary.
C) It is not necessary that the effect occurs concomitantly with the perfect cause.
D) The cause is always contingent.

Key Takeaway
The ninth argument refutes the idea that a perfect cause must immediately produce its effect; otherwise, the cause would be contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something and it does not happen, do you assume that your actions (the 'cause') automatically failed, or do you consider that God's power is not bound by your expectations? How does this lesson's refutation of necessitation change your view of unanswered prayers?
?
Think of a habit or sin you feel 'caused' by your environment or upbringing. Are you using the idea of necessary causality as an excuse? How does the argument that no created cause necessitates its effect free you to take responsibility?
?
If God's actions are not necessitated by any cause, then your relationship with Him is not mechanical. Do you treat your faith as a set of causes and effects (good deeds guarantee reward) or as a personal response to a free and sovereign Lord? What would change if you truly believed this?

Ibn Sina on Cessation of Cause and Effect
Ibn Sina's response: effect's cessation as sign of cause's prior cessation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's distinction between causal and epistemic implications when the effect ceases.

Core Idea
Effect cessation is a sign, not a cause, of the cause's prior cessation.

Ibn Sina anticipates a common objection: if the effect's ceasing implies the cause's ceasing, doesn't that make the cause dependent on the effect? His reply from the Isharaat clarifies a crucial logical nuance — the implication is epistemic, not causal. Let's examine his precise wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text of Ibn Sina's response from the Isharaat carefully. Note the key terms for ceasing (ارتفاع) and indication (يدل).
The term 'الشيخ الرئيس' refers to Ibn Sina.
2
Understand his distinction
Ibn Sina distinguishes between saying the effect's ceasing *causes* the cause's ceasing vs. it *indicates* that the cause ceased first. Grasp this difference.
The objection only works if the implication is causal.
3
Apply the distinction
Reflect on how Ibn Sina's answer repels the objection. The effect's ceasing is a sign — we infer the cause ceased earlier. This preserves the causal order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب الشيخ الرئيس عن كلام يشبه هذا السؤال : وذلك لأنه قال في كتاب الإشارات: « إنه يلزم من فرض ارتفاع المعلول ارتفاع العلة ، لا على معنى أن ارتفاع المعلول علة لارتفاع العلة ، بل على معنى أن ارتفاع المعلول يدل على أن العلة ارتفعت أولاً ، حتى لزم من ارتفاعها ارتفاع المعلول ، وعلى هذا التقدير فقد اندفع السؤال » .
And the Chief Master (Ibn Sina) answered regarding a statement similar to this objection: That is because he said in the Book of Isharaat: 'From the assumption of the invalidation of the effect follows the invalidation of the cause, not in the sense that the invalidation of the effect is a cause for the invalidation of the cause, but in the sense that the invalidation of the effect indicates that the cause was invalidated first, such that from its invalidation the invalidation of the effect followed. On this estimation, the objection is repelled.'
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's (الشيخ الرئيس) response from his Isharaat to an objection similar to the one raised in the ninth argument. He clarifies that when we say 'the invalidation of the effect implies the invalidation of the cause,' we do not mean that the effect's invalidation is the cause of the cause's invalidation. Rather, it is a sign (يدل) that the cause was invalidated first. The logical direction is: the cause ceased, then the effect ceased. Thus, the objection that this would make the cause contingent is avoided because the implication is epistemic, not causal.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the effect's ceasing implies the cause's ceasing, then the cause is made dependent on the effect, undermining causality.
Correction: Ibn Sina clarifies that the implication is epistemic: the effect's ceasing is a sign that the cause ceased first. The cause remains ontologically prior, and the dependency is one-way.

Checkpoint

A) The effect caused the cause to cease.
B) The cause ceased first.
C) The effect is independent.
D) The cause is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The cessation of an effect is a sign pointing to the prior cessation of its cause, not a cause of that cessation.

Observed Contingency Proves Complete Cause Does Not Necessitate Effect
The author argues from observed contingency that a complete cause does not necessitate its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observation of contingent changes in the world leads to the conclusion that a complete cause does not necessitate its effect, preserving divine freedom.

Core Idea
If a complete cause necessarily produced its effect, nothing in the universe would ever change.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument from the observed fact that things cease to exist. The author argues that if a complete cause necessarily necessitated its effect, then the cessation of effects would imply the cessation of the cause itself, leading to a contradiction. This reasoning establishes that divine causality is not deterministic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument is a proof by contradiction.
2
Understand the conditional premise
The author starts with a conditional: if effect cannot be separated from cause, then the cause would be contingent. Grasp why this conditional is set up.
This conditional is the key to the proof.
3
Examine the observation of contingency
The author points out that forms and accidents cease to exist. This observation is the empirical basis for the argument.
4
Follow the two-case analysis
The cause is either necessary by itself or contingent. Trace what follows in each case: necessary leads to contradiction, contingent leads to infinite regress.
Note how each case leads to an absurdity.
5
Recognize the conclusion
The conclusion is that the complete cause does not necessitate the effect. This means that God's causality is free, not deterministic.
This is a key doctrine in Ash'ari kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لذاته يمتنع انفكاك ذلك المؤثر عن ذلك الأثر ، لزم كون ذلك المؤثر ممكن الوجود ، وهذا محال ، فذلك محال . بيان الشرطية : إنا نشاهد أن هذه الصور والأعراض ، قد تبطل بعد وجودها . وإن كان كذلك ، فحينئذ بلزم من عدمها ، عدم لوازم تلك المؤثرات ، ويلزم من عدم اللازم عدم الملزوم . ينتج : أنه يلزم من عدم هذه الصور والأعراض ، عدم مؤثراتها . ثم ذلك المؤثر إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته، أو كان ممكناً لذاته ، فإن كان واجباً لذاته ، فحينئذ يلزم من عدم هذه الصور والأعراض ، عدم الموجود الذي هو واجب الوجود لذاته، لكن كل ما كان قابلا للعدم لم يكن واجب الوجود لذاته . أن الواجب لذاته ليس واجباً لذاته . هذا خلف . وأما إن كان المؤثر في هذه الصور والأعراض الزائلة [ أشياء ] ليست واجبة الوجود لذواتها ، بل كانت ممكنة لذواتها . فنقول : يلزم من عدم هذه الآثار والصفات ، عدم ما كان مؤثراً منها ، ثم عدمه لا بد وأن يكون أيضاً لعدم مؤثره ، ولا يزال يكون [ عدم ] كل معلول ، أسفل لعدم علته التي هي فوقه ، حتى ينتهي إلى العلة الأولى ، وحينئذ يلزم من عدم هذه الآثار عدم ذاته ، وذلك باطل . فيثبت : أنه لا يلزم من حصول المؤثر التام ، وجوب حصول الأثر معه .
For its essence, it is impossible for that effect to be separated from that cause, then that cause would be contingent in existence, and this is impossible, so that is impossible. Explanation of the conditional: We observe that these forms and accidents cease after their existence. And if that is so, then from their non-existence, the non-existence of the concomitants of those causes follows, and from the non-existence of the concomitant, the non-existence of the correlative follows. It results: that from the non-existence of these forms and accidents, the non-existence of their causes follows. Then that cause is either necessary by itself or contingent by itself. If it is necessary by itself, then from the non-existence of these forms and accidents, the non-existence of the being that is necessary of existence by itself follows, but everything that is receptive to non-existence is not necessary of existence by itself. The necessary by itself is not necessary by itself. This is a contradiction. And if the cause in these perishable forms and accidents is [things] that are not necessary of existence by themselves, but are contingent by themselves, then we say: from the non-existence of these effects and attributes, the non-existence of that which was their cause follows, then its non-existence must also be due to the non-existence of its cause, and it continues that [the non-existence] of every effect is lower than the non-existence of its cause which is above it, until it ends at the First Cause, and then from the non-existence of these effects, the non-existence of its essence follows, and that is false. Thus it is established: that from the attainment of the complete cause, the necessity of the attainment of the effect with it does not follow.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction to show that a complete cause does not necessitate its effect. The author begins by stating the conditional: if it is impossible for an effect to be separated from its cause, then the cause would be contingent, which is absurd. He then provides an observation: we see that forms and accidents cease to exist after having existed. From this, he argues that their ceasing implies the non-existence of their causes' concomitants, then the non-existence of the causes themselves. Next, he considers two possibilities for the cause: it is either necessary by itself or contingent by itself. If it were necessary, then its non-existence would be impossible, but since the effect ceases, the necessary cause would be subject to non-existence, a contradiction. If the cause is contingent, then chain of non-existence regresses until the First Cause, whose non-existence would follow from the effect's non-existence, which is false. Hence, it is established that the complete cause does not necessitate the effect's existence.

الواجب لذاته
الممكن لذاته
الأثر
العلة الأولى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies causality altogether.
Correction: The argument does not deny causality; it denies the necessity of effect from a complete cause, preserving divine freedom and contingency in the world.

B) The complete cause does not necessitate the effect.
C) The First Cause is impossible.
D) Effects are eternal and never cease.

Key Takeaway
The observed contingency of the world proves that a complete cause does not necessitate its effect, preserving divine freedom.

Avicenna on Cause and Effect Cessation
Avicenna clarifies causal priority in cessation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Avicenna's key distinction between the effect's cessation causing the cause's cessation versus merely indicating prior cessation, and how this resolves a fundamental objection in causal reasoning.

Core Idea
Effect's cessation signals, not causes, the cause's prior cessation.

This lesson examines Avicenna's response to a common objection about causality: if an effect ceases, does that mean the effect caused the cause to cease? Avicenna clarifies that the effect's cessation merely indicates that the cause had already ceased. By understanding this distinction, we see how causal priority is preserved without circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Avicenna's own wording come through before you interpret.
Notice the editorial brackets in the Arabic—they indicate words added for clarity.
2
Identify the key distinction
Avicenna distinguishes between two meanings: the effect's cessation being a cause of the cause's cessation, and the effect's cessation indicating that the cause had ceased first. Find where this distinction is made.
The phrase 'لا على معنى... بل على معنى' highlights the contrast.
3
Understand the resolved objection
Reflect on how this answer repels the original question. Why does the distinction matter for preserving causal priority?
4
Note the scholarly endorsement
The author adds that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri accepted this answer. Consider why this endorsement might strengthen the argument.
Al-Basri's approval shows the answer was influential in later kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينتج وأجاب الشيخ الرئيس عن كلام يشبه هذا السؤال : وذلك لأنه قال في كتاب الإشارات: « إنه يلزم من فرض ارتفاع المعلول ، [ ارتفاع ] العلة ، أن لا على معنى [ أن ] ارتفاع المعلول علة لارتفاع العلة ، . . بل على معنى ارتفاع [ المعلول ] يدل على أن العلة ارتفعت أولاً ، حتى لزم من ارتفاعها ارتفاع المعلول ، وعلى هذا التقدير فقد اندفع السؤال » . واعلم أن أبا الحسين البصري، ذكر أيضاً هذا الجواب في كتابه التصفح ، وارتضاه
It results, and the Chief Master answered concerning speech resembling this question: That is because he said in the Book of Indications (al-Isharat): 'It follows from the supposition of the cessation of the effect, [the cessation] of the cause, that not in the sense [that] the cessation of the effect is a cause for the cessation of the cause, ... but in the sense that the cessation of [the effect] indicates that the cause had ceased first, so that from its cessation the cessation of the effect followed, and on this assumption the question is repelled.' And know that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri also mentioned this answer in his book al-Tasaffuh, and approved it.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the answer of the Chief Master (Avicenna) to the previous argument. Avicenna, in his work al-Isharat, explains that when an effect ceases, it does not mean that the cessation of the effect is the cause of the cessation of the cause. Rather, the cessation of the effect indicates that the cause had already ceased first, which then necessitated the effect's cessation. On this interpretation, the original objection (that the complete cause does not necessitate the effect) is resolved. The author adds that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri also accepted this answer in his book al-Tasaffuh.

Correction: Avicenna explains that the effect's cessation indicates that the cause had already ceased first; it does not cause the cause's cessation.

Key Takeaway
Avicenna's answer shows that causal priority is preserved because the effect's cessation is a sign, not a cause, of the cause's cessation.
