Critique of Avicenna's Answer on Causality
The author critiques Avicenna's answer, showing it still leads to the First Cause being possible of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that Avicenna's answer fails because it leads to the First Cause being contingent and subject to non-existence.

Core Idea
Even if effects reveal cause cessation, the regress makes the First Cause possible of non-existence.

The author shares his own intellectual journey: he once accepted Avicenna's answer but eventually saw its flaw. He then constructs a logical argument showing that the cessation of observed effects forces a chain of causation that ultimately implies the First Cause could cease to exist—something impossible for the Necessary Existent. This lesson walks through that critique step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author begins by recalling his earlier acceptance, then states his current position, and finally elaborates the argument.
2
Trace the regress of indications
Identify the chain: observed forms/accidents cease → their cause ceases → that cause's cause ceases, and so on. Understand that this chain leads to either the First Cause ceasing or a condition of its causality ceasing.
The author says this regression forces one of two conclusions.
3
Why both conclusions are impossible
Both options imply that the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) is subject to annihilation, which is impossible. Therefore, Avicenna's answer is invalid and the original argument stands.
The argument shows that Avicenna's attempted defense actually undermines itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال مولانا رضي الله عنه : إنه مربي زمان طويل ، وكنت أظن أن هذا الجواب حق ، ثم لاح لي : أنه ضعيف ، وأن السؤال باقي ، وتقريره : أن نقول : هب أن ارتفاع المعلول لا يوجب ارتفاع العلة ، بل يكشف عن أن العلة ارتفعت أولاً ، حتى لزم من ارتفاعها ارتفاع المعلول، إلا أنا نقول : إنا نشاهد أن هذه الصور والأعراض قد تعدم بعد وجودها ، فوجب أن يدل ارتفاعها وزوالها على أن علتها قد ارتفعت وزالت ، حتى لزم من ارتفاع علتها ارتفاعها ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فارتفاع هذه الصور والأعراض ، يدل على ارتفاع [ عللها ، وارتفاع عللها يدل على ارتفاع ] علل عللها ، ولا يزال يدل عدم كل علة نازلة ، على عدم العلة الفوقانية ، فوجب أن يتأدى أجزاء الأمر إلى أن يدل ارتفاع هذه الصور والأعراض، إما على ارتفاع ذات العلة الأولى ، أو على ارتفاع قيد من القيود المعتبرة في كونه علة لما بعده، وعلى التقديرين فإنه يلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته [ ممكن الوجود لذاته ] قابلا للفناء والعدم ، وذلك محال . وإذا بطل هذا ، ثبت أنه لا يلزم من وجود العلة التامة المستجمعة لجميع الجهات المعتبرة في العلية ، كون المعول موجوداً . كون المعول موجوداً معه ، وذلك [ يبطل ] أصل الحجة المذكورة .
Our master, may God be pleased with him, said: He raised me for a long time, and I used to think that this answer was correct, then it became clear to me: that it is weak, and that the question remains. Its elaboration: we say: Assume that the cessation of the effect does not necessitate the cessation of the cause, but rather reveals that the cause had ceased first, so that from its cessation the cessation of the effect followed. But we say: We observe that these forms and accidents cease after their existence, so it is necessary that their cessation and disappearance indicate that their cause had ceased and disappeared, so that from the cessation of its cause its cessation followed. And if this is so, then the cessation of these forms and accidents indicates the cessation of [their causes, and the cessation of their causes indicates the cessation of] the causes of their causes, and the non-existence of every lower cause continues to indicate the non-existence of the higher cause, so it must follow that the parts of the matter lead to the cessation of these forms and accidents indicating either the cessation of the essence of the First Cause, or the cessation of a condition among the conditions considered in its being a cause for what follows. On both assumptions, it follows that the Necessary of Existence by itself [would be possible of existence by itself], receptive to annihilation and non-existence, and that is impossible. And if this is false, it is established that from the existence of the complete cause that gathers all the aspects considered in causality, the existence of the effect does not follow. The existence of the effect with it, and that [annuls] the basis of the mentioned argument.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents the author's critique of Avicenna's answer. The author first recalls that he once considered Avicenna's answer correct, but later realized it is weak. He then elaborates: even if we grant that the cessation of the effect only reveals that the cause had ceased first, we still observe forms and accidents ceasing. Their cessation indicates their cause's cessation, and that cause's cessation indicates its own cause's cessation, and so on up the chain. This regression leads to one of two conclusions: either the First Cause itself ceases, or a condition of its causality ceases. Both options imply that the Necessary Existent is contingent and subject to annihilation, which is impossible. Therefore, the original argument that the complete cause does not necessitate the effect remains valid, and Avicenna's objection is nullified.

Misconception: Some might think the author denies that an effect's cessation indicates its cause's cessation.
Correction: The author actually accepts that, but shows that the regress this creates forces the First Cause to be contingent, which is the real problem.

Tenth Argument: Permanence of Cause Implies Eternal Effects
The author presents a conditional argument: if cause permanence implies effect permanence, then an eternal chain of effects follows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical consequence of assuming that a permanent cause necessitates a permanent effect, and why that leads to an absurd conclusion contradicting the observed temporality of the world.

Core Idea
If a permanent cause forces permanent effects, then nothing new can ever happen.

The tenth argument tackles a key assumption in causal reasoning: does an eternal cause necessarily produce an eternal effect? The author shows that if we accept this premise, then everything from the First Cause down must be eternal, which clearly contradicts our experience of change and new events. This reveals that the premise must be rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'. The author is laying out a chain of reasoning.
The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the logical direction is clear.
2
Identify the conditional chain
The argument states: if permanence of cause implies permanence of effect, then that effect (as a cause for another) would also be permanent, and so on. Trace the chain from the First Cause to all existents.
This is a regress argument, but not an infinite regress; it's a chain that encompasses all created things.
3
Recognize the absurd consequence
The conclusion is that all existents would be permanent, meaning nothing new ever comes into being. This contradicts our everyday experience of change and temporality.
The unstated conclusion is that the original premise must be false – cause permanence does not necessitate effect permanence.
4
Understand the refutation
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: the premise leads to a false consequence, so the premise is false. The author thus refutes the idea that an eternal cause must have an eternal effect.
This argument defends the possibility of temporal events arising from an eternal cause (e.g., creation of the world in time).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : أن نقول : إن لزم من دوام العلة دوام المعلول ، لزم [ من دوام ذلك ] المعلول : دوام معلول ذلك المعلول . ثم الكلام في المعلول الثالث كما في المعلول الثاني والأول ، وعلى هذا التقدير، لزم من دوام وجود العلة الأولى ، دوام وجود جملة الموجودات ، حتى لا يحصل في العالم شيء من
The tenth: that we say: If the permanence of the cause necessitates the permanence of the effect, then the permanence of [that] effect necessitates the permanence of the effect of that effect. Then the discussion concerning the third effect is as concerning the second and the first. On this assumption, the permanence of the existence of the First Cause necessitates the permanence of the existence of the totality of existents, so that nothing in the world occurs...
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth argument in a series. The author states a conditional: if the permanence of a cause necessitates the permanence of its effect, then the permanence of that effect would likewise necessitate the permanence of its own effect. This chain of reasoning applies to the third effect as it did to the second and first. Consequently, if the First Cause is permanent, then all existents must be permanent, leading to the conclusion that nothing new occurs in the world—a clear violation of observed change and temporality. The text cuts off, but the implication is that such a conclusion is false, so the original premise (that cause permanence necessitates effect permanence) must be rejected.

Key Terms

دوام المعلول
العلة الأولى
جملة الموجودات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that effects are eternal because the First Cause is eternal.
Correction: The argument is a reductio: it shows that if the premise were true, effects would be eternal, but since they are not, the premise is false. The author does not claim effects are eternal.

Checkpoint

A) The First Cause is contingent.
B) All existents would be eternal.
C) Only the First Cause is eternal.
D) Effects can cease independently.

Key Takeaway
The permanence of cause does not necessitate permanence of effect, as evidenced by the occurrence of new events in the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument refutes the idea that God's permanence forces the world to be eternal. How does the fact that He creates new things every moment affect your hope and expectation in your personal relationship with Him?

If the permanence of cause necessitated permanence of effect, nothing could ever change. Thank Allah for the constant renewal of His creation. Commit to recognizing one new thing today as a gift from Him, beyond cause and effect.

Refutation of al-Basri's Answer on Causality
The author refutes al-Basri's answer and argues that the perfect cause does not necessitate simultaneous effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the disappearance of effects cannot be used to prove the perishability of the First Cause, and how this refutes the notion that a perfect cause necessitates a simultaneous effect.

Core Idea
The chain of causality cannot lead to the First Cause's annihilation.

In this passage, the author critiques a previous attempt to defend the idea that a perfect cause does not necessitate a simultaneous effect. He shows that if we accept that effects reveal the cessation of their causes, we would be forced to conclude that the First Cause itself is subject to annihilation—an impossibility. This refutation strengthens the orthodox position on divine causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to the logical flow and the names mentioned.
The passage includes editorial brackets; focus on the main argument.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
Identify Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's original answer: that the disappearance of an effect does not necessitate the disappearance of its cause, but rather reveals that the cause disappeared first.
The author grants this for the sake of argument.
3
Follow the author's reductio
Trace how the author uses the observation of forms and accidents ceasing to build a chain that leads to the impossible conclusion that the First Cause could perish.
The conclusion is that a perfect cause does not necessitate the simultaneous existence of its effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن أبا الحسين البصري، ذكر أيضاً هذا الجواب في كتابه التصفح، وارتضاه [ قال مولانا رضي الله عنه ] : إنه مربي زمان طويل، وكنت أظن أن هذا الجواب حق، ثم لاح لي : أنه ضعيف، وأن السؤال باقي، وتقريره : أن نقول : هب أن ارتفاع المعلول لا يوجب ارتفاع العلة، بل يكشف عن أن العلة ارتفعت أولاً، حتى لزم من ارتفاعها ارتفاع المعلول، إلا أنا نقول : إنا نشاهد أن هذه الصور والأعراض قد تعدم بعد وجودها، فوجب أن يدل ارتفاعها وزوالها على أن علتها قد ارتفعت وزالت، حتى لزم من ارتفاع علتها ارتفاعها، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فارتفاع هذه الصور والأعراض، يدل على ارتفاع [ عللها، وارتفاع عللها يدل على ارتفاع ] علل عللها، ولا يزال يدل عدم كل علة نازلة، على عدم العلة الفوقانية، فوجب أن يتأدى أجزاء الأمر إلى أن يدل ارتفاع هذه الصور والأعراض، إما على ارتفاع ذات العلة الأولى، أو على ارتفاع قيد من القيود المعتبرة في كونه علة لما بعده، وعلى التقديرين فإنه يلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته [ ممكن الوجود لذاته ] قابلا للفناء والعدم، وذلك محال. وإذا بطل هذا، ثبت أنه لا يلزم من وجود العلة التامة المستجمعة لجميع الجهات المعتبرة في العلية، كون المعول موجوداً. كون المعول موجوداً معه، وذلك [ يبطل ] أصل الحجة المذكورة.
And know that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri also mentioned this answer in his book al-Tasaffuh, and approved it. [Our master, may God be pleased with him, said]: He was raised for a long time, and I used to think that this answer was correct, then it became clear to me that it is weak, and that the question remains. And its exposition: that we say: Grant that the disappearance of the effect does not necessitate the disappearance of the cause, but rather reveals that the cause disappeared first, so that from its disappearance the disappearance of the effect followed. However, we say: we observe that these forms and accidents cease to exist after their existence. So necessarily their disappearance and passing away indicates that their cause has disappeared and passed away, so that from the disappearance of their cause their disappearance followed. And when the matter is thus, then the disappearance of these forms and accidents indicates the disappearance of [their causes, and the disappearance of their causes indicates the disappearance of] the causes of their causes. And the non-existence of every lower cause continues to indicate the non-existence of the higher cause. So necessarily the parts of the matter lead to the disappearance of these forms and accidents indicating either the disappearance of the essence of the First Cause, or the disappearance of a condition among the conditions considered in its being a cause for what comes after it. On both suppositions, it necessarily follows that the Necessary Existent in essence [possible in essence] would be susceptible to annihilation and non-existence, and that is impossible. And if this is nullified, it is established that from the existence of the perfect cause comprising all the aspects considered in causality, the existence of the effect does not necessarily follow. The existence of the effect with it, and that nullifies the basis of the mentioned argument.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a previous answer to a causal argument. The author begins by noting that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri presented an answer in his book and approved it. The author (Mawlana) initially thought it correct but later realized its weakness. The exposition proceeds as follows: Assume that the cessation of an effect does not cause the cessation of its cause, but rather reveals that the cause had already ceased. However, we observe that forms and accidents cease to exist. Their cessation indicates that their cause has ceased. This leads to a chain: cessation of lower causes indicates cessation of higher causes, ultimately pointing to either the cessation of the First Cause itself or the removal of a condition for its causality. Either way, the Necessary Existent would become possible, capable of annihilation—which is impossible. Therefore, the original assumption is false: a perfect cause does not necessitate the simultaneous existence of its effect. This refutes the argument that used such a necessity.

The Tenth Argument: Eternal Cause Prevents Change
A reductio ad absurdum argument against necessary simultaneity of cause and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reductio ad absurdum that shows a perpetual cause does not necessitate a perpetual effect in all cases.

Core Idea
If all effects were perpetual, the world would be changeless — but it isn't.

The author now presents a tenth argument that challenges the claim that an eternal cause must have an eternal effect. Using a chain of causation, he shows that accepting this premise leads to an absurd conclusion: the world would be devoid of all change. Since we observe change, the premise must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the logical structure of the argument.
The argument uses a chain of causation.
2
Understand the logical condition
The argument begins with a conditional: if perpetual cause implies perpetual effect, then apply this to the effect which is itself a cause for further effects.
3
Follow the chain
Trace the reasoning: the first effect, being a cause, would have its effect perpetual, and so on, leading to all effects being perpetual.
This creates an infinite regress of perpetual existence.
4
Identify the absurd conclusion
The conclusion is that if all existents are perpetual, no change would ever occur in the world. But we observe change, so the original premise must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : أن نقول : إن لزم من دوام العلة دوام المعلول، لزم [ من دوام ذلك ] المعلول : دوام معلول ذلك المعلول. ثم الكلام في المعلول الثالث كما في المعلول الثاني والأول، وعلى هذا التقدير، لزم من دوام وجود العلة الأولى، دوام وجود جملة الموجودات، حتى لا يحصل في العالم شيء من التغيرات [ البتة ] ومعلوم : أن ذلك باطل.
The tenth: that we say: if it follows from the perpetuity of the cause the perpetuity of the effect, then it follows from the perpetuity of that effect the perpetuity of the effect of that effect. Then the discussion concerning the third effect is like the discussion concerning the second and first. On this assumption, it follows from the perpetuity of the existence of the First Cause the perpetuity of the existence of the entire set of existents, such that no changes occur in the world at all. And it is known that this is false.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth argument against the claim that a perpetual cause requires a perpetual effect. The reasoning is as follows: If a cause that always exists necessarily means its effect also always exists, then that effect, being a cause for further effects, would also cause those effects to be perpetual. This chain repeats endlessly. Consequently, the perpetual First Cause would necessitate the perpetual existence of all effects, meaning no change whatsoever would occur in the world. But the world clearly does undergo change, so the original claim is false. The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum: accepting the premise leads to a contradiction with observable reality.

Key Terms
دوام
علة
معلول
تغيرات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument rejects all causality.
Correction: The argument only rejects the necessity that a perpetual cause must have a perpetual effect in all cases; it does not deny causality altogether.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd conclusion drawn from the assumption that perpetual cause requires perpetual effect?
A) That the First Cause would be multiple.
B) That the world would have no changes at all.
C) That effects would be prior to their causes.
D) That the universe would cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
The observation of change in the world disproves the claim that an eternal cause necessarily has an eternal effect in every case.

The Philosophers' Answer: Condition of Preceding Event's Passing
Philosophers state the condition for temporal events from an eternal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' core answer to how an eternal cause produces temporal events: each event requires the prior event to have passed away.

Core Idea
An eternal cause produces temporal effects one after another, each conditioned on the preceding event's passing.

The philosophers face the challenge of explaining how an unchanging eternal cause can give rise to a series of temporal events. They propose a solution: the cause remains the same, but the condition for each new event is the disappearance of the one before it. This lesson presents that core answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Then read the literal translation.
The passage is dense but straightforward.
2
Identify the condition
Find the phrase that states the condition for the emanation of each temporal event. Note that it is the passing away of the preceding event.
The condition is explicitly stated.
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on why this leads to an infinite temporal series without a first event. The eternal cause remains unchanged, but each event depends on the prior one's end.
This avoids a beginning in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الجواب عن هذا السؤال : أن المبدأ [ الفياض ] موجود ازلاً وأبداً، إلا أن شرط فيضان كل حادث عنه انقضاء الحادث الذي كان موجوداً قبله، فلهذا السبب قلنا : إنه يجب أن يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر، لا إلى [ أول ] والعلة الموجودة للكل، المؤثرة في حصول الكل هو ذلك القديم. قالوا والذي يقرر أن الأمر كذلك : أن الحركات إما طبيعية أو قسرية، وإما إرادية. والمعنى الذي ذكرناه حاصل في الكل من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة. أما الحركات الطبيعية، فهي مثل نزول الحجر من فوق إلى أسفل. وتقريره. أن الموجب لذلك النزول هو الثقل الحال في جوهر الحجر، وذلك الثقل باقي في جميع الأوقات غير متبدل إلا أن تأثيره في إيجاب كل جزء من أجزاء تلك الحركة، مشروط بانقضاء الجزء المتقدم، وذلك لأنه لولا أن ذلك الجسم انتهى بحركته المتقدمة، إلى ذلك الحد المعين من المسافة، وإلا لامتنع كون الثقل القائم به، موجباً انتقاله من ذلك الحد المعين إلى حد آخر، فالموجب لكل جزء من أجزاء تلك الحركة هو ذلك الثقل. أما شرط كون ذلك الثقل مؤثراً في ذلك الجزء المعين من الحركة، هو وصوله بالجزء المتقدم من الحركة إلى ذلك الجزء المعين من المسافة. وأما الحركات القسرية، فالأمر أيضاً كذلك، وذلك لأن الإنسان إذا رمى الحجر إلى فوق، فقد أودع فيه قوة توجب صعود ذلك الحجر إلى فوق قسراً، فالموجب لجميع أجزاء تلك الحركة، إلا أن تأثير القوة في الجزء المعين من تلك الحركة، مشروط بإنتهائه بالجزء المتقدم من الحركة، إلى ذلك الحد المعين، وأما في الحركات الاختيارية، فالأمر أيضاً كذلك. لأن الإنسان إذا أراد أن يذهب إلى البلد الفلاني ليجد غريمه فيطالبه بالدين .
The philosophers said: The answer to this question: that the [effulgent] principle exists eternally and everlastingly, but the condition for the emanation of every temporal event from Him is the passing away of the temporal event that existed before it. For this reason we said: it is necessary that every temporal event be preceded by another temporal event, without [first]. And the cause existing for all, the efficient cause of the occurrence of all, is that Eternal One. They said: and what establishes that this is the case: that motions are either natural, compulsive, or voluntary. And the meaning we mentioned obtains in all of these three divisions. As for natural motions, they are like the falling of a stone from above to below. And its explanation: that the cause of that falling is the weight residing in the substance of the stone, and that weight remains in all times unchanging, except that its effect in causing each part of those motions is conditioned upon the passing away of the preceding part. That is because if that body had not arrived by its preceding motion at that particular point of distance, then it would be impossible for the weight subsisting in it to cause its transfer from that particular point to another point. So the cause of each part of those motions is that weight. As for the condition of that weight being effective in that particular part of the motion, it is its arrival by the preceding part of the motion at that particular point of distance. And as for compulsive motions, the matter is also like that, because when a person throws a stone upward, he deposits in it a force that causes that stone to ascend upward compulsively. So the cause of all parts of that motion, except that the effect of the force in the particular part of that motion is conditioned upon its completion by the preceding part of the motion to that particular point. And as for voluntary motions, the matter is also like that, because when a person wants to go to a certain town to find his debtor and demand the debt.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' response to the question of how an eternal cause can produce temporal effects. They explain that the eternal principle (al-mabda' al-fayyad) exists eternally, but the condition for each temporal event (hadith) to emanate from it is the prior passing away of the preceding event. Thus every temporal event is preceded by another, forming an infinite temporal series without a first temporal event. The eternal cause is the cause of all. They then illustrate this with motions: natural motion (e.g., stone falling), compulsive motion (e.g., thrown stone), and voluntary motion (e.g., person walking). In each case, the cause of each successive part of motion remains constant (weight, force, or will), but its effect on a particular spatial point is conditioned upon the arrival at that point via the preceding motion. The cause does not change; only the condition of the prior part's completion changes. This explains how an unchanging cause can produce successive effects without itself undergoing change.

Key Terms
مبدأ فياض
حادث
شرط
فيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the philosophers deny a beginning to the world, but they assume an eternal world with an eternal cause, not a creation ex nihilo.
Correction: The philosophers argue for an eternal world with an eternal cause, but each temporal part depends on the passing of the previous part, avoiding simultaneity.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the condition for the emanation of each temporal event from the eternal principle?
A) The eternal principle must change its nature.
B) The preceding temporal event must have passed away.
C) The event must be caused by another temporal event.
D) No condition is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers answer that the condition for each new temporal event is the passing away of the preceding event, ensuring an eternal cause's unchanging nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does the philosophical picture of an eternal cause producing an infinite chain of temporal events make you feel closer to God or more distant? How does your answer reveal your underlying assumptions about God's relationship to time?
?
If every moment of your life depends on the passing of the previous moment as a condition for God's ongoing creation, how does that change the way you view the present moment? Are you living as if each moment is a direct gift from the Eternal, or do you take the flow of time for granted?
?
The philosophers argue that the eternal cause remains unchanged while effects change. How does this challenge you to rethink your own understanding of God's involvement in your daily struggles? Do you ever feel that God is uninvolved because things don't change immediately? What does this lesson demand you to reconsider about waiting for God's action in your life?

Illustrating the Answer with Three Types of Motion
Philosophers illustrate the answer with natural, compulsive, and voluntary motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how natural, compulsive, and voluntary motions illustrate the philosophers' principle that each successive part of motion depends on the completion of the prior part.

Core Idea
All motions—natural, forced, or chosen—follow the pattern: each part depends on the prior part's completion.

To demonstrate that their answer applies universally, the philosophers examine three types of motion: natural, compulsive, and voluntary. In each case, the underlying cause remains constant, yet its effect is contingent on the preceding part of the motion. This lesson explores these examples and shows how they support the core principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the motion examples
Read the Arabic passage describing natural, compulsive, and voluntary motions. Follow the translation to understand each example.
The passage is detailed; you may need to read slowly.
2
Identify the common structure
Notice that in each motion type, the cause (weight, force, will) is constant, but its effect on a particular part is conditioned by the prior part's completion.
The condition is the same as in the general answer.
3
Connect back to the philosophical answer
Reflect on how these examples illustrate the general principle: an unchanging cause produces successive effects because each effect requires the prior to have passed.
This reinforces the answer to the original question.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Philosophers' Argument on Causal Conditioning
Philosophers argue for conditioned emanation and illustrate with three motion types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that every temporal event is conditioned by the passing away of the previous event, and how they illustrate this with three types of motion.

Core Idea
An eternal cause can only produce a new effect after the previous effect ceases.

The philosophers present a sophisticated causal theory to explain how an unchanging eternal principle can give rise to a succession of temporal events. They argue that the key is a condition: each new event depends on the termination of its predecessor. To make this plausible, they provide examples from natural, coercive, and voluntary motions. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Start by reading the opening statement where the philosophers affirm the eternal principle and the condition of emanation. Notice that they assert an infinite regress of events without a first.
2
Examine the three motion examples
Study the natural, coercive, and voluntary motion examples. In each case, identify the constant cause and the condition that allows it to produce a new part of the motion.
The condition in each case is the completion of the preceding part.
3
Understand the conclusion
Read the concluding paragraph where the philosophers draw their inference: the eternal effecter remains unchanged, but its effect on each new event depends on the prior event's passing. They claim this is a rational method unique to their doctrine.
This conclusion leads to the theologians' objection in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الجواب عن هذا السؤال : أن المبدأ الفياض موجود ازلاً وأبداً ، إلا أن شرط فيضان كل حادث عنه انقضاء الحادث الذي كان موجوداً قبله ، فلهذا السبب قلنا : إنه يجب أن يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر ، لا إلى أول والعلة الموجودة للكل ، المؤثرة في حصول الكل هو ذلك القديم . قالوا والذي يقرر أن الأمر كذلك : أن الحركات إما طبيعية أو قسرية ، وإما إرادية . والمعنى الذي ذكرناه حاصل في الكل من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة

أما الحركات الطبيعية ، فهي مثل نزول الحجر من فوق إلى أسفل . وتقريره . أن الموجب لذلك النزول هو الثقل الحال في جوهر الحجر ، وذلك الثقل باقي في جميع الأوقات غير متبدل إلا أن تأثيره في إيجاب كل جزء من أجزاء تلك الحركة ، مشروط بانقضاء الجزء المتقدم، وذلك لأنه لولا أن ذلك الجسم انتهى بحركته المتقدمة ، إلى ذلك الحد المعين من المسافة ، وإلا لامتنع كون الثقل القائم به ، موجباً انتقاله من ذلك الحد المعين إلى حد آخر ، فالموجب لكل جزء من أجزاء تلك الحركة هو ذلك الثقل . أما شرط كون ذلك الثقل مؤثراً في ذلك الجزء المعين من الحركة ، هو وصوله بالجزء المتقدم من الحركة إلى ذلك الجزء المعين من المسافة . وأما الحركات القسرية ، فالأمر أيضاً كذلك ، وذلك لأن الإنسان إذا رمى الحجر إلى فوق ، فقد أودع فيه قوة توجب صعود ذلك الحجر إلى فوق قسراً ، فالموجب لجميع أجزاء تلك الحركة هو تلك القوة ، إلا أن تأثير القوة في الجزء المعين من تلك الحركة ، مشروط بإنتهائه بالجزء المتقدم من الحركة ، إلى ذلك الحد المعين . وأما في الحركات الاختيارية ، فالأمر أيضاً كذلك . لأن الإنسان إذا أراد أن يذهب إلى البلد الفلاني ليجد غريمه فيطالبه بالدين . فهذه الداعية هي الموجبة لانتقاله من هذا البلد إلى ذلك ، وهذه الداعية باقية مع جميع الأجزاء الحاصلة في تلك الحركة ، إلا أن الخطوة المتقدمة ، شرط لكون تلك الداعية مؤثرة في حصول الخطوة الثانية ، فإنه لولا أن ذلك الحصول إن إنتهى بسبب الخطوة الأولى إلى ذلك الحد المعين من المسافة ، وإلا امتنع كون تلك الداعية موجبة لانتقاله من ذلك الحد المعين إلى حد آخر

فيثبت بهذا : أن المؤثر في جميع هذه الحوادث موجود باقي مبرأ عن جميع جهات التغيرات ، إلا أن تأثيره في حدوث كل حادث متأخر ، مشروط بتقدم الحادث المتقدم، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر ولا إلى أول . وعند هذا قالوا : ظهر أنه لو انقطع حدوث الحوادث لحظة واحدة ، لامتنع حدوث الحوادث بعد ذلك أبداً ، وظهر أنه لا يمكن إسناد الحوادث المتعاقبة إلى المؤثر القديم ، إلا بهذا الطريق . قالوا : وهذا طريق معقول يتيسر على مذهبنا ، ولم يتيسر لأحد من أرباب المذاهب سوانا ، فكان ذلك دليلا على شرف قولنا ، وقوة كلامنا .

قال المتكلمون والمحققون : لا شك أنكم بالغتم في التدقيق والتحقيق إلا أن البحث الغامض باقي كما كان ، وذلك لأنا نقول : لا شك أن العلة المؤثرية القديمة، ما كانت موجودة لهذا الحادث المعين ، حال ما كان الحادث المتقدم عليه موجوداً ، ثم صارت في الوقت الثاني موجدة لهذا الحادث ، فصيرورة تلك العلة موجدة لهذا الحادث بعد أنها ما كانت كذلك : حكم حادث. فهذا الحكم الحادث، إما أن يفتقر إلى مؤثر ، وإما أن لا يفتقر إليه.. فإن كان الثاني فقد حدث أمر من الأمور لا لمؤثر ، وإذا عقل هذا في البعض ، فليعقل مثله في الكل. وهذا يوجب استغناء الممكن المحدث عن المؤثر . وهو محال

وأما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال : حدوث تلك المؤثرية والموجدية ،
The philosophers said: the answer to this question is that the emanation principle (al-mabda' al-fayyāḍ) exists eternally and perpetually, except that the condition for the emanation of every temporal event from it is the passing away of the temporal event that existed before it. For this reason we said: it must be that every temporal event is preceded by another temporal event, without a first. And the cause existing for all, effective in achieving all, is that eternal (al-qadīm). They said: and what establishes that the matter is so is that motions are either natural or coercive or voluntary. And the meaning we mentioned is present in all of these three divisions.

As for natural motions, they are like the falling of a stone from above to below. Its demonstration is that the necessitator of that falling is the heaviness (al-thiqal) present in the substance of the stone, and that heaviness remains in all times unchanging, except that its effect in necessitating each part of the parts of that motion is conditioned by the passing away of the preceding part. That is because if that body had not ended by its preceding motion at that specific limit of the distance, then it would be impossible for the heaviness subsisting in it to be a necessitator of its transfer from that specific limit to another limit. So the necessitator of each part of the parts of that motion is that heaviness. As for the condition of that heaviness being effective in that specific part of the motion, it is its arrival by the preceding part of the motion at that specific part of the distance. As for coercive motions, the matter is also so, and that is because when a person throws a stone upward, he has placed in it a force (quwwa) that necessitates the ascent of that stone upward coercively. So the necessitator of all the parts of that motion is that force, except that the effect of the force in the specific part of that motion is conditioned by its ending by the preceding part of the motion at that specific limit. As for voluntary motions, the matter is also so. Because when a person wants to go to such-and-such a town to find his debtor and demand payment of the debt, this motive (al-dā'iyya) is the necessitator of his transfer from this town to that, and this motive remains with all the parts occurring in that motion, except that the preceding step is a condition for that motive being effective in causing the second step. For if that arrival had not ended due to the first step at that specific limit of the distance, then it would be impossible for that motive to be a necessitator of his transfer from that specific limit to another limit.

So it is established by this that the effecter (al-mu'aththir) in all these temporal events is existent, eternal, free from all aspects of changes, except that its effect in causing each subsequent temporal event is conditioned by the precedence of the preceding temporal event. And this requires that every temporal event be preceded by another temporal event without a first. And at this they said: it has become apparent that if the occurrence of temporal events were interrupted for a single moment, it would be impossible for temporal events to occur after that ever, and it has become apparent that it is impossible to ascribe successive temporal events to the eternal effecter except by this way. They said: and this is a rational way that is feasible on our doctrine, and was not feasible for anyone of the adherents of doctrines other than us. So that was a proof of the honor of our speech and the strength of our discourse.

The theologians (al-mutakallimūn) and the investigators (al-muḥaqqiqūn) said: There is no doubt that you have gone far in exactness and investigation, but the obscure research remains as it was. That is because we say: There is no doubt that the eternal causal cause (al-‘illa al-mu’aththiriyya al-qadīma) was not causing this specific temporal event to exist while the preceding temporal event was existing, then in the second time it became causing this temporal event to exist. So the becoming of that cause a cause of existence for this temporal event after it was not so is an occurring state (ḥukm ḥādith). This occurring state either stands in need of an effecter or does not stand in need of it. If the second, then a matter from among matters has occurred without an effecter, and if this is conceived in some, then let the like of it be conceived in all. And this necessitates the independence of the contingent temporal thing from the effecter, and that is impossible (muḥāl). As for the first division, which is that it be said: the occurrence of that effecting and causing existence...
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed philosophical argument regarding causal conditioning and the theologians' response. It begins with the philosophers affirming an eternal principle (al-mabda' al-fayyāḍ) that is always existent, but they assert that each temporal event emanating from it is conditioned by the prior temporal event's passing away. This leads to an infinite regress of causes without a first event. They then illustrate this with three types of motion: natural (like a stone falling due to heaviness), coercive (like a stone thrown upward by force), and voluntary (like a person walking to another town due to a motive). In each case, the constant cause (heaviness, force, motive) remains unchanging, but its effect on each subsequent part of motion is conditioned by the completion of the preceding part. Thus, the eternal effecter (al-mu'aththir) is unchanging but its influence on each new event depends on the prior event's termination. The philosophers conclude that this demonstrates the impossibility of any gap in the chain of events and that this rational method is unique to their school, proving its superiority.

The theologians and investigators (al-mutakallimūn wa al-muḥaqqiqūn) respond by acknowledging the philosophers' precision but pointing out a remaining deep issue: the eternal cause's transition from not effecting a specific event (while the prior event existed) to effecting it (after the prior event's passing) is itself a new occurring state (ḥukm ḥādith). This new state requires a cause. If it has no cause, then something comes to be without a cause, which is absurd and would imply that contingent things can exist without an effecter—a contradiction. If it does have a cause, then that cause would be another temporal state, leading to an infinite regress. The text cuts off before fully discussing the second option, but the core objection is clear: the philosophers' account fails to explain the change in the causal relation itself.

Correction: The philosophers affirm an eternal cause that remains unchanged; the conditioning is not in the cause but in the relation of its effect to prior events.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers maintain that an unchanging eternal cause necessitates a chain of events without a first, each conditioned by the prior event's end.

In the example of the voluntary motion, the motive remains constant. What is the deepest motive driving your life right now? Is it sincerely for Allah, or is it mixed with worldly desires? Examine your heart: what would it take for you to change that motive today?

Theologians' Objection: The New State Problem
Theologians object that the change in divine causing is a new state needing a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' objection that the change in the eternal cause's relation to a new event is itself a new temporal state (ḥukm ḥādith) that requires a cause.

Core Idea
The shift from not-causing to causing is itself a temporal event needing explanation.

The philosophers presented a detailed causal theory, but the theologians point out a deep flaw: the transition from the eternal cause not effecting a specific event to effecting it is itself a new state. This state, being temporal, must have a cause. This leads to a dilemma that threatens the entire causal chain. Let's examine this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the theologians' acknowledgment and objection
Begin by reading the opening where theologians acknowledge the philosophers' precision but then identify the remaining problem: the change in the causal relation is a new occurring state (ḥukm ḥādith).
2
Understand the new state and its implications
Focus on the definition of the new state: the eternal cause was not causing the specific event while the prior event existed, then it becomes causing after the prior event ends. This 'becoming' is a new state.
3
Examine the two options
The theologians present two possibilities: either this new state has a cause or it does not. Analyze why both lead to absurdity: without a cause, something occurs without an effecter (impossible); with a cause, that cause would be another temporal state, leading to regress.
The passage cuts off before fully discussing the second option, but the core problem is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الجواب عن هذا السؤال : أن المبدأ الفياض موجود ازلاً وأبداً ، إلا أن شرط فيضان كل حادث عنه انقضاء الحادث الذي كان موجوداً قبله ، فلهذا السبب قلنا : إنه يجب أن يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر ، لا إلى أول والعلة الموجودة للكل ، المؤثرة في حصول الكل هو ذلك القديم . قالوا والذي يقرر أن الأمر كذلك : أن الحركات إما طبيعية أو قسرية ، وإما إرادية . والمعنى الذي ذكرناه حاصل في الكل من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة

أما الحركات الطبيعية ، فهي مثل نزول الحجر من فوق إلى أسفل . وتقريره . أن الموجب لذلك النزول هو الثقل الحال في جوهر الحجر ، وذلك الثقل باقي في جميع الأوقات غير متبدل إلا أن تأثيره في إيجاب كل جزء من أجزاء تلك الحركة ، مشروط بانقضاء الجزء المتقدم، وذلك لأنه لولا أن ذلك الجسم انتهى بحركته المتقدمة ، إلى ذلك الحد المعين من المسافة ، وإلا لامتنع كون الثقل القائم به ، موجباً انتقاله من ذلك الحد المعين إلى حد آخر ، فالموجب لكل جزء من أجزاء تلك الحركة هو ذلك الثقل . أما شرط كون ذلك الثقل مؤثراً في ذلك الجزء المعين من الحركة ، هو وصوله بالجزء المتقدم من الحركة إلى ذلك الجزء المعين من المسافة . وأما الحركات القسرية ، فالأمر أيضاً كذلك ، وذلك لأن الإنسان إذا رمى الحجر إلى فوق ، فقد أودع فيه قوة توجب صعود ذلك الحجر إلى فوق قسراً ، فالموجب لجميع أجزاء تلك الحركة هو تلك القوة ، إلا أن تأثير القوة في الجزء المعين من تلك الحركة ، مشروط بإنتهائه بالجزء المتقدم من الحركة ، إلى ذلك الحد المعين . وأما في الحركات الاختيارية ، فالأمر أيضاً كذلك . لأن الإنسان إذا أراد أن يذهب إلى البلد الفلاني ليجد غريمه فيطالبه بالدين . فهذه الداعية هي الموجبة لانتقاله من هذا البلد إلى ذلك ، وهذه الداعية باقية مع جميع الأجزاء الحاصلة في تلك الحركة ، إلا أن الخطوة المتقدمة ، شرط لكون تلك الداعية مؤثرة في حصول الخطوة الثانية ، فإنه لولا أن ذلك الحصول إن إنتهى بسبب الخطوة الأولى إلى ذلك الحد المعين من المسافة ، وإلا امتنع كون تلك الداعية موجبة لانتقاله من ذلك الحد المعين إلى حد آخر

فيثبت بهذا : أن المؤثر في جميع هذه الحوادث موجود باقي مبرأ عن جميع جهات التغيرات ، إلا أن تأثيره في حدوث كل حادث متأخر ، مشروط بتقدم الحادث المتقدم، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر ولا إلى أول . وعند هذا قالوا : ظهر أنه لو انقطع حدوث الحوادث لحظة واحدة ، لامتنع حدوث الحوادث بعد ذلك أبداً ، وظهر أنه لا يمكن إسناد الحوادث المتعاقبة إلى المؤثر القديم ، إلا بهذا الطريق . قالوا : وهذا طريق معقول يتيسر على مذهبنا ، ولم يتيسر لأحد من أرباب المذاهب سوانا ، فكان ذلك دليلا على شرف قولنا ، وقوة كلامنا .

قال المتكلمون والمحققون : لا شك أنكم بالغتم في التدقيق والتحقيق إلا أن البحث الغامض باقي كما كان ، وذلك لأنا نقول : لا شك أن العلة المؤثرية القديمة، ما كانت موجودة لهذا الحادث المعين ، حال ما كان الحادث المتقدم عليه موجوداً ، ثم صارت في الوقت الثاني موجدة لهذا الحادث ، فصيرورة تلك العلة موجدة لهذا الحادث بعد أنها ما كانت كذلك : حكم حادث. فهذا الحكم الحادث، إما أن يفتقر إلى مؤثر ، وإما أن لا يفتقر إليه.. فإن كان الثاني فقد حدث أمر من الأمور لا لمؤثر ، وإذا عقل هذا في البعض ، فليعقل مثله في الكل. وهذا يوجب استغناء الممكن المحدث عن المؤثر . وهو محال

وأما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال : حدوث تلك المؤثرية والموجدية ،
The philosophers said: the answer to this question is that the emanation principle (al-mabda' al-fayyāḍ) exists eternally and perpetually, except that the condition for the emanation of every temporal event from it is the passing away of the temporal event that existed before it. For this reason we said: it must be that every temporal event is preceded by another temporal event, without a first. And the cause existing for all, effective in achieving all, is that eternal (al-qadīm). They said: and what establishes that the matter is so is that motions are either natural or coercive or voluntary. And the meaning we mentioned is present in all of these three divisions.

As for natural motions, they are like the falling of a stone from above to below. Its demonstration is that the necessitator of that falling is the heaviness (al-thiqal) present in the substance of the stone, and that heaviness remains in all times unchanging, except that its effect in necessitating each part of the parts of that motion is conditioned by the passing away of the preceding part. That is because if that body had not ended by its preceding motion at that specific limit of the distance, then it would be impossible for the heaviness subsisting in it to be a necessitator of its transfer from that specific limit to another limit. So the necessitator of each part of the parts of that motion is that heaviness. As for the condition of that heaviness being effective in that specific part of the motion, it is its arrival by the preceding part of the motion at that specific part of the distance. As for coercive motions, the matter is also so, and that is because when a person throws a stone upward, he has placed in it a force (quwwa) that necessitates the ascent of that stone upward coercively. So the necessitator of all the parts of that motion is that force, except that the effect of the force in the specific part of that motion is conditioned by its ending by the preceding part of the motion at that specific limit. As for voluntary motions, the matter is also so. Because when a person wants to go to such-and-such a town to find his debtor and demand payment of the debt, this motive (al-dā'iyya) is the necessitator of his transfer from this town to that, and this motive remains with all the parts occurring in that motion, except that the preceding step is a condition for that motive being effective in causing the second step. For if that arrival had not ended due to the first step at that specific limit of the distance, then it would be impossible for that motive to be a necessitator of his transfer from that specific limit to another limit.

So it is established by this that the effecter (al-mu'aththir) in all these temporal events is existent, eternal, free from all aspects of changes, except that its effect in causing each subsequent temporal event is conditioned by the precedence of the preceding temporal event. And this requires that every temporal event be preceded by another temporal event without a first. And at this they said: it has become apparent that if the occurrence of temporal events were interrupted for a single moment, it would be impossible for temporal events to occur after that ever, and it has become apparent that it is impossible to ascribe successive temporal events to the eternal effecter except by this way. They said: and this is a rational way that is feasible on our doctrine, and was not feasible for anyone of the adherents of doctrines other than us. So that was a proof of the honor of our speech and the strength of our discourse.

The theologians (al-mutakallimūn) and the investigators (al-muḥaqqiqūn) said: There is no doubt that you have gone far in exactness and investigation, but the obscure research remains as it was. That is because we say: There is no doubt that the eternal causal cause (al-‘illa al-mu’aththiriyya al-qadīma) was not causing this specific temporal event to exist while the preceding temporal event was existing, then in the second time it became causing this temporal event to exist. So the becoming of that cause a cause of existence for this temporal event after it was not so is an occurring state (ḥukm ḥādith). This occurring state either stands in need of an effecter or does not stand in need of it. If the second, then a matter from among matters has occurred without an effecter, and if this is conceived in some, then let the like of it be conceived in all. And this necessitates the independence of the contingent temporal thing from the effecter, and that is impossible (muḥāl). As for the first division, which is that it be said: the occurrence of that effecting and causing existence...
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed philosophical argument regarding causal conditioning and the theologians' response. It begins with the philosophers affirming an eternal principle (al-mabda' al-fayyāḍ) that is always existent, but they assert that each temporal event emanating from it is conditioned by the prior temporal event's passing away. This leads to an infinite regress of causes without a first event. They then illustrate this with three types of motion: natural (like a stone falling due to heaviness), coercive (like a stone thrown upward by force), and voluntary (like a person walking to another town due to a motive). In each case, the constant cause (heaviness, force, motive) remains unchanging, but its effect on each subsequent part of motion is conditioned by the completion of the preceding part. Thus, the eternal effecter (al-mu'aththir) is unchanging but its influence on each new event depends on the prior event's termination. The philosophers conclude that this demonstrates the impossibility of any gap in the chain of events and that this rational method is unique to their school, proving its superiority.

The theologians and investigators (al-mutakallimūn wa al-muḥaqqiqūn) respond by acknowledging the philosophers' precision but pointing out a remaining deep issue: the eternal cause's transition from not effecting a specific event (while the prior event existed) to effecting it (after the prior event's passing) is itself a new occurring state (ḥukm ḥādith). This new state requires a cause. If it has no cause, then something comes to be without a cause, which is absurd and would imply that contingent things can exist without an effecter—a contradiction. If it does have a cause, then that cause would be another temporal state, leading to an infinite regress. The text cuts off before fully discussing the second option, but the core objection is clear: the philosophers' account fails to explain the change in the causal relation itself.

The Ash'ari Proof of a Perpetual Cause for Successive Events
Explains how the eternal agent causes successive events conditioned by prior ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that an eternal cause (God) creates successive temporal events, with each event conditioned by the previous one, and why this implies an unbroken chain.

Core Idea
God's eternal power creates each event conditioned on the previous one, not through change.

The Ash'ari school argues that the world's events are directly created by God, but not arbitrarily. This passage presents a rational proof using the example of a person's intention while walking. It shows that the eternal cause remains constant, but its effect on later events depends on prior events having occurred. This ensures the continuity of creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the walking example
Read the example of a person intending to move from one town to another. Notice that the intention (dā'iyah) remains throughout the movement, but each step is conditioned by the previous step having reached a certain point.
The intention is the cause, but the prior step is a condition for its effectiveness.
2
Extract the general principle
From the example, the author generalizes: the eternal effecter (God) is the cause of all events, but His causation of each later event is conditioned by the prior event having occurred.
This avoids attributing change to God or making the prior event an independent cause.
3
Grasp the chain implication
The conditioning requirement forces an unbroken sequence: every event must be preceded by another, with no first event. If the chain stopped, no later event could occur.
This supports the Ash'ari view of continuous creation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه الداعية هي الموجبة لانتقاله من هذا البلد إلى ذلك ، وهذه الداعية باقية مع جميع الأجزاء الحاصلة في تلك الحركة ، إلا أن الخطوة المتقدمة ، شرط لكون تلك الداعية مؤثرة في حصول الخطوة الثانية ، فإنه لولا أن ذلك الحصول إن إنتهى بسبب الخطوة الأولى إلى ذلك الحد المعين من المسافة ، وإلا امتنع كون تلك الداعية موجبة لانتقاله من ذلك الحد الميعن إلى حد آخر . فيثبت بهذا : أن المؤثر في جميع هذه الحوادث موجود باق مبرأ عن جميع جهات التغيرات ، إلا أن تأثيره في حدوث كل حادث متأخر ، مشروط بتقدم الحادث المتقدم، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر ولا إلى أول . وعند هذا قالوا : ظهر أنه لو انقطع حدوث الحوادث لحظة واحدة ، لامتنع حدوث الحوادث بعد ذلك أبداً ، وظهر أنه لا يمكن إسناد الحوادث المتعاقبة إلى المؤثر القديم ، إلا بهذا الطريق . قالوا : وهذا طريق معقول يتيسر على مذهبنا ، ولم يتيسر لأحد من أرباب المذاهب سوانا ، فكان ذلك دليلا على شرف قولنا ، وقوة كلامنا .
This intention (dā'iyah) is what necessitates his transfer from this town to that, and this intention remains with all the parts occurring in that movement, except that the preceding step is a condition for that intention being effective in the occurrence of the second step. For if that occurrence had not ended due to the first step at that specified limit of distance, then it would be impossible for that intention to necessitate his transfer from that specified limit to another limit. So it is established by this: that the effecter (mu'aththir) in all these events is an existent, perpetual, free from all aspects of changes, except that His effect in the occurrence of each subsequent event is conditioned by the prior event, and this requires that every event be preceded by another event, and so on to no first. And at this they said: it became clear that if the occurrence of events ceased for a single moment, the occurrence of events after that would be impossible forever, and it became clear that it is not possible to attribute the successive events to the eternal effecter (al-mu'aththir al-qadīm) except in this way. They said: this is a rational way that is feasible on our madhhab, and not feasible for any of the adherents of other madhhabs besides us, so this was a proof of the honor of our statement and the strength of our speech.

Commentary
This passage provides the Ash'ari proof for a perpetual cause of temporal events. It begins by analyzing a concrete example: a person intending to move from one place to another. The intention (dā'iyah) remains throughout the movement, but the effectiveness of that intention in producing each subsequent step is conditioned by the preceding step having reached a certain point. This shows that the eternal effecter (al-mu'aththir al-qadīm) – God – is the cause of all events, but His causation of each later event is conditioned by the prior event having occurred. Consequently, there must be an unbroken chain of events; if the chain were to stop for even a moment, no later event could ever occur. The author concludes that this rational proof is unique to the Ash'ari school and demonstrates its superiority.

The Mutakallimun's Objection: Causativity Itself Is Temporal
Raises a dilemma: the temporality of causativity requires a cause, leading to five alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a powerful objection to the Ash'ari argument from continuous creation, which argues that the very 'causativity' of the eternal agent is a temporal event requiring its own cause, and how the division into possibilities challenges the proof of a Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
If causativity itself is temporal, it needs a cause—leading to a dilemma that questions the proof of a Necessary Existent.

This lesson presents a critical objection raised by the mutakallimūn and the muḥaqqiqūn against the Ash'ari argument for continuous creation. The objection focuses on the temporality of the causal relationship between the eternal agent and each new event. By examining all possibilities for the cause of this temporal causativity, the objection reveals a deep challenge to the standard proof of a Necessary Existent. The passage is a masterful exercise in rational division (taqsīm) and will sharpen your understanding of causality and theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Try to follow the flow of the argument from the initial claim to the conclusion.
The passage ends mid-sentence listing five alternatives; that is the intended endpoint.
2
Identify the core claim
The objection begins by stating that the eternal cause's 'causativity' (mu'aththiriyyah) for a specific event is itself a temporal event (ḥukm ḥādith). Understand why this is a problem: if the cause was not causing before and then starts causing, that change is an event needing its own cause.
3
Trace the division tree
Follow the logical division: the temporal causativity either needs a cause or not. If not, causality is undermined. If it does, the cause is either non-existence or existence. Each branch leads to further rejections or consequences. Use the guided breakdown to map the tree.
Pay attention to how the philosophers' acceptance of infinite regress is used to block the proof of a Necessary Existent.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that this certain division forces one of five alternatives. Although the five are not listed here, reflect on what they might be based on the branches. Consider how this objection challenges the Ash'ari argument from the previous lesson.
You may want to brainstorm the five possibilities: e.g., 1) accept uncaused event, 2) accept causation by non-existence, 3) accept infinite regress of events, 4) accept circularity, 5) accept infinite regress of simultaneous causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المتكلمون والمحققون : لا شك أنكم بالغتم في التدقيق والتحقيق إلا أن البحث الغامض باقي كما كان ، وذلك لأنا نقول : لا شك أن العلة المؤثرية القديمة، ما كانت موجودة لهذا الحادث المعين ، حال ما كان الحادث المتقدم عليه موجوداً ، ثم صارت في الوقت الثاني مـوجـدة لهذا الحادث ، فصيرورة تلك العلة موجدة لهذا الحادث بعد أنها ما كانت كذلك : حكم حادث. فهذا الحكم الحادث، إما أن يفتقر إلى مؤثر ، وإما أن لا يفتقر إليه.. فإن كان الثاني فقد حدث أمر من الأمور لا لمؤثر ، وإذا عقل هذا في البعض ، فليعقل مثله في الكل. وهذا يوجب استغناء الممكن المحدث عن المؤثر . وهو محال. وأما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال : حدوث تلك المؤثرية والموجدية ، لا بد له من علة . فذلك المؤثر إما أن يكون عدماً ، أو وجوداً . فإن كان الأول ، وهو أن يقال : المؤثر في حدوث هذه المؤثرية ، عدم ذلك الحادث السابق ، فهذا باطل . لأنه لو جاز اسناد المعلول الموجد إلى العدم المحض في بعض الصور ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في سائر الصور ؟ وإذا كان كذلك ، فحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بوجود شيء من الممكنات على مؤثر موجود . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : تلك المؤثرية الحادثة ، لا بد لها من مؤثر موجود . فنقول : ذلك الموجود إما أن يقال : إنه هو الذي كان متقدماً عليه ، أو يجب أن يكون مقارناً لـه [ فإن كان الأول ] فلنجوز مثله في سائر الحوادث ، وحينئذ يلزم من تجويزه أن يقال : المقتضي لوجود كل حادث حادث [ آخر سابق [ عليه ] ] أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : هذا يوجب حدوث حوادث ، لا أول لها . إلا أنا نقول : هذا غير ممتنع عند جميع الفلاسفة ، بل هذا عين مذهبهم ، وصريح معتقدهم . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بوجود هذه الممكنات على وجود واجب الوجود ، وأما إن قلنا : إن تلك المؤثرية المتجددة ، لا بد لها من مؤثر موجود يكون حاصلا معها ، فذلك المؤثر إن كان هو الذي فرضناه أثراً له ، لزم الدور ، وإن كان موجوداً آخر ، لزم وقوع التسلسل في أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها، تحصل دفعة واحدة ، وذلك محال . فظهر بهذا التقسيم اليقيني : أن حدوث الحوادث اليومية لا بد فيها من التزام [ أحد ] أمور خمسة :
The mutakallimūn and the muḥaqqiqūn said: There is no doubt that you have reached the utmost in precision and investigation, but the deep research remains as before, because we say: There is no doubt that the eternal causal agent (al-'illah al-mu'aththiriyyah al-qadīmah) was not an existentifier (mūjidah) for this specific event at the time when the preceding event was existing, then in the second moment it became an existentifier for this event. So the becoming of that cause as an existentifier for this event after it was not so: a temporal event (ḥukm ḥādith). So this temporal event either needs a cause (mu'aththir) or does not need it. If the latter, then something has occurred that is without a cause, and if this is conceivable in some cases, then let its like be conceivable in all. This would entail that a contingent temporal thing (al-mumkin al-muḥdath) is independent of a cause, which is impossible. As for the first division: that the occurrence of that causativity (mu'aththiriyyah) and existentification (mūjidiyyah) must have a cause. Then that cause is either non-existence ('adam) or existence (wujūd). If the first, that is, it is said: the cause of the occurrence of this causativity is the non-existence of that prior event, this is false. For if it were permissible to attribute a caused effect (al-ma'lūl al-mūjad) to pure non-existence in some instances, why would its like not be permissible in all instances? If that were the case, then one could not infer from the existence of any contingent thing to an existent cause. As for the second division: that this temporal causativity must have an existent cause. We say: that existent is either said to be what preceded it, or it must be simultaneous with it. If the first, then let us allow its like in all other events, and then from allowing it it would follow that the requisite for the existence of every event is another event preceding it. The most that can be said is that this would necessitate the occurrence of events with no first. Except that we say: this is not impossible according to all the philosophers; rather this is the very doctrine and explicit belief of theirs. If that is the case, then one cannot infer from the existence of these contingent things to the existence of the Necessary Existent. And if we say: that renewed causativity must have an existent cause that is present with it, then if that cause is the one we supposed as its effect, circularity (dawr) follows; and if it is another existent, then an infinite regress (tasalsul) of causes and effects without end, occurring all at once, follows, and that is impossible. So from this certain division it appears that the occurrence of daily events necessarily involves the acceptance of one of five matters:
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents a powerful objection raised by the mutakallimūn and muḥaqqiqūn against the Ash'ari proof from the previous lesson. They argue that the very 'causativity' (mu'aththiriyyah) of the eternal agent with respect to any particular event is itself a temporal happening (ḥukm ḥādith) because at one moment the agent is not causing that event, and at the next moment it is. This temporal attribute must itself have a cause. The objection then proceeds to analyze all possibilities for this cause:
1. It may need no cause, which would imply that some things occur without a cause, undermining the principle of causality.
2. It may need a cause, which is then subdivided:
   a. The cause is non-existence (the prior event's non-existence). This is rejected because if non-existence can cause something, causality could be dispensed with entirely.
   b. The cause is an existent. That existent is either prior to the causativity or simultaneous.
      - If prior, then that prior cause is itself a temporal event requiring a cause, leading to an infinite regress of events (which the philosophers actually accept as possible). If such an infinite regress is allowed, it becomes impossible to prove a Necessary Existent.
      - If simultaneous, then that cause is either the same as the effect (circularity) or another existent (infinite regress of simultaneous causes and effects, which is impossible).
The passage concludes that the analysis forces one to accept one of five alternatives (though the five are not fully enumerated here). This objection critically examines the coherence of the Ash'ari model of continuous creation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection disproves the existence of God (the Necessary Existent).
Correction: The objection does not disprove God's existence; it challenges a specific Ash'ari argument for God from continuous creation by showing that the model of causativity leads to a dilemma. The objection is a critique of a particular account of causality, not of theism itself.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what is the problem if the cause of the temporal causativity is an existent simultaneous with it?
A) It leads to an infinite regress of simultaneous causes.
B) It makes God changeable.
C) It proves the impossibility of creation.
D) It requires the existence of multiple gods.

Key Takeaway
The temporality of causativity leads to a dilemma: either something happens without a cause, or one must accept an infinite regress or circularity, challenging the proof of a Necessary Existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter intellectual doubts about the proofs for God's existence—like this objection—does it unsettle your faith, or do you trust that God's reality is beyond all logical puzzles? Examine your heart: is your faith built on rational certainty or on a living relationship with the Creator? This week, commit to spending extra time in supplication asking God to strengthen your conviction in His existence beyond mere arguments.
?
The mutakallimun's objection reveals that even the most precise reasoning can lead to dilemmas. How do you respond when your understanding of God's actions seems to face logical difficulties? Do you become anxious, or do you humbly accept that human intellect has limits? Let this lesson remind you that God's wisdom is far greater than our comprehension. Take a moment now to recite 'آمَنَّا بِهِ كُلٌّ مِّنْ عِندِ رَبِّنَا' (We believe in it; all is from our Lord) and reflect on its meaning for your own faith journey.
?
If the proof for a Necessary Existent seems to fail under this objection, what then is the ultimate ground for your certainty in God? Is it reason alone, or is it the witness of creation in your heart, the guidance of the Qur'an, or the sweetness of faith? This lesson challenges you to find a foundation that cannot be shaken by any objection. What is that foundation for you? Resolve today to strengthen it through sincere worship and seeking knowledge with humility.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Division of Causes: Non-Existence and Preceding Existence
Sanusi divides possible causes into non-existence and existence, then examines the first two sub-branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author divides the possible causes of an event into non-existence and existence, and why the first two options (non-existence cause and preceding cause) fail to establish a Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
A cause cannot be non-existence, and a preceding cause leads to infinite regress.

The author begins by stating that every event must have a cause. He then divides the possible cause into either non-existence or existence. In this lesson, we explore the first two branches: the absurdity of a non-existent cause and the implications of a preceding cause, which the philosophers themselves accept but which undermines their argument for a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening premise
The author asserts that every event necessarily has a cause. Note the term 'علة'.
2
Understand the first division
The cause is either non-existence or existence. The first option is dismissed because it would allow any effect to come from nothing, making inference to a cause impossible.
3
Explore the second division: preceding cause
If the cause is existence, it either precedes or is simultaneous. For preceding, it leads to an infinite series of events with no first, which the philosophers accept, thus their proof for a Necessary Existent fails.
4
Reflect on the dilemma
The philosopher is forced into accepting infinite regress, which defeats his purpose of proving a Creator.
This shows the argument's dialectical power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بد له من علة . فذلك المؤثر إما أن يكون عدماً ، أو وجوداً . فإن كان الأول ، وهو أن يقال : المؤثر في حدوث هذه المؤثرية ، عدم ذلك الحادث السابق ، فهذا باطل . لأنه لو جاز اسناد المعلول الموجد إلى العدم المحض في بعض الصور ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في سائر الصور ؟ وإذا كان كذلك ، فحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بوجود شيء من الممكنات على مؤثر موجود . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : تلك المؤثرية الحادثة ، لا بد لها من مؤثر موجود . فنقول : ذلك الموجود إما أن يقال : إنه هو الذي كان متقدماً عليه ، أو يجب أن يكون مقارناً له . فإن كان الأول فلنجوز مثله في سائر الحوادث ، وحينئذ يلزم من تجويزه أن يقال : المقتضي لوجود كل حادث حادث آخر سابق عليه . أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : هذا يوجب حدوث حوادث ، لا أول لها . إلا أنا نقول : هذا غير ممتنع عند جميع الفلاسفة ، بل هذا عين مذهبهم ، وصريح معتقدهم . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بوجود هذه الممكنات على وجود واجب الوجود ، وأما إن قلنا : إن تلك المؤثرية المتجددة ، لا بد لها من مؤثر موجود يكون حاصلا معها ، فذلك المؤثر إن كان هو الذي فرضناه أثراً له ، لزم الدور ، وإن كان موجوداً آخر ، لزم وقوع التسلسل في أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها، تحصل دفعة واحدة ، وذلك محال . فظهر بهذا التقسيم اليقيني : أن حدوث الحوادث اليومية لا بد فيها من التزام أحد أمور خمسة : أولها : وقوع الحادث لا عن سبب مؤثر . وثانيها : إسناد الأثر الموجود إلى مؤثر معدوم . وثالثها : إستناد الأثر الموجود في الحال ، إلى مؤثر كان موجوداً قبل . ورابعها : التزام الدور . وخامسها : التزام التسلسل . ومتى التزم الفيلسوف واحد من هذه المقدمات الخمس ، فسد عليه دليل إثبات الصانع . فهذا سؤال قاهر في هذا المقام .
It necessarily has a cause. That effecting factor is either non-existence or existence. If the first, that is, it is said: the cause in the occurrence of this effecting-property is the non-existence of that previous event, then this is false. Because if attributing the effect produced to pure non-existence were permissible in some cases, why is it not permissible similarly in all cases? And if that is so, then one cannot infer from the existence of any possible thing to an existing cause. As for the second division: that it is said: that originated effecting-property necessarily has an existing cause. We say: that existing entity is either said to be that which preceded it, or must be simultaneous with it. If the first, then let us allow the like in all events, and then from allowing it it would follow that the necessitating factor for the existence of every event is another event preceding it. The utmost that can be said is: this necessitates an occurrence of events with no first. But we say: this is not impossible according to all philosophers; rather this is precisely their doctrine and explicit belief. And if the matter is so, then one cannot infer from the existence of these possibles to the existence of the Necessary Existent. And if we say: that renewed effecting-property must have an existing cause that occurs with it, then that cause, if it is that which we had supposed to be its effect, circularity follows; and if it is another existent, then there follows an occurrence of infinite regress in causes and effects with no end, occurring all at once, and that is impossible. So it becomes manifest by this certain division: that the occurrence of daily events necessarily requires committing to one of five matters: First: the event occurs without an effecting cause. Second: attributing the existing effect to a non-existent cause. Third: the existing effect in the present is based on a cause that existed before. Fourth: committing to circularity. Fifth: committing to infinite regress. And whenever the philosopher commits to one of these five premises, his proof for establishing the Maker is invalidated. So this is an irrefutable objection in this context.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is an argument by division (taqsīm) designed to force the opponent into an impossible choice. It opens with the premise that every originated event (ḥādith) must have a cause (ʿilla). The author divides the possible cause into two: non-existence (ʿadam) or existence (wujūd). The first option, attributing the effect to pure non-existence, is dismissed as absurd because if it were allowed in one case it would be allowed in all, making inference to an existing cause impossible. The second option is then subdivided: the existing cause either precedes the event or is simultaneous with it. If it precedes, then each event is caused by a prior event, leading to an infinite series of events with no first. The author notes that the philosophers themselves accept an infinite series of events, so this path actually supports their position, making it impossible to use their premises to prove a Necessary Existent. If the cause is simultaneous, then either it is the same as the effect (circularity) or it is another existent (infinite regress of simultaneous causes). Both are impossible. Thus, the author concludes that any explanation of daily events must fall into one of five unacceptable positions: (1) event without a cause, (2) effect from a non-existent cause, (3) present effect from a past cause (which leads to regress), (4) circularity, or (5) infinite regress. If a philosopher accepts any of these, his proof for the existence of a Creator collapses. This is presented as a decisive objection (sūʾāl qāhir).

Key Terms
علة
مؤثر
عدم
وجود
حادث
مؤثرية
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author rejects infinite regress absolutely.
Correction: The author does not reject infinite regress; he points out that philosophers accept it, which undermines their proof for a Necessary Existent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is attributing an effect to non-existence false?
A) Because it leads to infinite regress.
B) Because it would be permissible in all cases, making inference from existence to a cause impossible.
C) Because the philosophers do not accept it.
D) Because it requires a simultaneous cause.

Key Takeaway
The first two options (non-existence cause and preceding cause) fail to prove a Necessary Existent; the first is absurd, the second leads to infinite regress accepted by philosophers, invalidating their proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face something you cannot explain, do you default to attributing it to 'nothing' or to random chance, ignoring that every event has a cause? How does this lesson challenge that tendency?
?
The philosophers' belief in an infinite chain of causes leads to no proof for God. Have you ever accepted a worldview that logically excludes God, even while claiming to believe? Is there any area of your life where you are implicitly committed to an infinite regress of explanations that never reach God?
?
This passage shows that a wrong premise (attributing effects to non-existence) destroys all inference to a Creator. Are there any premises in your own thinking that, if applied consistently, would make it impossible to know God? How can you guard against that?
