The Simultaneous Cause and the Five Impossible Consequences
Sanusi completes the division with simultaneous cause, lists the five impossible consequences, and declares the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the remaining branches of the division: simultaneous cause leading to circularity or infinite regress, and the five impossible consequences that undermine any philosophical proof for a Creator.

Core Idea
A simultaneous cause leads either to circularity or infinite regress, both impossible; thus five unacceptable options remain.

Having shown that a preceding cause leads to an infinite series that philosophers accept, the author now examines the alternative: a cause simultaneous with the event. This leads to either circularity or an infinite regress of simultaneous causes. Both are impossible. The author then concludes that any explanation of everyday events must fall into one of five unacceptable positions, which would invalidate the proof for a Maker.

Guided Path
1
Read the simultaneous cause discussion
The author considers the case where the cause occurs simultaneously with the event. This cause either is the effect itself (circularity) or another existent (infinite regress of simultaneous causes). Both are impossible.
2
Identify the impossible circularity
If the cause is the same as the effect, that is circular (دور). The author states this leads to impossibility.
3
Identify the impossible regress
If it's another existent, then it requires an infinite chain of simultaneous causes and effects, which is impossible.
4
Review the five consequences
The author enumerates five necessary outcomes if one tries to explain everyday events: event without cause, effect from non-existent cause, effect from a past cause (which is the preceding case), circularity, and infinite regress.
5
Understand the refutation
If a philosopher accepts any one of these, his proof for the Maker is invalidated. This is presented as an irrefutable objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The author notes that the philosophers themselves accept an infinite series of events, so this path actually supports their position, making it impossible to use their premises to prove a Necessary Existent. If the cause is simultaneous, then either it is the same as the effect (circularity) or it is another existent (infinite regress of simultaneous causes). Both are impossible. Thus, the author concludes that any explanation of daily events must fall into one of five unacceptable positions: (1) event without a cause, (2) effect from a non-existent cause, (3) present effect from a past cause (which leads to regress), (4) circularity, or (5) infinite regress. If a philosopher accepts any of these, his proof for the existence of a Creator collapses. This is presented as a decisive objection (sūʾāl qāhir).

Key Terms
مؤثرية
دور
تسلسل
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five consequences are all equally valid logical possibilities.
Correction: The five are not all logically possible; some (like event without cause) are absurd, but the author lists them as the only outcomes if one does not accept an external necessary cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the five consequences that would invalidate the proof for the Maker?
A) Event occurring without an effecting cause.
B) Effect attributed to a non-existent cause.
C) Effect stemming from a cause that is itself an effect.
D) Committing to circularity.

Key Takeaway
The simultaneous cause option leads to either circularity or infinite regress, both impossible; thus the only remaining explanations for daily events are the five unacceptable ones, which refute the philosopher's proof.

The Philosophers' Strategy: Reducing Nine Objections to One
Philosophers claim nine objections reduce to one principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers respond to the nine objections by reducing them to a single core issue about preeternal conditions and the need for a preponderator.

Core Idea
All objections collapse into one: either eternity lacked conditions, or a preponderator is unnecessary.

After laying out nine objections against the philosophers' proof, the author now presents the philosophers' response. They do not tackle each objection individually; instead, they claim all nine stem from a single misunderstanding. This lesson introduces their unifying strategy and begins to examine the first answer. Pay close attention to how they frame the debate around God's will and time.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' opening claim
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how the philosophers state that all nine objections reduce to one point.
The text is incomplete; it cuts off during the explanation of the first answer.
2
Identify the core principle
Find the phrase that captures the reduction: 'either what is necessary for creation was not present in preeternity, or the possible does not need a preponderator.'
This is the key insight the philosophers want to pivot on.
3
Connect to the first answer
See how the first objection about God's will and time is rephrased in terms of presence of time in eternity.
The philosophers are reframing the objection to fit their core principle.
4
Reflect on the strategy
Consider why reducing objections to one principle is a powerful debate strategy. How does it simplify the defense?
This is a rhetorical move as much as a logical one.

The Five Premises That Invalidate the Philosopher's Proof
Enumeration of five premises that invalidate the proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five premises that would undermine the philosopher's proof of the Creator and why each is a logical problem.

Core Idea
Five logical traps that would collapse the philosophical argument for God.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the philosopher's proof of the Creator. It lists five premises that, if accepted, would invalidate the entire proof. Each premise is a logical fallacy or problematic assumption. Understanding these helps us see why the philosopher must avoid them to maintain a valid argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: it lists five items clearly.
The passage uses ordinal terms (first, second, etc.) to enumerate.
2
Identify each of the five premises
Look at each premise individually. The first is about something happening without an effective cause; the second attributes an effect to a nonexistent cause; the third bases a present effect on a past cause; the fourth involves circularity; the fifth infinite regress.
Each premise is a distinct logical error.
3
Understand why each premise is problematic
For each premise, consider why it undermines the proof. For example, if an event occurs without a cause, then the causal chain is broken, and the proof that requires a First Cause fails.
4
Consider the overall conclusion
The passage states that if the philosopher accepts any one of these premises, his entire proof collapses. This makes it a compelling objection.
The author calls it a 'compelling objection'.
5
Reflect on the strength of this objection

Each is a logical problem. The author concludes that accepting any one of these destroys the philosopher's proof for the Creator, making this a powerful challenge.

Reduction and Refutation of Nine Answers
The author reduces nine answers to two core claims and refutes both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author reduces nine objections to two core claims and refutes both, thereby reaffirming the necessity of a cause for contingent existence.

Core Idea
Nine objections collapse to two false premises; refuting them safeguards the proof of God.

In this passage, the author systematically dismantles nine objections to the classical proof from contingency. He shows that all these objections ultimately rely on one of two mistaken assumptions: either that contingent things can exist without a cause, or that all conditions for causality were eternally present. By exposing the absurd consequences of each, he clears the way for the theistic argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reduction
The author reduces nine answers to two core claims. Identify these two claims and see how they summarise the objections.
2
Refutation of the first claim
The first claim—that contingents are independent of a cause—is refuted by two aspects: it contradicts a self-evident truth and closes the door to proving God.
Study each aspect carefully.
3
Refutation of the second claim
The second claim—that all necessary conditions were present in pre-eternity—is refuted by showing it leads to an impossible regress.
4
Clarify what 'necessary for effectiveness' includes
The author lists the components of a complete cause, which the nine answers fail to satisfy.
This shows where the objections go wrong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت : أن هذه الجوابات التسعة راجعة ، إما إلى القول بأن الممكن المتساوي الطرفين غني عن المرجح والمؤثر وإما إلى القول بأن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية، ما كان حاصلا في الأزل . إلا أنا نقول : القول بأن الممكن غني عن المؤثر : قول باطل ، من وجهين :
الأول : أنه مقدمة بديهية أولية فالقدح فيه قدح في البديهيات
والثاني : إنا إذا حكمنا بأن الممكن قد يترجح أحد طرفيه على الآخر لا المرجح أصلا ، فحينئذ لا يمكننا أن نستدل بحدوث المحدثات ، وبإمكان الممكنات على افتقارها إلى مرجح ومؤثر ، وحينئذ ينسد علينا باب إثبات الصانع ، وأما القول بأن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ما كان حاصلا في الأزل ، فقد أبطلناه . حيث ذكرنا : أن مجموع ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ، إن لم يكن حاصلا في الأزل ، ثم صار حاصلاً فيما لا يزال ، فحينئذ إن قلنا : إنه حدث لا عن مؤثر ، عاد المحال المذكور .
وإن قلنا : إنه حدث لمؤثر، عاد التقسيم في كيفية تأثير المؤثر في تحصيل ذلك المجموع . فثبت : أن هذه الأجوبة التسعة ليست أجوبة عن الحجة البتة أصلا
واعلم أن قولنا : كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ، يدخل فيه ذات المؤثر ، والشرائط المعتبرة في مؤثرية المؤثر ، وزوال جميع الموانع ، وحضور الوقت المناسب وحضور المصالح المعتبرة . والأجوبة التسعة مشتركة في أن أحد هذه الأمور المعتبرة فاتت ، وحينئذ لا يكون المؤثر التام ، حاصلا في الأزل
فهذا بحث شريف عالي
So it is established that these nine answers reduce to either (1) the statement that the contingent thing whose two sides are equal is independent of the preponderator (murajjih) and the efficient cause (mu'aththir), or (2) the statement that whatever is necessary for effectiveness was present in pre-eternity (al-azal). But we say: the statement that the contingent is independent of the efficient cause is a false statement, from two aspects:
First: It is a primary intuitive premise (muqaddima badihiyya awwaliyya), so challenging it is a challenge to self-evident truths.
Second: If we judge that a contingent may have one of its sides preponderate over the other without any preponderator at all, then we cannot infer from the temporal origination of originated things and the contingency of contingent things their need for a preponderator and efficient cause, and thus the door to proving the Maker (ithbat al-sani') would be closed to us. As for the statement that whatever is necessary for effectiveness was present in pre-eternity, we have already refuted it, for we mentioned that the totality of what is necessary for effectiveness, if it was not present in pre-eternity and then became present in what is not-yet (fima la yazal), then if we say it occurred without an efficient cause, the aforementioned impossibility returns; and if we say it occurred from an efficient cause, the division returns regarding how the efficient cause affects the acquisition of that totality. So it is established that these nine answers are not answers to the argument at all.
Know that our statement 'whatever is necessary for effectiveness' includes the essence of the efficient cause (dhat al-mu'aththir), the conditions considered in the effectiveness of the efficient cause (al-shara'it al-mu'tabara fi mu'aththiriyyat al-mu'aththir), the removal of all obstacles (zawal jami' al-mawani'), the presence of the appropriate time (hudur al-waqt al-munasib), and the presence of the considered benefits (hudur al-masalih al-mu'tabara). The nine answers share that one of these considered matters was absent, and then the complete efficient cause (al-mu'aththir al-tamm) was not present in pre-eternity. This is a noble and elevated discussion.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of nine answers to a kalam argument (likely the argument from contingency for the existence of God). The author first reduces the nine answers to two core underlying claims: either they claim that a contingent thing with equal possibilities does not need a preponderator or efficient cause, or they claim that all necessary conditions for effectiveness were present in pre-eternity. He then refutes the first claim from two angles: first, it contradicts a self-evident primary premise (badihiyya) — the need for a cause for contingents is intuitive; second, if we accept that contingents can happen without a cause, then we cannot use the argument from contingency or temporal origination to prove the existence of the Creator, thus closing the door to theism. He then refutes the second claim: if the totality of necessary conditions for effectiveness was not present in pre-eternity but later became present, then either it occurred without a cause (impossible, because it would be the same problem) or it occurred from a cause (leading to an infinite regress regarding how that cause produced the totality). Therefore, the nine answers are invalid. Finally, he clarifies what 'all that is necessary for effectiveness' includes: the essence of the agent, the conditions for its effectiveness, removal of obstacles, appropriate time, and considered benefits. The nine answers all fail because at least one of these was absent, so the complete cause was not present in pre-eternity.

Correction: The author shows they rely on false premises that undermine the very possibility of proving God, making the refutation substantive and necessary.

The First Proof Against the First Answer: The Condition of Time
First proof against the first answer using a time dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma the author presents to refute the claim that Allah's will is necessarily attached to a specific time and independent of cause.

Core Idea
If the time of creation was present in pre-eternity, the world would be eternal; if not, it becomes a condition contradicting necessity.

The author now moves to the second station: refuting each of the nine answers individually. The first answer asserts that Allah's will being attached to a specific time is necessary and thus needs no cause. The author declares this false and provides the first of several proofs, centering on the role of that specific time. This proof uses a dilemma to expose the inconsistency: either the time was present in pre-eternity or it was not, and both options lead to problems.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise logical structure: a conditional chain.
2
Identify the dilemma's two branches
The passage presents two possibilities: (1) the specific time was present in pre-eternity, (2) it was not. Trace how the author handles each branch and note the conclusion for each.
The first branch leads to eternal effect; the second branch makes time a condition that was already present.
3
Understand the refutation's force
The author claims that all conditions for Allah's effectiveness were present in pre-eternity. Therefore, even if time was not present initially, it must have been present as a condition, leading again to eternal effect. This contradicts the temporal origination of the world.
Thus the first answer fails because it cannot avoid the implication of eternal creation.
4
Connect to the broader context
This proof is the first of several arguments against the first answer. Recognize that the entire discussion aims to defend the temporal creation of the world against philosophical objections.
The author assumes a decisive proof that all conditions were present in pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : في بيان ضعف كل واحد من تلك الجوابات
أما الجواب الأول ، وهو قوله : « إن تعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت واجب ، والواجب غني عن العلة ، فنقول : هذا الكلام باطل .
ويدل عليه وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إن إرادة الله تعالى إذا اقتضت إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين ، كان حضور ذلك الوقت المعين ، أحد الأمور التي عند حضوره ، تتم المؤثرية والموجدية . فنقول : ذلك الوقت ، إن كان حاضراً في الأزل ، وجب حصول الأثر ، وإن لم يكن حاضراً ، كان أحد الأمور المعتبرة في كونه تعالى موجداً للعالم ، فإننا قد بينا بالبرهان القاطع : أن كل الأمور المعتبرة في مؤثرية الله تعالى في وجود العالم ، قد كان حاضراً في الأزل.
The second station: On explaining the weakness of each of those answers.
As for the first answer, which is his statement: 'Indeed, the attachment of the will of Allah Almighty to bringing the world into existence at that particular time is necessary (wajib), and the necessary is independent of a cause ('illa),' we say: This statement is false.
Proofs indicate it:
The first proof: If the will of Allah Almighty necessitates bringing the world into existence at that particular time, then the presence of that particular time is one of the things at whose presence effectiveness and causing-to-exist are completed. We say: That time, if it was present in pre-eternity (al-azal), then the occurrence of the effect would be necessary; and if it was not present, then it is one of the considered matters in His being an originator of the world, for we have already demonstrated by decisive proof that all the considered matters in the effectiveness of Allah Almighty in the existence of the world were present in pre-eternity.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage begins the second station of the discussion, which aims to show the weakness of each of the nine answers individually. The first answer claims that Allah's will being attached to creating the world at a specific time is necessary, and the necessary does not need a cause. The author declares this false and provides proofs. The first proof: If Allah's will necessitates creation at that specific time, then the presence of that specific time is one of the conditions that complete effectiveness. The author then presents a dilemma: if that time was present in pre-eternity (al-azal), then the effect (the world) should have occurred eternally (which contradicts its temporal origination). If it was not present, then it is a condition for Allah being the originator. But the author asserts that they have already proven by decisive proof that all conditions for Allah's effectiveness in the existence of the world were present in pre-eternity. Therefore, the time must have been present, leading to eternal creation — a contradiction. Hence the first answer fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Allah's will is free from conditions, so the specific time for creation does not affect His action.
Correction: The author shows that the specific time is a necessary condition for effectiveness. If it was not present in pre-eternity, it must have been added as a condition, but then all conditions were already present, implying eternal creation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first proof refutes the claim of necessity by showing that the specific time must have been either present in pre-eternity (leading to eternal creation) or absent (making it a condition that was always present, which also leads to eternal creation).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that a condition (time) is unavoidable for creation. Does this challenge your view of Allah's absolute independence? How comfortable are you with the idea that even Allah's actions are framed by certain conditions?
?
If the time of your own existence was determined in pre-eternity, what does that mean for your sense of purpose? Does it make you feel insignificant or part of a divine plan?
?
The dilemma forces a choice between eternal creation or a condition that was always present. Which horn seems more plausible to you, and why? Does your answer reveal anything about how you think about God and time?

Second Proof: A Dilemma on the Scope of Divine Will
The author presents a dilemma about the will's scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against the first answer, which uses a dilemma about whether Allah's will is equally suitable for creating the world at any time or only at a specific time.

Core Idea
If Allah's will is equally related to all times, its specification to one time needs a cause.

The author continues his refutation of the first answer by presenting a second proof. He sets up a dilemma: is Allah's will suitable for creating the world at all times, or only at the appointed time? If it is suitable for all times, then its actual determination to that specific time becomes a contingent fact that requires an explanation. This passage begins the argument but is cut off before its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author presents a binary choice.
The passage is cut off; the conclusion is missing.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author considers two cases: either the will is suitable for all times, or only for that specific time. Find these in the text.
The second possibility is introduced by 'aw' (or).
3
Analyze the logical consequence of the first possibility
If the will is suitable for all times, then its relation to each time is identical. Therefore, it's contingent that it chose this specific time. That requires a preponderator (murajjih). The argument is incomplete here.
Think about why this challenges the first answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second proof in explaining the corruption of this answer: We say: The will of Allah Almighty — either it is said that it is suitable for necessitating bringing the world into existence at all times, or it is not suitable except for necessitating bringing the world into existence at that particular time. If the first, then the relation of that will to necessitating bringing the world into existence at that time is like its relation to necessitating bringing the world into existence at all other times. And if the matter is so, then the will's necessitating bringing the world into existence at that time rather than all other times would be a contingent matter. Then if preponderance occurred without a preponderator...
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the first answer. The author sets up a dilemma: either Allah's will is suitable for necessitating creation at all times, or it is suitable only for that specific time. If it is suitable for all times, then the will's relation to the specific time is the same as its relation to any other time. Therefore, the will's actual determination to bring about the world at that specific time rather than others is a contingent matter (ja'iz). If that contingent preponderance occurred without a preponderator (murajjih), then this would be impossible. The argument is incomplete due to the cut-off text, but the intended conclusion is presumably that such a preponderance without a cause is invalid, thus the first answer fails.

Refuting the First Answer: The First Proof on Creation's Timing
Razi presents a dilemma refuting the claim that God's will necessitates creation at a specific time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Razi poses against the claim that God's will is necessarily attached to a specific time, proving that the world must be eternal.

Core Idea
If the time was present in eternity, the effect must be eternal.

Razi now moves to the second station of his discussion, where he systematically refutes each answer given by his opponents. Here he tackles the first answer, which asserts that God's will is necessarily attached to a particular moment. He presents a sharp dilemma that forces the opponent to either accept the eternity of the world or concede a contradiction in their reasoning. This lesson will walk you through that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice how Razi introduces the second station and then targets the first answer.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وجب حصول الأثر' — this is the key consequence.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The proof presents a conditional: if the specific time was present in eternity, the effect must occur; if not, then time is a considered matter lacking in eternity. Identify both branches.
The first horn leads to the world being eternal; the second horn implies God's effectivity was incomplete.
3
Understand why this refutes the first answer
The opponent claimed that God's will is necessarily attached to that time and needs no cause. The dilemma shows that either the world must be eternal (contradicting creation in time) or God's effectivity was not complete in eternity (contradicting divine perfection).
This refutation does not assume the eternity of the world; it is a reductio against the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمور المعتبرة فاتت، وحينئذ لا يكون المؤثر التام، حاصلا في الأزل. فهذا بحث شريف عالي. المقام الثاني: في بيان ضعف كل واحد من تلك الجوابات. أما الجواب الأول، وهو قوله: "إن تعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت واجب، والواجب غني عن العلة، فنقول: هذا الكلام باطل. ويدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: إن إرادة الله تعالى إذا اقتضت إحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، كان حضور ذلك الوقت المعين، أحد الأمور التي عند حضوره، تتم المؤثرية والموجدية. فنقول: ذلك الوقت، إن كان حاضراً في الأزل، وجب حصول الأثر، وإن لم يكن حاضراً، كان أحد الأمور المعتبرة في كونه تعالى موجداً للعالم، فإننا كنا بينا بالبرهان القاطع: أن كل الأمور المعتبرة في مؤثرية الله تعالى في وجود العالم، قد كان حاضراً في الأزل.
The considered matters have passed, and then the complete effect is not obtained in eternity. This is a noble, high discussion. The second station: on explaining the weakness of each of those answers. As for the first answer, which is his statement: 'The attachment of the will of God Most High to bringing about the world at that time is necessary, and the necessary is independent of a cause,' we say: This speech is false. And it is indicated by proofs: The first proof: If the will of God Most High required bringing about the world at that specific time, then the presence of that specific time is one of the matters at whose presence the power to effect and bring into existence are completed. We say: That time, if it was present in eternity, then the effect must occur; and if it was not present, then it is one of the considered matters in His being a bringer-into-existence of the world, for we have already shown by decisive proof that all the considered matters in the effectivity of God Most High in the existence of the world were present in eternity.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage begins by introducing the second station, which aims to show the weakness of each of the answers given earlier. Then it addresses the first answer, which claimed that God's will being attached to creating the world at a specific time is necessary, and the necessary needs no cause. The author declares this statement false and proceeds to provide proofs. The first proof is a dilemma: If the specific time was present in eternity, then the effect (the world) should have occurred in eternity. If it was not present, then the time is one of the considered matters for God's effectivity, and it was already proven that all such matters were present in eternity. Thus, in either case, the world should be eternal, contradicting the claim that it was created at a later time.

Key Terms
إرادة الله
المؤثر التام
الأزل
الموجدية
المؤثرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof assumes the world is eternal from the start.
Correction: The proof is a reductio ad absurdum: it shows that the opponent's own premises (God's will attached to a specific time, all considered matters present in eternity) lead to the conclusion that the world must be eternal, which contradicts the opponent's claim that it was created later.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what follows if the specific time was present in eternity?
A) The effect must occur in eternity.
B) The effect must occur later.
C) The effect is impossible.
D) The effect is contingent.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that if God's will is attached to a specific time, that time must either be eternal (making the world eternal) or absent (making God's effectivity incomplete), both of which are unacceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refutation of the Claim that Resolve Suffices for Creation
Author enumerates four arguments against the sufficiency of resolve for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intention (qaṣd) is necessary for creation and why resolve (ʿazm) alone cannot bring about an effect.

Core Idea
Resolve cannot transform into intention; creation requires genuine intention.

The author here refutes the idea that a mere resolve (ʿazm) to create is sufficient for bringing the world into existence. He lists several key arguments: creation requires intention, resolve and intention are distinct in essence, resolve cannot turn into intention, and intention must be newly produced. These arguments collectively show the error of those who claim that resolve alone is enough.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Get an overview of the author's series of claims. Read the Arabic once, then the translation.
2
Identify the four main points
Notice the structure: 'we will show that... and we show that... and we show that...'. List each argument in your own words.
3
Examine the distinction between resolve and intention
Understand why they are essentially different according to the author.
The essence (māhiyya) of each is different.
4
Consider the impossibility of transformation
Why can't resolve become intention? Think about the logical implication.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Understand why these points collectively invalidate the first answer.
The passage ends by stating that this is a collection of proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسنبين أيضا : أن الإيجاد والتكوين لا يمكن إلا بالقصد إلى الإيجاد، ونبين أن ماهية العزم مخالفة لماهية القصد، ونبين أن العزم على الفعل، يمتنع أن ينقلب بعينه قصداً إلى الفعل، وأنه لا بد من الاعتراف بأنه يبطل ذلك وأنه يحدث القصد. وعند هذا يظهر فساد قول من يقول : إن ذلك العزم يكفي في إحداث العالم في الوقت المعين. فهذه جملة الدلائل المذكورة على إبطال الجواب الأول.
And we will also show: that bringing into existence and creation are not possible except with intention to bring into existence, and we show that the essence of resolve is different from the essence of intention, and we show that resolve to do an act cannot turn into intention to do that very act, and that it is necessary to acknowledge that it invalidates that and that intention occurs. And at this point, the corruption of the statement of whoever says: 'That resolve suffices for bringing about the world at the specified time' becomes apparent. So this is a collection of the proofs mentioned to invalidate the first answer.

Key Terms

Correction: The author insists they are distinct in essence and cannot transform; a separate intention must be produced.

Checkpoint

The Benefit Does Not Necessitate the Act
Author argues that benefit does not necessitate timing of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a greater benefit in a particular time does not compel God to create at that time, and how the argument preserves divine free agency.

Core Idea
Greater benefit does not bind the Creator.

In this passage, the author takes on a subtle objection: if a certain time offers more benefit, why didn't God create the world at that exact moment? The argument rigorously shows that if benefit compelled creation, God would be a necessitated being, not a free agent. On the other hand, if benefit does not compel, then the actual timing requires an additional reason—otherwise the choice would be arbitrary. This logical move defends divine freedom while refuting the notion that mere benefit can dictate the divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: a hypothesis, a disjunction, and two branches leading to impossibility.
The argument is structured as a dilemma.
2
Identify the two branches
The author asks: does the extra benefit necessitate creation at that time or not? Identify each branch and its consequence.
First branch: necessitarianism. Second branch: need for extra preponderator or arbitrariness.
3
Understand the first branch's implication
If benefit necessitates, then God would be 'wajib bi-dhat' (necessitated by essence) rather than a free agent. Ask yourself why that is problematic for Islamic theology.
This branch is rejected because it undermines divine freedom.
4
Trace the second branch's dilemma
If benefit does not necessitate, then why was the world created at that time? Either an additional preponderator is needed (so benefit alone is insufficient) or the choice is without any preponderator (impossible). See how both outcomes are problematic.
This shows that benefit cannot be the sole reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : هب أن ذلك الوقت ، اختص بهذه الزيادة من المصلحة . إلا أنا نقول : اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك الزيادة من المصلحة ، هل يقتضي وجوب تخصيص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت ، أو لا يقتضي وجوب هذا التخصيص ؟ فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يكون إله العالم موجباً بالذات لا فاعلاً بالاختيار ، وإن كان الثاني فنقول : على هذا التقدير لا يمتنع مع اختصاص ذلك الوقت المعين، بهذا النوع من المرجح أن لا يحدث العالم في ذلك الوقت ، لأن كل ما كان ممكناً ، فإنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال ، فليفرض مع حصول هذا المرجح تارة ، حصول هذا الاختصاص ، وتارة لا حصول . فامتياز الحصول عن اللاحصول، إن توقف على انضمام مرجح إليه ، فحئنئذ لا يكون مجرد اختصاص ذلك الوقت بذلك القدر من المصلحة ، كافياً في حصول الرجحان . وإن لم يتوقف على انضمام مرجح زائد إليه ، فحينئذ يلزم رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن المتساوي على الآخر لا لمرجح . وهو محال.
The second proof: We say: Suppose that time was distinguished by this additional benefit. But we say: Does the distinction of that time by that additional benefit necessitate the specification of bringing about the world at that time, or does it not necessitate this specification? If the first, then the God of the world would be necessitated by essence, not a free agent by choice. And if the second, then we say: on this assumption, it is not impossible, despite the specification of that determined time by this type of preponderator, that the world not occur at that time, because every possible thing, its occurrence being supposed does not entail an impossibility. So let it be assumed, with the occurrence of this preponderator, sometimes the occurrence of this specification, and sometimes its non-occurrence. Then the distinction of occurrence from non-occurrence, if it depends on the addition of another preponderator, then the mere distinction of that time by that amount of benefit would not be sufficient for obtaining preponderance. And if it does not depend on the addition of an extra preponderator, then the preponderance of one side of the equal possible over the other without a preponderator would follow, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 66

Key Terms
المصلحة (al-maṣlaḥa)
المرجح (al-murajjiḥ)
الموجب بالذات (al-mūjib bi-dhāt)
الفاعل بالاختيار (al-fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a greater benefit automatically obligates God to create at that time.
Correction: The argument shows that if benefit necessitated, God would be a necessitated being, contradicting His free agency. If it does not necessitate, then benefit alone is insufficient to explain why He chose that time.

Checkpoint
What follows if the benefit necessitates the specification of creation?
A) God would be a free agent
B) God would be necessitated by essence
C) The world would not occur
D) The benefit is insufficient

Key Takeaway
The presence of greater benefit in a time does not compel God to create there; doing so would either make God necessitated or leave the choice arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Third Argument: Refuting Time as an Independent Cause
Author sets up a trilemma and refutes the first possibility with two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the beneficial time cannot be a cause by itself, and how that leads to two problematic consequences: essential difference of times and potential for arbitrary causation.

Core Idea
Time cannot be a cause; claiming it leads to absurdities.

We now examine the third argument against the proposal that the determined time is the cause of creation. The author sets up a trilemma: either the benefit is due to the time's essence, its concomitants, or neither. He then refutes the first option by showing it leads to two unacceptable conclusions. This passage lays out the first refutation, focusing on the logical consequences of attributing causality to time itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the trilemma
Read the opening statement where the author presents three possibilities for why a specific time has benefit. Recognize that the first option is 'due to the essence of that time.'
The trilemma sets up the logical structure: essence, concomitants, or neither.
2
Examine the first objection: essential difference and infinite regress
Trace the reasoning: if one time is a cause by essence and others are not, then times must be different essences, leading to an infinite regress of sequential events.
The argument uses the principle that difference in effects implies difference in causes.
3
Examine the second objection: potential for arbitrary causation
Consider the second objection: if time can cause by essence, then it could be a cause for any event, which is problematic because it would allow events to arise from time itself.
The author questions why one time would be limited to one effect if it has causal power by essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في إبطال هذا الكلام : أن نقول : اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك المصلحة الراجحة ، إما أن يكون لذات ذلك الوقت ، أو لشيء من لوازمه ، أو لا لشيء من لوازمه . والأول باطل من وجهين : الأول : إنه لما كان ذلك الوقت المعين موجباً لذلك الأثر الخاص، وسائر الأوقات لم تكن كذلك، لزم أن تكون هذه الأوقات ماهيات مختلفة وحقائق متباينة . لأن الاختلاف في اللوازم، يدل على الاختلاف في الملزومات، وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون الأوقات عبارة عن حوادث متعاقبة ، وأمور متلاصقة متلاحقة ، فيكون هذا قولاً بأن كل حادث مسبوق بحادث آخر لا إلى أول. الثاني : إنه لما كان للوقت المعين، صلاحية اقتضاء الأثر المعين لذاته، وهذا هو المصلحة المعينة، كان هذا حكماً بأن الوقت المعين صالح لأن يكون علة للأثر المعين، وإذا كان هذا جائز، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون مقتضى الوقت المعين، علة لحدوث الحوادث المعينة ؟ وإذا كان محتملاً،
The third proof in invalidating this statement: We say: The distinction of that time by that preponderant benefit is either due to the essence of that time, or due to something from its concomitants, or due to nothing from its concomitants. The first is false in two ways: The first: Since that determined time is a cause for that specific effect, and other times are not like that, it would follow that these times are different essences and distinct realities. Because difference in concomitants indicates difference in the subjects, and then it would follow that times are sequential events and contiguous successive matters, so this would be a statement that every event is preceded by another event without a first. The second: Since the determined time has the suitability to necessitate the determined effect by its essence, and this is the determined benefit, this is a judgment that the determined time is suitable to be a cause for the determined effect. And if this is permissible, then why is it not permissible that the requirement of the determined time is a cause for the occurrence of determined events? And if it is possible...
Source pages p. 67

Key Terms

اللوازم (al-lawāzim)
الماهيات (al-māhiyyāt)
الحوادث المتعاقبة (al-ḥawādith al-mutaʿāqiba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that time itself can have causal efficacy.
Correction: The author shows this leads to infinite regress and reification of accidents, so time cannot be a cause by essence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument claims that if time had causal power, it would lead to an infinite regress—no beginning. Have you ever tried to trace the causes of your own existence back in time? Did you feel that sinking feeling of an endless chain? This lesson forces you to confront that only Allah can be the First Cause. How does this strengthen your certainty in a Creator who initiated time itself?
?
The author refutes the idea that a specific time has intrinsic suitability for a specific effect. In your daily life, are you tempted to think that certain times (e.g., moments of worship) have inherent blessing, as if the time itself causes spiritual benefit? How can you redirect your heart to see that all blessing comes directly from Allah, not from the mere passage of time?

The Impossibility of Preponderance Without a Preponderator
Argument showing preponderance without a preponderator is impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why preponderance without a preponderator is logically impossible according to the kalam argument presented by Sanusi.

Core Idea
A possible cannot be preferred without a preponderator, or the world could fail to exist.

Sanusi presents a subtle dialectical argument to refute the idea that the benefit of a specific time can serve as a sufficient preponderator for creation. The argument shows that either another preponderator is needed or we end up with an impossible scenario. Let's walk through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author’s own compressed style come through before analysing.
The argument is a conditional proof — pay attention to the 'if' structure.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage refers to a second possibility (presumably from a previous division). Determine what is being assumed and what is being rejected.
The first possibility is not given, but the focus is on the second where the preponderator is the time's benefit.
3
Examine the conditional reasoning
The passage argues: if the specification of time does not require an additional preponderator, then preponderance would occur without a cause. If it does require one, then the benefit alone is insufficient.
This is the core logical move.
4
Understand the impossible conclusion
The conclusion 'that is impossible' (وهو محال) shows that the assumption leads to a contradiction. Realise why preponderance without a preponderator is impossible.
5
Reflect on the bigger picture
Consider how this argument supports the principle that every contingent event requires a cause. This is foundational in kalam.
This connects to the broader doctrine of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالإختيار، وإن كان الثاني فنقول: فعلى هذا التقدير لا يمتنع مع اختصاص ذلك الوقت المعين بهذا النوع من المرجح أن لا يحدث العالم في ذلك الوقت، لأن كل ما كان ممكناً، فإنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فليفرض مع حصول هذا المرجح تارة حصول هذا الاختصاص، وتارة لا حصول. فامتياز الحصول عن اللاحصول، إن توقف على انضمام مرجح إليه، فحينئذ لا يكون مجرد اختصاص ذلك الوقت بذلك القدر من المصلحة كافياً في حصول الرجحان. وإن لم يتوقف على انضمام مرجح زائد إليه، فحينئذ يلزم رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن المتساوي على الآخر لا لمرجح. وهو محال.
By choice, and if the second, we say: according to this estimation, it is not impossible, together with the specification of that specific time with this type of preponderator, that the world not occur in that time, because every possible thing, from the supposition of its occurrence an impossibility does not follow. So let it be supposed with the occurrence of this preponderator sometimes the occurrence of this specification, and sometimes not occurrence. So the distinguishing of occurrence from non-occurrence, if it depends on the joining of a preponderator to it, then the mere specification of that time with that measure of benefit is not sufficient for the occurrence of preponderance. And if it does not depend on the joining of an additional preponderator to it, then there follows preponderance of one of the two sides of the equal possible over the other without a preponderator. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument (a proof by contradiction) against the claim that the specific time's benefit can serve as a preponderator for the world's creation at that time. The argument proceeds by examining the second of two possibilities (the first presumably dealt with earlier). It states: if the preponderance of that specific time does not require an additional preponderator for the world to occur at that time, then it would be possible that the world does not occur at that time despite the preponderator. This is because for any possible, assuming its occurrence does not lead to an impossibility. Then we consider whether the specification (of time) occurs with the preponderator or not. If the distinction between occurrence and non-occurrence of the specification requires an additional preponderator, then the mere benefit of that time is not sufficient for preponderance. If it does not require an additional preponderator, then we would have preponderance of one side of an equal possible (i.e., a possible that has no inherent preference) over the other without any preponderating factor. That is impossible because it violates the principle that a possible cannot be distinguished from its equal without a preponderator. The conclusion is marked by "وهو محال" (and that is impossible). This refutes the opponent's position.

Key Terms
مرجح (murajjih, preponderator)
ممكن متساوي (possible equal in both sides)
اختصاص (specification)
مصلحة (benefit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The benefit of a specific time is itself sufficient to give preponderance for creation at that time.
Correction: The argument shows that either an additional preponderator is needed (making the benefit insufficient alone) or preponderance occurs without any cause, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would happen if the specification of a time's benefit does not require an additional preponderator?
A) It would be impossible for the world to exist at that time.
B) It would lead to preponderance of one side over the other without a preponderator.
C) It would prove the existence of a necessary being.
D) It would show that times are different in essence.

Key Takeaway
Preponderance of a possible without a preponderator is impossible, refuting the idea that a time's benefit alone can determine creation.

What will you do differently now?

The argument shows that without a preponderator, preponderance is impossible—yet you often act as if your own choices are self-sufficient. When you succeed, do you attribute it to your own wisdom or to the One who granted preponderance? Repent of any self-reliance and commit to thanking Him for every inclination toward good.

Refuting Benefit-Based Specification of Time
Refutation of benefit-based specification via three-part division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third argument refutes the claim that a specific time's benefit is the cause for creation, by showing that any possible relationship between time and benefit leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
The benefit cannot cause creation; it either makes time an essential cause or leads to infinite regress.

After the opponent argued that the benefit of a specific time is why the world was created at that time, the author provides a third refutation. He systematically divides the possible ways the benefit could be related to the time—by essence, by concomitant, or accidentally—and demonstrates each possibility fails. This lesson walks through that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three divisions
Read the opening lines where the author divides the possible ways the benefit is specified to the time: by essence, by a concomitant, or by neither. This sets up the structure.
These three categories exhaust all possibilities.
2
Examine the first division and its two refutations
Focus on the first division (benefit by essence). The author gives two refutations: first leads to infinite regress of accidents, second implies the time itself could cause the world, negating God.
The first refutation shows a regress; the second shows a denial of the Creator.
3
See how the same refutation applies to the second division
The second division (benefit by a concomitant) is refuted using the same arguments, because the concomitant ultimately traces back to the time as cause.
The author says 'everything we mentioned returns here.'
4
Understand why the third division fails
The third division (accidental specification) is refuted because it makes the benefit contingent, which raises the original objection of needing a preponderator, thus the problem remains unsolved.
This closes all possibilities, proving the opponent's answer is corrupt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في إبطال هذا الكلام : أن نقول : اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك المصلحة الراجحة ، إما أن يكون لذات ذلك الوقت ، أو لشيء من لوازمه ، ولا لشيء من لوازمه . والأول باطل من وجهين : الأول : إنه لما كان ذلك الوقت المعين موجباً لذلك الأثر الخاص ، سائر الأوقات لم تكن كذلك ، لزم أن تكون هذه الأوقات ماهيات مختلفة وحقائق متباينة . لأن الاختلاف في اللوازم ، يدل على الإختلاف في الملزومات ، وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون الأوقات عبارة عن حوادث متعاقبة ، وأمور متلاصقة متلاحقة ، فيكون هذا قولاً بأن كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر لا إلى أول . الثاني : إنه لما كان للوقت المعين، صلاحية اقتضاء الأثر المعين لذاته ، وهذا هو المصلحة المعينة ، كان هذا حكماً بأن الوقت المعين صالح لأن يكون علة للأثر المعين، وإذا كان هذا جائز ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون مقتضى الوقت المعين، علة لحدوث الحوادث المعينة ؟ وإذا كان محتملاً، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى لحدوث العالم في القوت المعين ، هو ذلك الوقت المعين وحينئذ يلزم نفي الصانع وهو باطل ؟ أما انقسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : المقتضى لحدوث تلك الخاصية المعينة لازم من لوازم ذلك الوقت ، فكل ما ذكرناه في إبطال القسم الأول فهو عائد ههنا ، لأن ههنا قد جعلنا ذلك الوقت علة لتلك الخاصية ، وجعلنا تلك الخاصية علة لتلك المصلحة . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك الخاصية لما لم يكن لذاته، ولا لشيء من لوازم ذاته ، فحينئذ يكون اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك المصلحة من الجائزات ، فكل ما ذكرناه في اختصاص ذلك الوقت بحدوث العالم فيه ، فإنه عائد في اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك المصلحة . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذا الجواب فاسد .
The third argument in refuting this statement: We say: The specification of that time with that preponderant benefit is either due to the essence of that time, or due to something from its concomitants, or not due to anything from its concomitants. The first is false in two ways: First: Since that specific time necessitates that specific effect, while other times are not like that, it follows that these times are different essences and distinct realities. Because difference in concomitants indicates difference in the things they are concomitant to. Then it follows that times are expressions of successive accidents and contiguous, consecutive things. So this would be a statement that every accident is preceded by another accident without a first. Second: Since the specific time has, in its essence, the suitability to necessitate the specific effect, and this is the specific benefit, this would be a judgment that the specific time is fit to be a cause for the specific effect. And if this is permissible, why is it not permissible that the necessitator of the specific time is a cause for the occurrence of specific accidents? And if it is possible, why is it not permissible that the necessitator of the occurrence of the world in the specific time is that specific time itself? Then it would follow the negation of the Maker, and that is false. As for the second division: It is said: The necessitator for the occurrence of that specific property is a concomitant of that time. Then everything we mentioned in refuting the first division returns here, because here we have made that time a cause for that property, and made that property a cause for that benefit. As for the third division: That the specification of that time with that property is neither due to its essence nor due to anything from the concomitants of its essence. Then the specification of that time with that benefit would be among contingent things. Then everything we mentioned regarding the specification of that time with the occurrence of the world in it returns regarding the specification of that time with that benefit. So it is established by what we have mentioned that this answer is corrupt.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (the third argument against the opponent's response). It refutes the idea that the specific time's preponderant benefit is the cause for the world's creation at that time. The argument divides the possible ways the benefit can be associated with the time: (1) by the essence of the time, (2) by a concomitant of the time, (3) by nothing essential or concomitant (i.e., accidentally). The first is refuted by two objections: First, if the time essentially necessitates the benefit, then other times must be different in essence, leading to an infinite regress of temporal accidents. Second, if the time is fit to cause a specific effect, why not cause the world itself, which would negate God. The second division (by a concomitant) is refuted by the same arguments because the concomitant is ultimately traced back to the time as cause. The third division (accidentally) is refuted by noting that if the specification is accidental, then it is a contingent matter, and the same problems that apply to the time of creation (namely, the need for a preponderator) apply to the specification of the benefit, leading back to the original objection. Thus, all possibilities fail, and the opponent's answer is corrupt.

Key Terms
ذات (essence)
لوازم (concomitants)
علة (cause)
أثر (effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that times are actually different essences.
Correction: The argument does not assert that times are different; it shows that if the benefit were by essence, that would follow, leading to an absurdity. The premise is thus false.

Checkpoint
How many ways does the argument divide the specification of the benefit to the time?
A) Two.
B) Three.
C) Four.
D) Five.

Key Takeaway
All possible ways the benefit could be specified to the time lead to unacceptable consequences, so the opponent's benefit-based answer is corrupt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you reflect on the timing of events in your life—a hardship, a delay, an unexpected blessing—do you secretly measure God’s wisdom by the benefit you perceive? Will you repent of that and submit to His timing without demanding a reason?

Think of a time you avoided a good deed because you saw no immediate benefit, or excused a sin because it brought some temporary gain. Does that not mirror the opponent’s error of assuming benefit is the cause? How can you, from now on, obey Allah solely because He commands, even when benefit seems absent? What concrete step will you take today to break that pattern?
?
The argument shows that if benefit determined God’s actions, it would either lead to infinite regress or negate the Creator. In your daily reliance on Allah, do you truly trust that His decrees are wise even when they seem pointless or harmful? Or do you internally demand that He justify Himself to you? Refutation of the Second and Third Divisions of the Specific Time Answer
Refutes second and third divisions of specific time answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second and third divisions of the 'specific time' answer fail, and how they reduce to previously refuted arguments.

Core Idea
The second and third divisions merely reintroduce the same fallacy from the first.

In this passage, the author completes his refutation of a proposed answer for why the world was created at a specific time. Having already refuted the first division, he now turns to the second and third, showing that they ultimately rely on the same flawed reasoning. By the end, he declares the entire answer invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Note the structure: a hypothetical objection, then two divisions, then a conclusion.
2
Identify the core argument per division
For the second division, note that it makes time a cause for a property, which was already refuted. For the third, it makes the benefit contingent, which again falls under the earlier refutation.
3
Understand the overall refutation
See how the author concludes that both divisions lead back to the same problems, thus the entire answer is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى لحدوث العالم في القوت المعين ، [ هو ذلك الوقت المعين ] وحينئذ يلزم نفي الصانع وهو باطل ؟
أما انقسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : المقتضى لحدوث تلك الخاصية المعينة لازم من لوازم ذلك الوقت ، فكل ما ذكرناه في إبطال القسم الأول فهو عائد ههنا ، لأن ههنا قد جعلنا ذلك الوقت علة لتلك الخاصية ، وجعلنا تلك الخاصية علة لتلك المصلحة

So why is it not permissible that the determinant for the occurrence of the world at the specific moment is that specific moment itself? And then the negation of the Creator would be entailed, which is false? As for the second division: it is that one says: the determinant for the occurrence of that specific property is a necessary concomitant of that time. So everything we mentioned in refuting the first division applies here, because here we have made that time a cause for that property, and we have made that property a cause for that benefit. As for the third division: [it is] that the specification of that time with that property, since it is not due to its essence nor to any concomitant of its essence, then the specification of that time with that benefit would be among contingent matters. So everything we mentioned in the specification of that time with the occurrence of the world in it applies to the specification of that time with that benefit. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that this answer is invalid.

Commentary
This passage refutes the second and third divisions of a proposed answer explaining why the world was created at a specific time. The author first poses a hypothetical objection: if the specific time itself were the determinant, that would imply atheism, so it is false. Then he examines the second division, which claims the determinant is a necessary concomitant of the time. The author argues that this makes the time a cause for the property, and the property a cause for the benefit, thereby reverting to the same line of reasoning already refuted in the first division. For the third division, where the specification of the time is not essential, the benefit becomes contingent, and again the earlier arguments apply. Thus the entire answer is invalidated.

Sixth Proof: Time Does Not Affect Benefits
Proof that creation timing doesn't affect benefits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the specific time of creation does not affect the benefits God can give to creatures, based on the definition of benefit as pleasure or removal of pain.

Core Idea
God's power to give benefit is not restricted by time.

The opponents argued that benefits are tied to the time of creation. In this brief proof, the author dismantles that claim by defining what benefit truly means. Once we see that benefit is simply pleasure or relief from pain, and that God can grant these at any moment, the whole argument collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the compact style and logical flow.
2
Identify the definition of benefit
The author defines 'benefit' (maslaha) in the first sentence. Find the terms for pleasure and pain. This definition is the key premise.
3
Understand the implication
Since God is all-powerful, He can bring about pleasure or remove pain at any time, regardless of when the world was created. Therefore, the specific time of creation is irrelevant to the benefits creatures receive.
This refutes the opponent's claim that benefits are tied to a particular time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن كل مصلحة يمكن عودها إلى واحد من المكلفين ، فتلك المصلحة لا معنى لها إلا إيصال نفع في الدنيا أو في الآخرة إليه ، ولا معنى للنفع إلا اللذة والسرور ، أو دفع الألم والغم ، وكل ذلك فإنه تعالى قادر على إيصاله إلى المكلف ، سواء خلق [العالم] في ذلك الوقت ، أو خلقه في وقت آخر. فيثبت : أنه لا تختلف أحوال الخلق في المصالح والمفاسد ، بسبب حدوث العالم في الوقت المعين، أو عدم حدوثه في ذلك الوقت . فقد ظهر بهذه البيانات : ضعف هذا الجواب . [ والله أعلم ]
The sixth proof: Every benefit that can return to one of the obligated persons, that benefit has no meaning except conveying a benefit in this world or the Hereafter to him, and benefit has no meaning except pleasure and delight, or repelling pain and grief. And all of that, He, exalted, is able to convey to the obligated person, whether He created the world in that time or created it at another time. So it is established that the conditions of creatures do not differ in benefits and harms due to the occurrence of the world at the specific time or its non-occurrence at that time. So it has become clear through these statements: the weakness of this answer. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (the sixth) against a previous answer by opponents. The author defines 'benefit' (maslaha) as either conveying pleasure or repelling pain. Since God is capable at all times to grant these benefits to the obligated (mukallaf), the specific time of the world's creation is irrelevant. Therefore, the opponents' claim that benefits are tied to a particular time is refuted. The proof concludes by stating the weakness of the answer they gave.

Correction: Benefits are defined as pleasure and relief from pain, and God can grant these at any time, so creation timing is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Creation's precise timing does not affect the benefits God bestows, because benefit is defined as pleasure or removal of pain, which God can grant at any time.

Refuting Pre-Eternity as a Barrier to Creation
Refutation of pre-eternity as a barrier to creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that pre-eternity (al-azal) is a barrier preventing God's creative act, and appreciate the implication for divine causality.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity is not a barrier; it is a temporal concept that does not impede God's power.

The fourth answer sought to explain why God did not create the universe in pre-eternity by claiming that pre-eternity itself is a barrier. But this simply pushes the question back. In this passage, the author presents two powerful arguments showing the weakness of this claim. Let's see how he exposes the logical flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation and identify the two aspects
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author introduces two arguments (wajh) against the fourth answer. Try to understand the gist of each.
The second argument is cut off in the source, but its direction is clear.
2
Analyze the first argument
The first argument points out that if pre-eternity were a barrier, then its removal would be a condition for creation. This implies that the necessary condition for God's effect-producing power was absent in pre-eternity, which the author claims has already been disproven.
Think about why this reasoning is self-defeating.
3
Consider the second argument (incomplete)
The author indicates he will prove that the dependence of the possible upon the cause occurs in pre-eternity, directly refuting the barrier notion. Even though the text is cut off, we can infer the counter-argument.
This aligns with the author's broader stance on eternal causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الرابع : وهو قولهم : إنما امتنع حصول الإيجاد في الأزل ، لأن الأزل مانع من الإيجاد . فنقول : هذا الجواب أيضاً ضعيف . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن على هذا التقدير يكون أحد الأمور المعتبرة في صحة الإيجاد هو زوال الأزل . فيرجع حاصل هذا الكلام إلى أن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ، ما كان حاصلا في الأزل . لكنا أبطلنا هذا الكلام بالبرهان اليقيني . الثاني : إنا سنقيم الدلائل القاهرة على أن إستناد [؟] الممكن إلى المؤثر أزلاً
As for the fourth answer: which is their saying: The occurrence of bringing into existence was prevented in pre-eternity only because pre-eternity is an obstacle to bringing into existence. So we say: This answer is also weak. And aspects indicate it: The first: On this estimation, one of the matters considered for the validity of bringing into existence is the removal of pre-eternity. So the gist of this speech reduces to the fact that everything necessary for the effect-producing power was not present in pre-eternity. But we have nullified this statement by certain proof. The second: We will soon establish compelling proofs that the dependence of the possible upon the effect-producing power is in pre-eternity...
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage refutes the fourth answer, which claims that pre-eternity (al-azal) itself is a barrier (mani') to creation, so God could not create in pre-eternity. The author presents two arguments (wajh). The first argument states that if this were true, then the removal of pre-eternity would be a necessary condition for creation, implying that the necessary conditions for God's causation were absent in pre-eternity—a claim the author says he has already disproven by certain proof. The second argument is cut off but indicates that the author will prove the dependence of the contingent (al-mumkin) upon the effective cause (al-mu'aththir) in pre-eternity, directly countering the claim that pre-eternity prevents creation. The passage highlights the author's commitment to the eternal causal power of God.

Correction: Pre-eternity is merely a temporal concept; it does not have causal power to obstruct God's will. The author shows that treating it as a barrier leads to logical absurdities.

Al-Razi's Refutation: Reductio ad Absurdum on Causality
Al-Razi refutes a previous answer by reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses reductio ad absurdum to refute the claim that the demand for a preponderant is invalid.

Core Idea
A consistent demand for a cause must apply to all possibilities, not just one.

Al-Razi now turns to a powerful logical weapon: reductio ad absurdum. He shows that if the opponent's argument were correct, it would force them to accept that accidents happen without a cause — an absurdity that undermines their entire position. Let's trace his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the logical connectors: 'if...then', 'because', 'so'.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
Identify what al-Razi claims must follow if the opponent's statement were true: that accidents occur without an effecter and possibles are preponderated without a preponderant.
This is the heart of the reductio — showing an unacceptable implication.
3
Trace the reductio chain
Follow how al-Razi shows that the opponent's own question ('why this time?') applies to all possible times, making it a false question. Therefore, the demand for a preponderant is invalid, and by analogy the opponent's argument fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بيان ضعف هذا الجواب : أن نقول : إن كان الكلام الذي ذكرتم صحيحاً ، لزمكم أن تحكموا بأن الحادث يحدث لا لمؤثر ، وأن يترجح الممكن لا لمرجح ، وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا : ليس حدوث هذا الحادث في هذا الوقت، أولى من حدوثه في سائر الأوقات ، فوجب أن يكون اختصاص هذا الحادث بهذا الوقت المرجح . فيقال : ولو أنا قدرنا : أن هذا الحادث حدث في وقت آخر ، لا في هذا الوقت، لكان هذا السؤال عائداً فيثبت : أن هذا السؤال عائد على جميع التقديرات ، وما كان كذلك كان سؤالاً باطلاً ، فوجب أن يكون طلب هذا المرجح باطلاً ، وأن يحكم بأنه يجوز أن يختص حدوث هذا الحادث المعين بهذا الوقت لا المرجح ، وكما أن هذا الكلام باطل ، فكذلك الكلام الذي ذكرتموه يجب أن يكون باطلا .
The second aspect in clarifying the weakness of this answer: that we say: If the statement you mentioned were correct, you would be obligated to judge that the accident occurs without an effecter, and that the possible is preponderated without a preponderant. That is because when we say: the occurrence of this accident at this time is not more appropriate than its occurrence at other times, then it becomes necessary that the specification of this accident to this time is due to the preponderant. So it is said: And if we suppose that this accident occurred at another time, not at this time, this question would return and it would be established that this question returns upon all estimations, and whatever is like that is a false question, so it becomes necessary that the demand for this preponderant is false, and that it is judged that it is permissible for the occurrence of this specific accident to be specified to this time without a preponderant. And just as this statement is false, so likewise the statement you mentioned must be false.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (reductio ad absurdum) of a previous answer. Al-Razi argues that if the opponent's claim were true, it would lead to absurd consequences: accidents occurring without a cause and possibles being determined without a preponderant. He then shows that the opponent's own question (why this time rather than another) applies to all possible times equally, making it a false question. Therefore, the demand for a preponderant in that specific case is invalid, and by analogy, the opponent's original argument is also invalid.

Key Terms
حادث
مؤثر
مرجح
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a question can be asked in every possible scenario, it must be a valid and meaningful question.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that if a question applies to all possibilities indiscriminately, it becomes meaningless and serves as a false question, revealing a flaw in the reasoning that generated it.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi claim necessarily follows if the opponent's statement were correct?
A) That accidents occur without a cause and possibles are preponderated without a preponderant
B) That the world is eternal
C) That God is temporal
D) That time is infinite

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's reductio shows that demanding a preponderant for each specific time leads to an absurd infinite regress, making the opponent's argument self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Razi's argument shows that questioning every possible time leads to an infinite and false demand. How does this affect your trust in Allah's perfect timing? Will you commit to accepting His decree without demanding a specific cause?

Consider a blessing you attribute to a particular action of yours. If you applied the same logic of demanding a preponderant to every other possible time you could have performed that action, would you be forced to admit that the attribution is arbitrary? How does that humble you and remind you that all blessings come from Allah alone? What is one blessing you will actively stop taking credit for?
?
Al-Razi's refutation exposes inconsistency in demanding a cause for one thing but not for another. Where in your own life do you hold inconsistent standards—accepting some events as 'just because' while insisting on reasons for others? Which area will you work on to submit fully to Allah's wisdom?

Universality Proves the Demand for a Preponderant
Al-Razi argues that the recurrence of the demand proves its validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi considers the universality of the demand for a preponderant as evidence for its validity, not against it.

Core Idea
A question that applies to all cases cannot be dismissed—it proves the rule.

Al-Razi addresses a key objection: if the demand for a preponderant arises in every possible scenario, doesn't that make it meaningless? In this passage, he turns the objection on its head. He argues that the very universality of the demand, free from any counterexample, is the strongest proof of its soundness. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that an opponent claimed the demand for a preponderant is invalid because it recurs endlessly. Al-Razi now refutes that.
This passage is the third aspect of his refutation.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Al-Razi uses the opponent's concession (that the demand returns on all estimations) to his advantage.
3
Identify the logical structure
The argument is: (1) The proposition is universal. (2) A universal proposition with no counterexample is sound. (3) Therefore, recurrence proves soundness.
Look for the conditional: 'if we suppose... without a preponderant... that would be a contradiction'.
4
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: Why does a lack of counterexample strengthen a claim? How does this apply to other theological arguments?
This is a key method in kalām: universality without exception indicates truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في دفع هذا الجواب : أن نقول : لما سلمتم أن هذا الطلب عائد على كل التقديرات ، كان هذا من أدل الدلائل على صحة هذا الطلب ، وذلك لأنا إذا ادعينا أن اختصاص الحادث بالوقت المعين ، جواز حصوله قبل ذلك الوقت وبعده ، لا بد وأن يكون المرجح . فلو قدرنا أنه حصل اختصاص حادث بوقت معين لا المرجح ، كان ذلك نقضاً على تلك القضية ، ومبطلاً لها . أما لما كانت هذه القضية مطردة سليمة عن النقض ، كان ذلك من أدل الدلائل على صحتها . فيثبت : أن عود هذا الطلب على كل التقديرات مما يوجب صحة هذه القضية ، ولا يوجب فسادها . والله أعلم
The third aspect in repelling this answer: that we say: Since you have conceded that this demand returns upon all estimations, this is among the strongest evidences for the soundness of this demand. That is because if we claim that the specification of the accident to a particular time, given the possibility of its occurrence before that time and after it, must be due to a preponderant. So if we suppose that the specification of an accident to a particular time occurred without a preponderant, that would be a contradiction to that proposition and an invalidation of it. But since this proposition is universally applicable and free from contradiction, that is among the strongest evidences for its soundness. So it is established that the return of this demand upon all estimations necessitates the soundness of this proposition and does not necessitate its corruption. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that the demand for a preponderant is invalid because it recurs in all cases. Al-Razi argues that the very recurrence is evidence of the soundness of the demand. He explains that the proposition (every specification of an accident to a time requires a preponderant) is universal and has no counterexample; therefore it is correct. The fact that the question applies to all estimations does not weaken it but strengthens it.

Key Terms
طلب
تقدير
مرجح
قضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a question applies to all cases, it is too broad to be meaningful or valid.
Correction: Al-Razi demonstrates that a universal proposition with no counterexample is actually the strongest kind of truth. The recurrence across all estimations proves the proposition's validity, not its weakness.

Checkpoint

A) The demand is false
B) The demand is sound
C) The demand is irrelevant
D) The demand requires no answer

Key Takeaway
The universality of the demand for a preponderant across all possible cases confirms, not weakens, its validity in kalām reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
