Where in your own life have you dismissed a principle simply because it seemed too broad or repetitive, rather than allowing its universality to confirm its validity? Will you now reconsider that stance?
?
Al-Razi insists that if even one counterexample existed, the proposition would be invalid. Think of a belief you hold dear. Is there a single exception you have allowed to creep in that actually undermines its truth? Are you willing to examine that exception honestly and either abandon the belief or remove the exception?
?
The recurrence of the demand across all estimations is not a flaw but a proof. When you find yourself asking the same question in every situation—like 'why this rather than that?'—do you treat that as a weakness in your reasoning or as a sign that you are touching something fundamental? What would change in your life if you embraced that recurring question as evidence of its soundness?

The Necessity of Priority Before the First Accident
Al-Razi argues that necessary knowledge implies priority before the first accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that the question 'why did the world occur now?' remains valid even if no prior time exists, based on the necessary priority of non-existence over existence.

Core Idea
Our innate knowledge forces us to admit priority even before time begins.

Al-Razi confronts a sophisticated objection: if there is no time before the first moment, how can we ask 'why now?' He dismantles this by appealing to a universal, self-evident truth—that non-existence precedes existence for every contingent thing. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the literal translation. Notice how al-Razi first presents the opponent's view, then his own objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two sides
Separate the opponent's claim (the seventh answer) from al-Razi's refutation. The opponent says the question is invalid because no prior time exists; al-Razi says it's weak because we know non-existence precedes existence.
3
Understand the key logical move
Al-Razi argues that our innate nature (فطرة) testifies that non-existence comes before existence for every accident. This implies a 'priority' that is not necessarily temporal but rational, undermining the opponent's assumption.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that 'before' always means temporal priority. Al-Razi shows it can be a conceptual priority inherent in our knowledge.
This resolves the apparent paradox.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب السابع : وهو قولهم : إن قول القائل : لم حدث العالم في هذا الوقت ، ولم يحدث قبله ؟ إنما يصح لو حصل قبل هذا الوقت وقت آخر ، وعندنا : أن هذا الوقت هو أول الأوقات ، فلم يحصل قبله وقت آخر . فسقط هذا السؤال . فنقول : هذا الجواب أيضاً ضعيف . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن عدم كل حادث سابق على وجوده ، فلولا حصول القبلية قبل ما فرضتموه أول الحوادث وإلا لما شهدت الفطرة بأن عدم كل شيء قبل وجوده
As for the seventh answer: it is their statement: The saying of the one who says: 'Why did the world occur at this time and not occur before it?' is only valid if there is another time before this time. But with us, this time is the first of times, so there is no other time before it. So this question falls. We say: This answer is also weak. Evidences for it are aspects: The first: We know by necessity that the non-existence of every accident precedes its existence. So if priority did not occur before what you assumed to be the first of accidents, then innate nature would not testify that the non-existence of everything is before its existence.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the seventh answer, which claims that the question 'why now?' is invalid because there is no time before the first accident. Al-Razi refutes this by appealing to necessary knowledge: we know intuitively that non-existence precedes existence for every accident. This implies a 'beforeness' (priority) even before the first accident, otherwise our innate knowledge would be wrong. Thus the question is valid.

Misconception: That the concept of 'before' always requires a prior temporal moment or time.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that 'before' can refer to a rational priority (قبلية) inherent in our knowledge, not necessarily a temporal one. Our innate nature testifies that non-existence precedes existence, and this priority holds even if there is no time before the first moment.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi establishes that the priority of non-existence over existence is a necessary truth known by innate nature, and this priority does not depend on the existence of prior time.

Will you let that question pierce you today, or will you bury it in busyness?

The Logical Contradiction in 'The Capable Gives Preponderance'
Logical analysis of two interpretations of 'al-qadir yurajjih'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'the capable gives preponderance' must imply an additional concept beyond mere capability, and why denying that leads to a contradiction or absurdity.

Core Idea
If 'gives preponderance' adds nothing, the action happens by chance, which is intellectually impossible.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah examines two interpretations of the common theological phrase 'al-qadir yurajjih' (the capable gives preponderance). The author shows that both interpretations lead to problems: the first contradicts itself, and the second implies that actions occur without any cause, which is rejected by basic reason. This analysis is fundamental to understanding the necessity of a preponderator in causal agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two possibilities
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice that the author presents two possible interpretations of 'al-qadir yurajjih'.
The first interpretation says 'yurajjih' adds a new concept; the second says it does not.
2
Understand the first possibility's contradiction
The author shows that if 'yurajjih' adds a concept, then saying 'no additional preponderator' is a combination of affirmation and negation—a direct contradiction.
This is a logical argument: you cannot both affirm and deny the same thing.
3
Understand the second possibility's absurdity
If 'yurajjih' adds nothing, then the agent's relation to action and inaction is equal, yet the action occurs without specification. This implies the action happens by chance, which is repudiated by the primacy of intellects.
The author says this is an explicit statement that the action occurred without an efficient cause.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both possibilities fail: the first is self-contradictory, the second is absurd. Therefore, the phrase 'al-qadir yurajjih' necessarily implies an additional concept of preponderance.
This establishes the need for a preponderator in causal agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان الأمر كذلك ، فقولنا بعد هذا الكلام : إنه حصل ذلك الرجحان لا المرجح زائد : كلام متناقض لما سبق . لأنا على هذا التقدير ، سلمنا أن المفهوم من قولنا : يرجح ، زائد على المفهوم من كونه قادراً . فقولنا : إنه لا لمرجح زائد : يكون جمعاً بين النفي والإثبات، وأنه محال . فقولنا : القادر يرجح : يفيد إثباتاً زائداً على كونه قادراً . وقولنا : لا لمرجح : يفيد نفيه . فكان هذا جمعاً بين النقيضين . وأنه فاسد . وأما الاحتمال الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه ليس لقولنا : يرجح : مفهوم زائد على كونه قادراً . فحينئذ يرجع حاصل هذا الكلام : إلى أن القادر الذي نسبته إلى الفعل وإلى الترك على السوية ، والمريد الذي كانت نسبة إرادته إلى الفعل وإلى الترك على السوية : فإنه حال بقائه على هذا الاستواء يدخل ذلك الفعل في الوجود، من غير أن يخصصه ذلك القادر بالإيقاع والترجيح . ومعلوم أن ذلك مدفوع في بدائه العقول ، بل هذا تصريح بأن هذا الفعل وقع لا بإيقاع الفاعل ، بل على سبيل الاتفاق من غير تأثير مؤثر ، وإيجاد موجد . ومعلوم أنه باطل . وأما الأمثلة التي ذكروها ، فهي محض التلبيس . وذلك لأن المخير بين شرب قدحين من الماء فإنه ما لم يمد يده إلى أخذهما وما لم يحض أحدهما بالقصد إلى أخذه ، فإنه لا يترجح أخذ ذلك القدح على أخذ القدح الثاني . فرجحان ذلك القدح على غيره ، إنما كان لأن ذلك الفاعل خصه بتوجيه القصد إليه ، وبعد اليد إليه . فكيف يقال : إنه ترجيح أحد المقدورين على الآخر لا لمرجح ؟ بل المثال المطابق لقولهم : أن يقال : إن ذلك الإنسان بقي متردداً بين أخذ أحد هذين القدحين ، ولم يخص واحداً منهما بتوجيه القصد إليه ، ولا بمد اليد إليه . ثم إن أحد ذلك القدحين، ارتفع بنفسه وانصب في حلقه . ومعلوم أن امتناع ذلك : معلوم في بديهة العقل . فيثبت : أن المثال الذي ذكروه محض التلبيس
If the matter is like that, then our statement after this speech: 'that preponderance occurred without an additional preponderator' is contradictory to what preceded. Because on this estimation, we concede that the concept from our statement 'he gives preponderance' is additional to the concept of his being capable. So our statement: 'there is no additional preponderator' would be a combination of negation and affirmation, and that is impossible. So our statement: 'the capable gives preponderance' indicates an additional affirmation beyond his being capable. And our statement: 'there is no preponderator' indicates its negation. So this is a combination of two contradictories, and it is invalid. As for the second possibility: that it is said: our statement 'he gives preponderance' has no additional concept beyond his being capable. Then the gist of this speech returns to: that the capable whose relation to action and to abandonment is equal, and the willing whose will's relation to action and to abandonment is equal, that he, while remaining in this equality, that action comes into existence, without that capable specifying it by performance and preponderance. And it is known that this is repudiated by the primacy of intellects. Rather, this is an explicit statement that this action occurred not by the agent's performance, but by chance, without the influence of an influencer and the bringing into existence of a bringer into existence. And it is known that it is false. As for the examples they mentioned, they are pure obfuscation. That is because the one given the choice between drinking two cups of water, if he does not extend his hand to take them and does not attend one of them with the intention of taking it, then the taking of that cup does not become preponderant over the taking of the second cup. So the preponderance of that cup over others was only because that agent specified it by directing intention to it and extending the hand to it. So how can it be said: it is a preponderance of one of the two possible acts over the other without a preponderator? Rather, the example consistent with their statement is to say: that person remained hesitant between taking one of these two cups, and did not specify one of them by directing intention to it nor by extending the hand to it. Then one of those cups rose by itself and poured into his throat. And it is known that the impossibility of that is known by the primacy of the intellect. So it is established that the example they mentioned is pure obfuscation.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis and refutation of two interpretations of the phrase 'the capable gives preponderance' (al-qadir yurajjih) and a defense of the necessity of a preponderator (murajjih) against the charge of infinite regress. The author begins by considering the first interpretation: if 'yurajjih' implies an additional concept beyond mere capability, then saying 'there is no additional preponderator' is a direct contradiction (jamm' bayn al-nafy wa al-ithbat), which is impossible. He then considers the second interpretation: if 'yurajjih' adds no new concept, then the claim reduces to the idea that a capable agent with equal relation to both action and inaction, and whose will is equally related, can have an action come into existence without specification. The author declares this repudiated by the primacy of intellects (bada'i' al-'uqul), as it implies the action occurs by chance without any effective cause. He then addresses the example of two cups of water used by opponents. He argues that in reality, the preponderance of one cup over the other occurs only because the agent directs intention and hand to it. The correct analogy consistent with the opponent's claim would be if the person remained completely neutral and one cup rose by itself—something obviously impossible by intellectual necessity. Thus the example is pure obfuscation (mahd al-talbis).

Key Terms
الترجيح (al-tarjih)
المرجح (al-murajjih)
القادر (al-qadir)
بدائه العقول (bada'i' al-'uqul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'the capable gives preponderance' means the agent can cause an action without any additional preference or specification.
Correction: The passage shows that this view leads to the absurd conclusion that the action occurs by chance, without any efficient cause, which is repudiated by the primacy of intellects.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would be the consequence if 'yurajjih' has no additional meaning beyond capability?
A) The agent would be considered fully capable but not acting.
B) The action would occur without any specification or causation, which is impossible.
C) The agent would be identical to a necessitated cause.
D) The concept of capability would be negated.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'al-qadir yurajjih' necessarily implies an additional concept of preponderance beyond capability; otherwise, agency would be reduced to chance.

?

Refutation of the Example of the Two Cups
Refutation of the two-cups example used to deny a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the example of the two cups does not support the claim of preponderance without a preponderator, and what the correct example would be.

Core Idea
The example of the two cups actually requires a preponderator—the agent's intention and hand movement.

Critics of the necessity of a preponderator sometimes cite the example of a person choosing between two cups of water, claiming that the choice occurs without an additional cause. In this passage, the author exposes this as pure obfuscation. He shows that in the real scenario, the agent's intention and hand are the preponderator, and the only scenario consistent with their claim would be physically impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the example given by the opponents
Read the passage that begins 'As for the examples they mentioned...' and identify the example of the two cups.
The opponents claim this shows preponderance without a preponderator.
2
Understand why the example is misleading
The author points out that in the actual scenario, the agent extends his hand and intends to take one cup. This intention and hand movement are the preponderator.
So the example actually includes a preponderator, contrary to their claim.
3
Consider the author's corrected example
The author constructs a scenario consistent with their claim: the person remains hesitant, does not intend or move toward either cup, yet one cup rises by itself and pours into his throat.
This is the logical conclusion of their view.
4
Realize the impossibility
The corrected scenario is obviously impossible by intellectual necessity. Thus, the opponents' example is pure obfuscation.
This reinforces the necessity of a preponderator.

First Objection and Answer: Why This Intention Rather Than Another?
Objection about why a specific intention occurred and the answer that intentions terminate with necessary intention from above.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the preponderance theory and how the author answers it by terminating intentions in a necessary intention from the higher world.

Core Idea
Intentions cannot regress infinitely; they end at a necessary intention from above.

Having established that an action requires a preponderator, the objector now asks: what preponderated the intention itself? This threatens an infinite regress. In this passage, the author provides a decisive answer that anchors the chain of intentions in a necessary intention from the higher world, according to specific causes and dispositions unknown to creatures.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the objection is framed and the structure of the answer.
The objection assumes the agent's action is already determined; the question is about the intention itself.
2
Identify the objection's core
The objector asks: why did the agent direct his intention to one cup rather than the other? This challenges the preponderance theory by asking what preponderates the intention.
This is a classic regress argument.
3
Understand the answer's logic
The answer denies that each intention must be caused by another intention ad infinitum. Rather, intentions terminate at a necessary intention from the higher world.
This necessary intention comes from above, not from the agent's free choice.
4
Note the key terms and concepts
Focus on terms like 'infinite regress' (التسلسل), 'necessary intention' (إرادة ضرورية), and 'higher world' (العالم الأعلى). Understand their roles in the argument.
5
Reflect on theological implications
Consider how this answer fits into Ash'ari occasionalism and the debate on free will. Does it deny free will or confine it within a determined chain?
The common misunderstanding is that it denies free will entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي ههنا لهم سؤالان : السؤال الأول : أن يقال : هب أنه مد اليد إلى أحد القدحين ، ووجه القصد إليه . فلم حصل هذا القصد دون القصد الثاني ؟ وجوابه : أن يقال : الإرادات والقصود ، لا يجب استناد كل واحد منها إلى آخر ، على سبيل التسلسل ، بل تنتهي تلك الإرادات والقصود إلى إرادة ضرورية ، قصد ضروري، يحصل فيه من العالم الأعلى ، بحسب أسباب معينة ، واستعدادات مخصوصة . لا اطلاع للخلق على كيفياتها .
There remains for them two questions: The first question: that it is said: Suppose he extended his hand to one of the two cups and directed his intention to it. So why did this intention occur rather than the second intention? Its answer: that it is said: The wills and intentions do not necessarily have each one of them trace back to another in the manner of infinite regress. Rather, those wills and intentions terminate at a necessary will, a necessary intention, that occurs from the higher world, according to specific causes and particular dispositions. The creatures have no knowledge of their modalities.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection and its answer. The objector concedes the preponderator but asks: why did the agent direct his intention to one cup rather than the other—what preponderates the intention itself? This threatens infinite regress. The answer is that wills and intentions do not require an infinite chain of preponderators. They terminate at a necessary intention that comes from the higher world (al-'alam al-a'la), i.e., from God or the angelic realm, according to specific causes (asbab mu'ayyana) and particular dispositions (isti'dadat makhsusa) whose modalities creatures cannot know. This avoids regress by positing an ultimate necessary intention that is not freely chosen by the created agent but is determined by causes and dispositions from above.

Key Terms
الإرادات (al-iradat)
القصور (al-qusud)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
إرادة ضرورية (irada daruriyya)
العالم الأعلى (al-'alam al-a'la)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This answer denies free will entirely by making all intentions predetermined from above without any role for the creature.
Correction: The answer confines free will within a chain that terminates at a necessary intention from the higher world, consistent with Ash'ari occasionalism. Creatures have dispositional causes but the ultimate determinant is from the higher world, whose modalities are unknown.

Checkpoint

B) In a necessary intention from the higher world.
C) In the agent's own free choice without further cause.
D) In the preponderance of the action itself.

Key Takeaway
The first objection is resolved by asserting that intentions do not regress infinitely but terminate at a necessary intention from the higher world, determined by unknown causes and dispositions.

?

Second Objection and Answer: Free Agent vs Necessitated Cause
Objection and answer distinguishing free agent from necessitated cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key distinctions between a free agent and a necessitated cause that defend the Ash'ari view on causality.

Core Idea
The free agent acts with consciousness and internal motive; the necessitated cause acts blindly.

This lesson addresses a powerful objection: if every action requires a preponderator, how is a free agent different from a necessary cause like fire? The answer lies in two crucial distinctions—consciousness and the role of a strong motive. These distinctions protect the Ash'ari understanding of a free, responsible agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Pay close attention to the objector's argument: if a preponderator is necessary, then the capable agent becomes like a necessitated cause, yet we intuitively know they are different.
2
Identify the two distinctions in the answer
The answer gives two differences. Find the first distinction regarding consciousness (shu'ur) and the second distinction regarding the role of strong motive and decisive will.
The second distinction is cut off in the passage, but the key idea is present.
3
Reflect on why consciousness matters
Consider why having consciousness of one's action makes an agent truly capable and responsible, unlike a blind natural cause.
4
Understand how strong motive relates to free choice
The second distinction shows that when power and will are equal, no action occurs, but a strong motive and decisive will lead to action. This shows that the agent is not forced externally but internally motivated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : أن يقال : إن كان الأمر على ما ذكرتم من أن القادر ما لم ينضم إليه المرجح ، فإنه يمتنع أن يصدر عنه الأثر . فحينئذ لا يبقى بين القادر وبين الموجب فرق لكن الفرق معلوم بالبديهة ، فكان ما ذكرتموه باطلاً . والجواب : أنا ذكرنا في باب الدواعي والصوارف : أن الفرق بين الموجب ، وبين المختار من وجهين : الأول : أن الموجب لا شعور له بما يصدر عنه البتة ، والقادر المختار هو الذي يحصل له الشعور بالآثار الصادرة عنه . والثاني : أن الواحد منا حال كونه متساوي القدرة والإرادة بالنسبة إلى وجود الفعل وعدمه ، فإنه يمتنع أن يصدر عنه الفعل ، والعلم بذلك ضروري ، أما إذا حصلت الداعية القوية ، والإرادة الجازمة ، وانضمت إلى
The second question: that it is said: If the matter is as you mentioned, that the capable, unless a preponderator is joined to him, from him cannot issue the effect. Then no difference remains between the capable and the necessitated cause. But the difference is known by intuition, so what you mentioned is false. The answer: We mentioned in the chapter on motives and deterrents that the difference between the necessitated and the free agent is from two aspects: The first: that the necessitated has absolutely no consciousness of what issues from him, while the capable free agent is the one who obtains consciousness of the effects issuing from him. And the second: that one of us, while being equal in power and will with respect to the existence of the action and its non-existence, it is impossible for the action to issue from him, and knowledge of that is necessary. But when the strong motive and the decisive will occur, and they are joined to ...
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and the beginning of its answer. The objector argues that if the Ash'ari position requires a preponderator (murajjih) for any action to occur, then the capable agent (al-qadir) becomes indistinguishable from a necessitated cause (al-mujib) like fire that burns necessarily. Yet the difference is intuitively known. The answer recalls an earlier discussion in the chapter on motives and deterrents (bab al-dawa'i wa al-sawarif). The first distinction is epistemic: the necessitated cause has no consciousness (shu'ur) of its effect, while the free agent has consciousness. The second distinction is that when a person's power and will are equally related to action and its omission, action cannot issue; but when a strong motive (da'iya qawiyya) and decisive will (irada jazima) occur, action follows. This shows that the free agent's action is not necessitated in the same way as a natural cause, because it requires conscious motive and will. The text cuts off before completing the second distinction, but the point is that the presence of a strong motive does not make the agent a necessitated cause, since the agent has consciousness and the motive is internal.

Key Terms
الموجب (al-mujib)
المختار (al-mukhtar)
الشعور (al-shu'ur)
الداعية (al-da'iya)
الإرادة الجازمة (al-irada al-jazima)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position that a preponderator is necessary for action eliminates any difference between a free agent and a natural cause, making human freedom illusory.
Correction: The Ash'ari answer distinguishes the free agent from a natural cause by two factors: the agent has consciousness of his action, and the action follows from an internal strong motive and decisive will, not from external compulsion.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, what is the first difference between a necessitated cause (al-mujib) and a free agent (al-mukhtar)?
A) The free agent has power, the necessitated does not.
B) The free agent has consciousness of his action, while the necessitated has none.
C) The free agent acts without any motive.
D) The necessitated agent acts by will, the free agent by nature.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari answer preserves the distinction between a free agent and a necessitated cause by emphasizing that the free agent acts with consciousness and an internal decisive will, not under external compulsion.

Refuting the Objection on Polar Points and the Necessity of a Preponderator
Author refutes first objection and begins deeper argument on temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection about celestial poles by showing that the specific motion necessitates the poles, and then begins a deeper argument that the specification of the world's origin to a particular time requires a preponderator.

Core Idea
Motion determines poles, but the motion itself needs a preponderator.

In this passage, the author tackles an objection about the arbitrary selection of polar points on the celestial sphere. The objectors argue that since all points are identical, choosing two as poles is arbitrary. The author responds by linking the poles to the specific manner of motion. However, the objectors then ask why that particular motion occurred. This leads the author into a proof for the world's temporal origination, arguing that all times are equally possible and thus specification requires a determining cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the objection and response.
Notice how the author first answers the initial objection and then anticipates a second objection.
2
Identify the two objections
Distinguish the first objection (about poles) from the second (about the motion itself). See how the author shifts the discussion.
The first objection is answered by the necessity of motion; the second asks why the motion occurred.
3
Understand the proof for temporality
Focus on the argument that the world's origin at a particular time is possible before, after, and at that moment equally, so specification requires a preponderator.
This proof is the first station of the answer to the second objection.

Source Text

ولنرجع إلى التفصيل فنقول:
أما النقض الأول: وهو قولكم: النقط المعترضة في سطح الفلك متساوية في تمام الماهية، فتعين نقطتين في جملة تلك النقط القطبية، دون سائر النقط، يكون رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح، فنقول: الجواب عنه: إن الحركة ومتى وقعت على الوجه المخصوص المعين، وجب تعين هاتين النقطتين للقطبية، لأنه محال عقلاً: أن تقع الحركة على هذا الوجه الخاص، إلا وتتعين هاتان النقطتان للقطبية، فإن قالوا: هب أن الأمر كذلك، إلا أنا نقول: فلم وقعت حركة الفلك على هذا الوجه الخاص المعين، دون سائر الوجوه المحتملة؟ فنقول: هذا هو النقض الثاني، ولنا في الجواب عنه مقامات:
المقام الأول: إنا قد دللنا على حدوث العالم في الوقت الذي حدث فيه، وحدوثه أيضاً قبل ذلك ممكناً. إذ لو كان حدوثه قبل ذلك ممتنعاً لذاته ثم انقلب ممكناً لذاته لكان الشيء الواحد قد انقلب من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي، وهو محال. وأيضاً: فحدوثه بعد أن حدث بدلاً عنه أيضاً ممكن بعين هذا الدليل. فيثبت: أن ابتداء حدوث العالم في ذلك الوقت وقبله وبعده: ممكن. وأن ذلك الإمكان ثابت في كل الأوقات على السوية. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فإن اختصاص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين، مع كونه مساوياً لسائر الأوقات، رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح، وأنه محال. فهذا تمام تقرير الدليل المذكور. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول: النقض المذكور إنما يتوجه على هذا الدليل، إذا أقمتم البرهان على أن جرم هذا الفلك المعين، قابل للحركة على هذا الوجه المعين، وقابل لسائر الحركات المضادة لها، وعلى
Let us return to detail and say: As for the first objection, which is your statement: 'The points intersecting the surface of the celestial sphere are equal in complete essence; so the determination of two points among those polar points, to the exclusion of the other points, would be a preponderance of one side of a possible over the other without a preponderator.' We say: The answer to it is that motion, when it occurs in that particular determined manner, necessitates the determination of these two points as polar, because it is rationally impossible for motion to occur in this specific manner without these two points being determined as polar. If they say: 'Granted that this is the case, but we say: Why did the motion of the sphere occur in this particular specific manner, rather than other possible manners?' We say: This is the second objection. For it we have stations of answer: The first station: We have demonstrated the temporality of the world at the time it occurred, and its temporality before that was possible. For if its temporality before that had been impossible per se, then it changed to possible per se, then the same thing would have changed from impossibility per se to possibility per se, which is impossible. Also, its temporality after it occurred instead is also possible by the same proof. So it is established that the beginning of the world's temporality at that time, before it, and after it is possible, and that possibility is established in all times equally. And if this is the case, then the specification of the world's temporality to that particular time, given its equality to all other times, would be a preponderance of one side of a possible over the other without a preponderator, and that is impossible. This is the complete formulation of the mentioned proof. And if you know this, we say: The mentioned objection is only directed against this proof if you provide the proof that this specific celestial body is receptive to motion in this specific manner and is receptive to other motions that oppose it, and upon
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the first objection regarding the celestial poles. The objectors claim that since all points on the sphere's surface are identical, selecting two as poles without a preponderator is arbitrary. The author responds by stating that the specific manner of motion necessarily determines which points become poles—it is not arbitrary but rationally necessitated by the motion. The objectors then shift their question: why did the motion itself occur in that specific way? The author identifies this as a second objection and provides his first station of answer. He presents a proof for the world's temporal origination in a particular moment. The argument is that the world's origination at that time, as well as before and after, is equally possible. If it were impossible before, then it would have changed from impossibility to possibility, which is absurd. Since all times are equally possible, the specification of that moment requires a preponderator—otherwise it would be arbitrary. This is a standard kalam argument for the necessity of a determining cause. The passage ends as the author prepares to condition the applicability of the objection.

حركة (motion)
حدوث العالم (temporality of the world)
إمكان (possibility)
امتناع ذاتي (impossibility per se)
رجحان (preponderance)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection about poles shows that the world's structure is arbitrary or random.
Correction: The author shows that the poles are necessitated by the specific motion; the real question is why that motion occurred, which requires a preponderator, not randomness.

Checkpoint
What is the author's first station answer to the second objection?
A) The motion of the sphere is eternal.
B) The specification of the world's temporality to a particular time requires a preponderator.
C) The poles are determined by divine command directly.
D) The points are not truly equal.

Key Takeaway
The specification of the world's origin to a particular time, given equal possibility, necessitates a preponderator.

The passage argues that equal possibilities demand a cause. In your daily life, you often assume things 'just happen'—a missed opportunity, a chance meeting. Will you commit today to trace every meaningful event back to its ultimate Determiner, or will you continue to live as if randomness rules?
?
The proof forces you to confront the question: If God did not preponderate your faith at this moment, would you even be reading this? How does this dependency move you from intellectual acknowledgment to actual gratitude and reliance on Him, right now?

The First Objection and Its Answer on Celestial Poles
The author presents the first objection and its answer regarding celestial poles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that celestial poles cannot be specified without a preponderator by appealing to the necessity arising from the specific motion.

Core Idea
The motion itself determines the poles; no external preponderator is needed.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection against the necessary existence of celestial poles. The objection argues that since all points on the sphere are essentially equal, selecting two as poles must require an external cause. The author's answer is crucial: the specific manner of motion itself necessitates the poles, rendering the objection invalid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: all points on the sphere are equal in essence, so specifying any two as poles without a preponderator (murajjih) seems arbitrary.
The objection is based on the principle of preponderance requiring a preponderator.
2
Grasp the principle involved
Note that the objection assumes that any specification among equals must have an external cause. This is a key principle in kalam.
3
See how the motion provides the specification
The answer argues that when motion occurs in a specific manner, it logically necessitates certain points as poles. No additional preponderator is needed beyond the motion itself.
Think of a rotating sphere: the axis of rotation determines the poles.
4
Reflect on logical impossibility
The author states it is rationally impossible for the motion to occur in that specific manner without those two points being poles. This shows that the poles are not arbitrary but necessitated.
This refutes the objection by showing that the specification is inherent in the motion.

Let us return to the detailed discussion, and we say: As for the first objection: it is your statement: 'The points interspersed on the surface of the sphere are equal in their complete essence; so the specification of two points among those points as the poles, to the exclusion of the rest, constitutes a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator.' We say: The answer to it: When the motion occurs in that specific determined manner, then the determination of these two points as poles becomes necessary, because it is rationally impossible for the motion to occur in this specific manner without these two points being determined as poles.

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection and its answer. The objection claims that since all points on the celestial sphere are essentially equal, specifying any two as poles—rather than others—would be an arbitrary preponderance without a preponderator (murajjih). The author refutes this by arguing that the motion itself, when it occurs in a specific manner, necessarily determines which points become poles. The motion's specific direction and axis make it logically impossible for those two points not to be the poles. Thus, no additional preponderator beyond the motion is needed.

Counter-Objection on the Equality of Motions
Opponent's counter-objection and first proof of essential equality of motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's counter-objection to the distinction between motions and the first proof that all motions are essentially equal in essence.

Core Idea
All motions share the same essence because they are defined as transition from one direction to another.

In this passage, the opponent challenges the author's earlier distinction by arguing that motions occurring in different manners are not essentially different. Instead, the opponent provides two proofs that all motions are equal in essence. The first proof is based on the definition of motion itself. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-objection
Read the opponent's opening statement where they challenge the assumption that motions differ in essence. They ask for evidence of such a difference.
2
Identify the two proofs
The opponent states there are two proofs. We will focus on the first proof, which is complete. Note the logical structure: definition → shared property → conclusion.
The second proof is incomplete in the source.
3
Analyze the first proof
The first proof argues that the meaning of motion is transition from one direction to another, and all motions share this meaning, so they must be equal in essence. Consider whether this argument is sound.

The first proof is that the definition of motion is merely 'transition from one direction to another,' and all motions share this meaning, so they are essentially the same. The second proof is that bodies are equal in essence, and whatever is true of one is true of another. However, the text cuts off before completing the second proof.

Distinguishing Motion from Origination: An Objection and Answer
Author distinguishes motion of a celestial sphere from origination of the world in an objection-response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the motion of a celestial sphere does not require a preponderator in the same way the origination of the world does, and how the author defends his origination argument against this objection.

Core Idea
Motion preference does not imply preference without a cause; essences may differ.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential objection to his argument for the world's origination. The objector claims that the specific motion of a celestial sphere would also require a preponderator without a cause, just like the world's origination at a specific time. The author responds by showing that the two cases are not analogous, because the sphere may only be receptive to that one motion. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector argues that the specific motion of a celestial sphere, like the world's origination, would require a preponderator without a cause. Read the first part of the passage where the author paraphrases this objection.
2
Identify the objector's hidden premise
The author points out that for the objection to hold, one must assume the sphere is equally receptive to all motions. Why is this premise problematic? Look at how the author argues against it.
3
Follow the author's counter-argument
The author explains that being receptive to one type of motion does not imply receptivity to its contrary. He cites the principle that different essences may have different necessary concomitants.
4
Contrast with the origination case
The author then distinguishes by noting that in the origination case, there was a transition from impossibility to possibility, whereas the sphere always had its motion. This is the key difference.
5
Summarize the lesson
Now that you know the distinction, you can see why the origination argument remains sound. The author successfully deflects the objection by showing the cases are not parallel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا التقرير فإنه يلزم أن يكون اختصاص جرم الفلك المعين وبهذه الحركة المعينة رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح. فعليكم أن تقيموا الدلالة على أن ذات الفلك المعين قابلة لجميع أنواع الحركات المختلفة حتى يتوجه النقض. ومعلوم أن هذه المقدمة ليست بينة لذاتها، لأن الحركة من المشرق إلى المغرب مضادة للحركة من المغرب إلى المشرق ومخالفة لها. ولا يلزم من كون الشيء قابلاً لصفة كونه قابلاً لما يخالف تلك الصفة وتضادها. لأنه لم يثبت في العقل أن الماهيات المختلفة يجب استواؤها في اللوازم والأحكام، فمن المحتمل أن يقال: جرم هذا الفلك لا يقبل إلا هذا النوع من الحركة، وبهذا التقدير لم يكن اختصاص هذا الفلك المعين بهذه الحركة المعينة رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح، بخلاف الدليل الذي ذكرناه. لأنا قلنا: العالم لما حدث في ذلك الوقت، فلو كان حدوثه قبل ذلك ممتنعاً لزم أن يقال: إنه انتقل من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي، وهو محال. أما ههنا فجرم الفلك لم ينتقل من نوع من الحركة إلى نوع آخر، بل الحاصل فيه ليس إلا هذا، فلم يبعد أن يقال: الممكن ليس إلا هذا النوع، أما سائر الأنواع فهي ممتنعة أبداً. فظهر الفرق بين البابين.
This formulation [of yours] would necessitate that the specification of a particular celestial body with this particular motion be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderator. So you must provide evidence that the essence of that particular celestial body is receptive to all different types of motion, so that the refutation may be directed. And it is known that this premise is not self-evident, because motion from east to west is contrary to motion from west to east and different from it. And it does not follow from a thing's being receptive to a property that it is receptive to what is contrary to that property and opposed to it. For it is not established in reason that different essences must be equal in their necessary concomitants and judgments. So it is possible to say: the body of this celestial sphere accepts only this type of motion. On this estimation, the specification of this particular celestial sphere with this particular motion would not be a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator, unlike the proof we mentioned. For we said: the world, when it originated at that time, if its origination before that had been impossible, it would have to be said that it had transitioned from essential impossibility to essential possibility, which is absurd. But here, the body of the celestial sphere did not transition from one type of motion to another type; rather, what obtains in it is only this [motion]. So it is not far-fetched to say: the possible is only this type; as for the other types, they are forever impossible. Thus the difference between the two cases is apparent.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer in which the author defends his earlier argument about the origination of the world. The objector had claimed that the specific motion of a celestial sphere would also require a preponderator without a cause, similar to the author's argument that the world's occurrence at a specific time requires a preponderator. The author responds by first stating that to make the objection work, the objector must prove that the celestial sphere is equally receptive to all types of motion—a premise that is not self-evident. He then explains why this premise is weak: motion from east to west is contrary to motion from west to east, and being receptive to one does not imply being receptive to its contrary, because essences (mahiyyat) are not necessarily equal in their attributes. Thus, it is possible that the sphere only accepts that one motion, making its specification not a case of preference without a preponderator. The author then distinguishes this from the origination argument: in the case of the world, there was a transition from impossibility to possibility (if the world had always been impossible before its occurrence), which is absurd; but in the case of motion, the sphere always had that motion and did not transition. Therefore, the two cases are different.

الرجحان بلا مرجح
الإمكان
الامتناع
الحركة
الماهية
التماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies the need for a preponderator altogether.
Correction: The author only shows that the motion case is not analogous to origination; he does not deny the principle of preponderator in general.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that the specification of a particular motion to a celestial sphere does not necessarily require a preponderator without a cause?
A) Because the sphere's motion is caused by its nature.
B) Because the objector has not proven that the sphere accepts all types of motion equally.
C) Because all motions are essentially the same.
D) Because the sphere's motion is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The motion of a celestial sphere is not analogous to the origination of the world because the sphere may only be naturally receptive to that motion, whereas the world's origination required a transition from impossibility to possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The author distinguishes between a transition (impossible to possible) and a fixed state. In your spiritual life, are you mistaking a fixed nature (like a trial) for a change (like punishment), causing resentment? How can you accept that some things are simply as they are by God's wisdom, not requiring a cause you understand?

The Objector's Two Proofs for Motion Identity
Objector enumerates two proofs for motion identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's claim that all motions are identical in essence and the two proofs they offer.

Core Idea
The objector argues all motions share the same generic meaning and bodies are similar.

A key objection to proving God's existence from motion is that motions might be identical in essence, making it impossible to distinguish necessary from contingent. The objector presents two arguments: first, all motions are just 'transition from place to place'; second, all bodies are alike, so what applies to one must apply to its like. Understanding these objections is essential before we see how the author dismantles them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's challenge
The objector first questions the author's distinction between motions. Then they present two proofs. Read carefully to identify each proof.
The first proof is about the definition of motion as transition.
2
Analyze the first proof
The objector says motion means 'transition from place to place,' and all motions share this meaning, so they must be identical in essence.
Think about whether a shared generic meaning makes two things identical in their specific essence.
3
Analyze the second proof
The objector says all bodies are identical in essence, so what is possible for one celestial sphere should be possible for its like, leading to the original objection.
This proof relies on the principle of 'like for like' and the similarity of celestial spheres.
4
Reflect on the overall objection
Consider how these two proofs, if accepted, would undermine the author's argument about the world's beginning.
The objector aims to show that the author's reasoning leads to an absurdity.

فإن قالوا: هذا الفرق الذي ذكرتم إنما يتلخص لو ثبت أن الحركة الواقعة على هذا الوجه الخاص مخالفة بالماهية للحركة الواقعة على سائر الوجوه، فما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك؟ بل نقول: الدليل على تساوي هذه الحركات في تمام الماهية وجهان: الأول: إنه لا معنى للحركة إلا الانتقال من حيز إلى حيز، والحركات بأسرها متساوية في هذا المعنى، فوجب كونها متساوية في تمام الماهية. الثاني: إن الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية وكل ما صح على شيء فإنه يصح على مثله. ثم إن الفلك الأعظم متحرك من المشرق إلى المغرب، وفلك الثوابت متحرك من المغرب إلى المشرق، وإذا ثبت تماثل الأجسام بأسرها، وجب أن يصح على كل واحد من هذين الفلكين ما حصل للآخر من الحركة. وحينئذ يعود الإلزام. هذا تمام تقرير هذا السؤال. واعلم أن هذا الكلام ضعيف. أما قولهم: ما الدليل على أن هذه الحركات مختلفة بالماهية؟ قلنا: الدليل لا يلزمنا في المقام. وذلك لأنا قلنا: اختصاص حدوث العالم بوقت معين يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح. وأنتم ادعيتم أن مثل هذا الإلزام لازم في صورة الحركة. فيا لم تقيموا الدليل على أن قبول الفلك المعين لجميع أنواع الحركة على السوية، فإنه لا يتم إلزامكم. فيثبت: أنكم أنتم المطالبون بذكر هذا الدليل. بل نقول: لو كانت الحركتان متساويتين بالنسبة إلى ذلك الفلك المعين، لكان اختصاصه بتلك الحركة المعينة دون سائر الحركات رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن لا لمرجح، وهو محال. وأما الذي احتجوا به أولاً على تماثل هذه الحركات في تمام الماهية: فضعيف. لأنا نقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: [المفهوم] الذي ذكرتموه من الحركة: مفهوم جنسي. ويدخل تحته أنواع كثيرة، ويكون كل نوع من أنواع الحركة: ممتازاً عن الآخر بفصل مقوم لماهيته المخصوصة. ألا ترى أن العقلاء اتفقوا على أن الحركة من المركز إلى المحيط مخالفة بالماهية للحركة من المحيط إلى المركز، مع كون كل واحدة منهما مشاركة للأخرى في كونها انتقالاً من حيز إلى حيز فكذا ههنا. وأما الذي احتجوا به ثانياً من أن الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية، فوجب أن يصح على كل واحد... لكنه لا يلزم من تماثل الأجسام في الماهية أن تكون متساوية في قبول الحركات المختلفة، لأن القبول قد يختلف باختلاف الجهات والمقادير وغيرها. وقد بينا أن الحركات مختلفة بالماهية، فلا إلزام.
Then they said: 'This difference you mentioned is only validated if it is established that the motion occurring in this particular manner is different in essence from motions occurring in all other manners. So what is the evidence that it is so? Rather, we say: the evidence for the equality of these motions in complete essence is two aspects: First: there is no meaning of motion except transition from one place to another, and all motions are equal in this meaning, so they must be equal in complete essence. Second: bodies are equal in complete essence, and whatever is valid for a thing is valid for its like. Then the greatest celestial sphere is moved from east to west, and the sphere of the fixed stars is moved from west to east. If the similarity of all bodies is established, then it must be valid for each of these two spheres [to have] the motion that obtained in the other. And then the [original] objection returns.' This is the complete formulation of this question. Know that this speech is weak. As for their saying, 'What is the evidence that these motions are different in essence?' we say: the evidence is not incumbent upon us in this context. That is because we said: the specification of the world's origination to a particular time demands a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator. And you claimed that such an objection is necessary in the case of motion. So why have you not established the evidence that the particular celestial sphere accepts all types of motion equally? For then your objection is not completed. So it is established that you are the ones required to mention this evidence. Rather, we say: if the two motions were equal with respect to that particular celestial sphere, then its specification to that particular motion without other motions would be a preponderance of one side of the possible without a preponderator, which is absurd. As for their first argument for the similarity of these motions in complete essence: it is weak. For we say: why is it not possible to say that the concept which you mentioned of motion is a generic concept, and under it enter many species, and each species of motion is distinguished from the other by a differentia that constitutes its specific essence? Do you not see that the rational scholars agreed that motion from the center to the circumference is different in essence from motion from the circumference to the center, despite each of them sharing with the other in being a transition from one place to another? So likewise here. As for their second argument, that bodies are equal in complete essence, so it must be valid for each one... but it does not follow from the similarity of bodies in essence that they are equal in accepting different motions, because acceptance may differ due to directions, magnitudes, and other factors. And we have shown that motions are different in essence, so there is no [necessary] imposition.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection from the opponents and the author's detailed refutation. The objector first claims that the author's distinction only holds if motions are different in essence, and then they provide two proofs that all motions are identical in essence. The first proof: all motions share the meaning of transition (intiqal), so they are identical in essence. The second proof: all bodies are similar in essence, so what is possible for one body (like a specific motion) must be possible for another similar body; thus the celestial spheres should be able to exchange motions, leading back to the original objection. The author then refutes both proofs. First, he shifts the burden of proof: the objector must show that the sphere accepts all motions equally, not the author. He argues that if the motions were equal for that sphere, the specification would be problematic, but that is exactly what the objector needs to prove. Then he attacks the first proof by noting that 'motion' is a genus with species; the shared meaning of transition is generic, and specific differences (e.g., direction) differentiate species, just as motion from center to circumference is different from its opposite. Thus motions are not identical in essence. For the second proof, the author (as available in the OCR) argues that body similarity does not entail equal receptivity to all motions, as receptivity can vary due to other factors. The refutation is cut off in the OCR, but the gist remains.

Key Terms
تماثل الحركات
تمام الماهية
الانتقال من حيز إلى حيز
تماثل الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing a generic meaning (e.g., transition) implies complete identity of essence.
Correction: The author will later argue that 'transition' is only a genus; species differ by differentia.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof the objector gives for all motions being identical in essence?
A) Motions are all created by God.
B) All motions share the meaning of 'transition from one place to another'.
C) Celestial spheres are made of the same substance.
D) Motion from east to west is the same as from west to east.

Key Takeaway
The objector's two proofs rely on the assumptions that a shared generic definition constitutes identity of essence and that body similarity implies equal receptivity to all motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a position, do you ever rely on the mere fact that two things share a name or a description to conclude they are essentially the same? How might this lesson expose a hidden flaw in your reasoning? Commit to examining your arguments for such false equivalences this week.
?
The objector assumes that what is possible for one body is possible for another simply because they are 'similar.' Are there areas in your life where you assume that another person's abilities, struggles, or blessings automatically apply to you? How does this assumption reveal a lack of gratitude or humility? Repent for any such presumptuous comparisons.
?
In proving motion identity, the objector reduces motion to mere 'transition.' Do you ever reduce the depth of Islamic concepts—like worship, patience, or love—to simplistic definitions that strip them of their true essence? How can you train yourself to embrace the richness of these concepts as taught in the Qur'an and Sunnah, rather than accepting shallow interpretations?

The Author's Shift of Burden
Author dismisses objection and reverses burden of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author turns the tables by arguing that the objector must prove the sphere accepts all motions equally.

Core Idea
The author shifts the burden: the objector must prove, not assume, motion equality.

The author dismisses the objection as weak and immediately challenges the objector's demand for evidence. He points out that it is the objector who needs to prove that the celestial sphere accepts all types of motion equally; otherwise, their objection collapses. He then shows that if motions were equal for that sphere, the specification to one motion would be absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's initial dismissal
The author begins by calling the objection weak. Then he responds to the demand for evidence.
Notice the shift from defense to offense.
2
Identify the burden shift
The author says: you claimed motion leads to an absurdity, so you must prove the sphere accepts all motions equally.
This is a common logical move: the one who makes a claim must provide evidence.
3
Understand the reductio
If motions were equal, the sphere's specification to one motion would be a preponderance without preponderator, which is absurd.
This mirrors the original argument about the world's beginning.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the objector must provide evidence about the celestial sphere?
A) Because the author does not know anything about the sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your critique of someone else's argument was completely parallel, only to be shown that you were missing a key proof? How does this passage expose the danger of assuming symmetry in logic, and how will you check your own reasoning next time?
?
The author demands that the objector prove the sphere's equal receptivity. What foundational assumptions about your own beliefs have you taken for granted that might need proving? Are you willing to shoulder the burden of proof for your doubts, or do you expect others to disprove you?
?
In controversies about faith or worldview, who bears the burden of proof? This passage flips the burden onto the objector. How does this challenge your approach when you find yourself objecting to a truth claim—do you first ensure your own premises are sound?

Refutation of the Two Proofs
Author refutes the two proofs for motion identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the objector's two proofs for motion identity.

Core Idea
Motion is a genus with species; body similarity does not imply equal receptivity.

The author now directly engages the objector's two proofs. He argues that 'transition' is only a generic concept, and motions are distinguished by specific differentiae, like motion from center to circumference versus its opposite. For the second proof, he asserts that body similarity does not entail equal acceptance of different motions, since receptivity can vary due to direction, magnitude, etc.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the first proof
The author says the concept of motion is generic, with many species under it, each distinguished by a differentia.
He gives the example of motion from center to circumference versus the opposite.
2
Read the refutation of the second proof
The author argues that even if bodies are similar in essence, they may differ in their receptivity to motions due to other factors.
The passage is partially reconstructed but the point is clear.
3
Reflect on the overall refutation
Combine the two refutations: motions are not identical in essence, so the objector's objection fails.
This completes the author's response.

And we have shown that motions are different in essence, so there is no [necessary] imposition.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection from the opponents and the author's detailed refutation. The objector first claims that the author's distinction only holds if motions are different in essence, and then they provide two proofs that all motions are identical in essence. The first proof: all motions share the meaning of transition (intiqal), so they are identical in essence. The second proof: all bodies are similar in essence, so what is possible for one body (like a specific motion) must be possible for another similar body; thus the celestial spheres should be able to exchange motions, leading back to the original objection. The author then refutes both proofs. First, he shifts the burden of proof: the objector must show that the sphere accepts all motions equally, not the author. He argues that if the motions were equal for that sphere, the specification would be problematic, but that is exactly what the objector needs to prove. Then he attacks the first proof by noting that 'motion' is a genus with species; the shared meaning of transition is generic, and specific differences (e.g., direction) differentiate species, just as motion from center to circumference is different from its opposite. Thus motions are not identical in essence. For the second proof, the author (as available in the OCR) argues that body similarity does not entail equal receptivity to all motions, as receptivity can vary due to other factors. The refutation is cut off in the OCR, but the gist remains.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a generic definition, they are identical in essence.
Correction: The author clarifies that a genus is not the complete essence; species are defined by specific differentiae.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show that motions can be different in essence despite sharing the generic meaning of transition?
A) Motion of the sun and moon.
B) Motion from center to circumference vs. circumference to center.
C) Motion of a bird and a fish.
D) Motion from east to west vs. west to east.

Key Takeaway
The objector's arguments are refuted: motion is a genus with species, and body similarity does not imply equal receptivity to all motions.

Refutation of the Objection on Celestial Motions
Author refutes the objection that motions are different in essence and demands proof from the opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author shifts the burden of proof in the debate over celestial motions, demanding evidence from the objector.

Core Idea
The objector must prove that motions differ in essence, not just assume it.

In this passage, the author addresses a critical objection against the argument from celestial motions. The objector claims that the difference in direction between the spheres shows they are not alike. The author responds by demanding evidence that the sphere is equally receptive to both motions, turning the tables on the objector.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The author first presents the objector's argument: if all bodies are alike, then motions should be interchangeable between the spheres. But the outer sphere moves east-west and the fixed stars sphere moves west-east, so they are not alike.
Notice that the objector assumes the motions are different in essence.
2
Identify the author's critique
The author dismisses the objection as weak and shifts the burden. He asks: 'What is the evidence that these motions are different in essence?' and argues that the objector has not provided proof.
The author claims the evidence is not incumbent upon him in this context.
3
Follow the logical counter
The author argues that the objector must prove that the specific sphere equally receives all types of motion. Without that proof, the objection is incomplete.
This shifts the burden from the author to the objector.
4
Grasp the final argument
The author further argues: if the two motions were equally possible for that sphere, then specifying one motion without a preponderator would be impossible (محال). Thus, the objector's assumption leads to an absurdity.
This strengthens the author's rebuttal by showing the objector's own position is untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه يصح على مثله . ثم إن الفلك الأعظم متحرك من المشرق إلى المغرب ، وفلك الثوابت متحرك من المغرب إلى المشرق ، وإذا ثبت تماثل الأجسام بأسرها ، وجب أن ، وجب أن يصح على كل واحد من هذين الفلكين ، ما حصل للآخر من الحركة . وحينئذ يعود الإلزام. هذا تمام [ تقرير (۱) ] هذا السؤال واعلم أن هذا الكلام ضعيف . أما قولهم : ما الدليل على أن هذه الحركات مختلفة بالماهية ؟ قلنا : الدليل لا يلزمنا في المقام . وذلك لأنا قلنا : اختصاص حدوث العالم بوقت معين : يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجع . وأنتم ادعيتم أن مثل هذا الإلزام لازم في صورة الحركة . فيا لم تقيموا الدليل على أن قبول الفلك المعين الجميع أنواع الحركة (۲) على السوية ، فإنه لا يتم إلزامكم . فيثبت : أنكم أنتم المطالبون بذكر هذا الدليل . بل نقول : لو كانت الحركتان متساويتين بالنسبة إلى ذلك الفلك المعين ، لكان اختصاصه بتلك الحركة المعينة دون سائر الحركات رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن لا لمرجح ، وهو محال
It is valid for the like of it. Then the greatest sphere moves from east to west, and the sphere of the fixed stars moves from west to east. And if the similarity of all bodies is established, it would be necessary that whatever motion occurs for the other should be valid for each of these two spheres. Then the objection returns. This completes the formulation of this question. And know that this speech is weak. As for their saying: 'What is the evidence that these motions are different in essence?' We say: The evidence is not incumbent upon us in this context. That is because we said: The specification of the world's coming-to-be at a specific time requires a preponderance of one of the two possible ends without a preponderator. And you claimed that such an objection is necessary in the case of motion. So why have you not established the evidence that the specific sphere equally receives all types of motion? For then your objection is not complete. Thus it is established that you are the ones required to mention this evidence. Rather we say: If the two motions were equal with respect to that specific sphere, then its specification with that particular motion to the exclusion of other motions would be a preponderance of one of the two possible ends without a preponderator, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its rebuttal concerning the motion of the celestial spheres. The author first restates the objection: if the celestial spheres are alike in being bodies, then whatever motion applies to one should apply to the other. But the outer sphere moves east-west and the sphere of the fixed stars moves west-east. The objector argues that this difference in motion shows that they are not alike. The author then responds: the objector claims the motions are different in essence, but they have not provided evidence that the sphere is equally receptive to all motions. The author argues that the burden of proof is on the objector. Further, if the two motions were equally possible for that sphere, then its specification with one motion without a preponderator would be impossible, as it would be an unjustified preference. Thus the objector cannot merely assume the motions are different in essence; they must prove it.

Key Terms
فلك
حركة
ماهية
رجحان
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector assumes that because the spheres move in opposite directions, their motions are necessarily different in essence without needing proof.
Correction: The author argues that the difference in direction alone is insufficient; one must prove that the motions are essentially different and that the sphere is not equally receptive to both.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would be the consequence if the two motions were equally possible for the same sphere?
A) Both motions would occur simultaneously.
B) The sphere would have no motion.
C) The specification of one motion would be a preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible.
D) The motions would be identical in essence.

Key Takeaway
The burden of proof falls on those who claim motions are different in essence; assuming difference without evidence leads to the impossible consequence of preponderance without a preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted on an opinion without providing evidence, expecting others to disprove it? How does this lesson expose the weakness of that approach, and what will you do the next time you catch yourself doing it?

The objector assumed his observation was enough. In what areas of your faith or life have you settled for assumptions rather than seeking deeper proof? What is one assumption you will now examine more closely?
?
The author shifts the burden to the objector. Are there arguments you make about others where you have not established your own premises? Will you, today, commit to verifying your assumptions before blaming or judging?

Motion as a Genus: Rebuttal of Sameness
Author rebuts first proof: motion is a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion is a generic concept with distinct species, not a single essence.

Core Idea
Motion is a genus, not one essence.

The author addresses the claim that all motions are identical in essence. He points out that the concept of motion they use is actually a generic concept, encompassing many species each with its own specific differentia. This rebuttal uses logical classification to show that motions can share the generic name yet differ essentially.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the translation. Notice how the author begins by stating the proof is weak.
2
Identify the generic concept argument
The author says the concept of motion is a generic concept under which many species fall. Each species is distinguished by a differentia that constitutes its specific essence.
This is a key logical move: what the opponents thought was a single essence is actually a genus.
3
Analyze the example
The author gives the example of motion from center to periphery vs. periphery to center. These are both transitions but differ in essence. This illustrates the genus-species relationship.
Notice that rational people agree these are essentially different.
4
Connect to the main point
Understand that this rebuttal refutes the claim that motions are identical. The opponent's proof is weak because it confuses generic similarity with specific identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي احتجوا به أولاً على تماثل هذه الحركات في تمام الماهية : فضعيف . لأنا نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : [ المفهوم ] الذي ذكرتموه من الحركة : مفهوم جنسي . ويدخل تحته أنواع كثيرة ، ويكون كل نوع من أنواع الحركة : ممتازاً عن الآخر بفصل (٤) مقوم لماهيته المخصوصة . ألا ترى أن العقلاء اتفقوا على أن الحركة من المركز إلى المحيط ، مخالفة بالماهية للحركة من المحيط إلى المركز، مع كون كل واحدة منهما مشاركة للأخرى في كونها انتقالاً من حيز إلى حيز فكذا ههنا . وأما الذي احتجوا به ثانياً من أن الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية، فوجب أن يصح على كل مواحد منها ما يصح على الآخر. فنقول: هذا بناء على أن الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية. وهذه المقدمة ضعيفة. وأيضاً : فلو سلمنا ذلك، إلا أن مذهب الفلاسفة أن الجسمية صورة حالة في مادة. ومذهبهم : أن مادة كل فلك مخالفة لمادة الفلك الآخر، وإذا كان كذلك ، لم يلزم من كون أحد الفلكين قابلاً لنوع مخصوص من أنواع الحركة ، كونه قابلاً لسائر أنواع الحركة(۱) ولأجل هذا الحرف . زعموا : أن الخرق والإلتشام ممتنعان على الأفلاك . وأيضاً : فبتقدير أن يثبت تماثل الأجسام ، لزم القول بأن كل ما يصح على هذه الأجسام السفلية فإنه يصح أيضاً على الأجرام الفلكية ، وحينئذ لا يختص الإلزام المذكور بتعين النقطتين للقطبية ، وتعين تلك الحركة المخصوصة بأن تكون أولى بالوقوع . بل يعود الإلزام في اختصاص جرمية ذلك الفلك ، بذلك الشكل المخصوص [ وبذلك الحيز المخصوص (۲)] وبتلك الصفات المخصوصة [ والله أعلم (٣) ]
As for their first proof concerning the similarity of these motions in their complete essence, it is weak. For we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the concept of motion you mentioned is a generic concept, and under it fall many species, and each species of motion is distinguished from another by a differential (فصل) constitutive of its specific essence? Do you not see that the rational people agree that motion from the center to the periphery is different in essence from motion from the periphery to the center, even though each of them shares with the other in being a transition from one place to another? So likewise here. As for their second proof, that bodies are equal in complete essence, so it must be that whatever is valid for one is valid for the other. We say: This is based on the premise that bodies are equal in complete essence, and this premise is weak. Moreover, even if we grant that, the doctrine of the philosophers is that corporeality (جسمية) is a form residing in matter, and their doctrine is that the matter of each sphere is different from the matter of the other sphere. If this is so, it does not follow that because one sphere is receptive to a specific type of motion, it is receptive to all types of motion. For this reason they claimed that penetration and coalescence are impossible in the spheres. Furthermore, even if the similarity of bodies were established, it would necessitate saying that whatever is valid for these sublunary bodies is also valid for celestial bodies. Then the aforementioned objection would not be restricted to the determination of the two poles and the determination of that specific motion as being most likely to occur. Rather the objection would recur concerning the specification of that sphere's corporeality with that specific shape, that specific place, and those specific attributes. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage contains two rebuttals to proofs for the sameness of motions and bodies. First, the author rebuts the claim that all motions are identical in essence. He argues that motion is a generic concept under which many species are subsumed, each distinguished by a differentia that constitutes its specific essence. He gives the example of motion from center to periphery and from periphery to center, which are different in essence despite both being transitions from one place to another. Second, the author rebuts the claim that all bodies are identical in essence. He states this premise is weak. Even if granted, the philosophers hold that corporeality is a form in matter, and the matter of each sphere differs. Hence, receptivity to one motion does not imply receptivity to all. He further argues that if bodies were completely alike, then sublunary and celestial bodies would share properties, leading to absurd consequences. Thus the original objection about the specification of motion and shape is not unique.

Key Terms
جنس
فصل
ماهية
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All motions are identical in essence because they share the name 'motion'.
Correction: Motion is a genus with species; sharing the generic name does not imply identical essence. Different motions can have different specific essences.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the concept of motion is:
A) A single essence
B) A generic concept with species
C) Non-existent
D) A specific differentia

Key Takeaway
Motion is a genus containing essentially different species; thus not all motions share a single essence.

What deeply held belief about yourself or your religion must you re-examine because you assumed it was a single 'essence' when it is actually a category with distinct species?
?
If motion as a genus contains essentially different species, then your own actions—your prayers, your work, your relationships—likely also have species with different essences. Which action in your daily life have you been treating as a monolith, and what concrete step will you take today to recognize its internal diversity and respond accordingly?

Bodies Are Not Identical: Rebuttal of Sameness in Bodies
Author rebuts second proof: bodies not identical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that all bodies are identical in essence, using arguments about matter and consequences.

Core Idea
Bodies differ due to different matter and forms.

The author rejects the claim that all bodies are equal in complete essence. He points out that the premise itself is weak, and even if granted, the philosophers' doctrine of matter and form shows that bodies can differ, and that assuming sameness leads to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the author first attacks the premise, then provides an alternative explanation, then draws out consequences.
2
Examine the attack on the premise
The author says the premise that bodies are equal in complete essence is weak. This is a direct rebuttal of the foundation of the proof.
3
Understand the matter-form argument
Even if granted, the philosophers hold that corporeality is a form in matter, and the matter of each sphere is different. Therefore, receptivity to one motion does not imply receptivity to all.
This shows that difference in matter can cause difference in properties.
4
Follow the reduction to absurdity
If bodies were completely alike, then sublunary and celestial bodies would share all properties, which would lead to absurd conclusions about the specification of shape, place, etc.
This strengthens the rebuttal by showing the consequences of the opposing view.
