The Third Objection: Planets in Simple Spheres
Author presents a difficult objection and begins an answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the simplicity of celestial spheres concerning planetary placement, and the first part of the author's response distinguishing the outermost sphere.

Core Idea
The hardest objection to sphere simplicity is answered by limiting simplicity to the highest sphere.

The author now tackles a particularly difficult objection: if the celestial sphere is simple and uniform, why do planets occupy only specific parts of it? This objection seems to undermine the entire notion of celestial simplicity. The author acknowledges its difficulty and begins to answer by distinguishing the outermost sphere from the others.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objection: planets occupy specific places in the sphere, challenging its simplicity.
Notice the author calls it 'difficult and problematic'.
2
Identify the author's acknowledgment
The author admits the objection is tough before launching into the answer. This shows intellectual honesty.
3
Understand the first aspect of the answer
The author restricts the philosophers' proof of simplicity to the outermost sphere (the one determining directions). The other spheres have no proof of simplicity and contain planets.
This defuses the objection for spheres that are not proven simple.

Thus the objection loses its force for spheres that are not simple. The text cuts off before completing the first aspect, likely continuing on the next page.

Refutation of the Premise that All Bodies are Equal in Essence
Author refutes the premise of body equality and defends distinct celestial matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the premise that all bodies are equal in essence is rejected by the philosophers, and how the author defends the distinct natures of celestial bodies.

Core Idea
Equality of bodies in essence is a weak premise; celestial bodies have distinct matters and motions.

The author confronts an implicit objection that assumes all bodies are identical. He declares the premise weak and offers three arguments: the philosophers' view of corporeality as a form in distinct matters, the basis for rejecting penetration in spheres, and the consequences if equality were true. This lesson unpacks those arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author structures his refutation with multiple 'also' (وأيضاً) points.
The passage is dense; take your time to follow each argument.
2
Identify the three arguments
The author gives three separate lines of refutation. Find each one: the weak premise, the philosophers' doctrine on matter, and the consequence if equality were accepted.
The second argument includes the key concept of الخرق والإلتئام.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why does it matter whether celestial bodies have distinct matters? How does this defend the philosophers' view of the cosmos?
This connects to broader theological and philosophical debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يصح على كل مواحد منها ما يصح على الآخر. فنقول: هذا بناء على أن الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية. وهذه المقدمة ضعيفة. وأيضاً: فلو سلمنا ذلك، إلا أن مذهب الفلاسفة أن الجسمية صورة حالة في مادة. ومذهبهم: أن مادة كل فلك مخالفة لمادة الفلك الآخر، وإذا كان كذلك، لم يلزم من كون أحد الفلكين قابلاً لنوع مخصوص من أنواع الحركة، كونه قابلاً لسائر أنواع الحركة. ولأجل هذا الحرف زعموا: أن الخرق والإلتئام ممتنعان على الأفلاك. وأيضاً: فبتقدير أن يثبت تماثل الأجسام، لزم القول بأن كل ما يصح على هذه الأجسام السفلية فإنه يصح أيضاً على الأجرام الفلكية، وحينئذ لا يختص الإلزام المذكور بتعين النقطتين للقطبية، وتعين تلك الحركة المخصوصة بأن تكون أولى بالوقوع. بل يعود الإلزام في اختصاص جرمية ذلك الفلك، بذلك الشكل المخصوص وبذلك الحيز المخصوص وبتلك الصفات المخصوصة. والله أعلم.
That what is valid for each one of them should be valid for the other. We say: This is based on the fact that bodies are equal in their complete essence. And this premise is weak. Also: even if we concede that, the doctrine of the philosophers is that corporeality is a form inherent in matter. And their doctrine is that the matter of each sphere is different from the matter of the other sphere, and if that is the case, then from the fact that one of the two spheres is receptive to a specific type of movement, it does not follow that it is receptive to all types of movement. Because of this point they claimed that penetration and reunion are impossible for the spheres. Also: even if the similarity of bodies is established, it would necessitate saying that everything that is valid for these lower bodies is also valid for the celestial bodies, and then the aforementioned objection would not be specific to the determination of the two points for polarity, nor to the determination of that specific movement as being more appropriate to occur. Rather, the objection would return regarding the specificity of the corporeality of that sphere with that specific form, that specific location, and those specific attributes. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an implicit objection that assumes all bodies are identical in essence. The author begins by pointing out that the objection relies on the premise that bodies are equal in their complete essence (تمام الماهية), which he immediately declares weak. He then provides three supporting arguments. First, even if we grant the premise, the philosophers hold that corporeality (الجسمية) is a form (صورة) inhering in matter (مادة), and that the matter of each celestial sphere is distinct. Therefore, being receptive to one type of motion does not imply receptivity to all; this is why they affirm the impossibility of penetration and reunion (الخرق والإلتئام) in the spheres. Second, if bodies were truly similar, then whatever is possible for lower (sublunar) bodies would also be possible for celestial bodies, which would undermine the specific assignments of the poles and the unique motion of the spheres—the objection would then apply to the very specificity of the sphere's form, location, and attributes. The author ends with 'والله أعلم' (And God knows best), indicating closure of this argument.

Simplicity Only of the Outermost Sphere
The author distinguishes the outermost sphere as simple while other spheres are not proven simple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only the outermost sphere is proven simple and how this resolves the objection about star locations.

Core Idea
Only the starless outermost sphere is proven simple.

This passage presents the first of three responses to the third objection, which questioned why stars are placed in specific parts of the celestial sphere. The author argues that the philosophers' proof of simplicity applies solely to the outermost sphere—the one that determines directions. Since the other spheres contain stars embedded in their substance, they cannot be proven simple. This distinction removes the force of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: a claim about the outermost sphere, then a contrast with other spheres, then a conclusion that removes the objection.
The key is the contrast between the greatest sphere and the star-containing spheres.
2
Identify the two categories of spheres
The author distinguishes between the greatest sphere (outermost) and the other spheres. Observe what characterizes each: the greatest sphere has no stars; the others have stars embedded in them.
The term 'مركوزة' means 'embedded' or 'fixed'.
3
Understand the logical implication
The presence of stars in some sides of a sphere but not others would give it differing natures, contradicting simplicity. Therefore, only the sphere without stars can be proven simple.
This is the core of the argument: simplicity requires homogeneity, which stars would disrupt.
4
See how the question is removed
Since the simple sphere has no stars, and the star-containing spheres are not proven simple, there is no need to explain why stars are in specific places of a simple sphere. The objection fails.
The phrase 'فزال السؤال' means 'so the question is removed'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول: إن الذي ذكره الحكماء على كون الفلك بسيطاً: مختص بالفلك الأعظم، الذي هو فلك المحدد للجهات، أما سائر الأفلاك فلم يحصل لهم دليل على كونها بسيطة. وهذه الكواكب مركوزة في سائر الأفلاك، وأما الفلك الأعظم فلم يثبت حصول شيء من الكواكب فيه، فالفلك الذي اختلفت طبائع جوانبه، بسبب حصول الكواكب في بعض جوانبه دون البعض، لم يثبت بالدليل كونه بسيطاً، والفلك الذي ثبت بالدليل كونه بسيطاً، لم يحصل فيه شيء من الكواكب، فزال السؤال.
The first aspect: That which the philosophers mentioned regarding the sphere being simple is specific to the greatest sphere, which is the sphere determining the directions. As for the other spheres, they have no proof that they are simple. And these stars are embedded in the other spheres. As for the greatest sphere, it has not been established that any star is in it. So the sphere whose sides have different natures due to the presence of stars in some of its sides but not others has not been proven by evidence to be simple; and the sphere which has been proven by evidence to be simple has no star in it. Thus the question is removed.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is the first of three responses to the third objection regarding why a specific place in the sphere is allocated to stars. The author argues that the philosophers' proof of the sphere's simplicity (البساطة) applies only to the outermost sphere—the 'falak al-a'zam' (greatest sphere) which determines the directions (جهات). Other spheres, where stars are actually embedded (مركوزة), have no proof of simplicity. He reasons that a sphere whose sides differ in nature because stars exist in some parts and not others cannot be proven simple; conversely, the sphere proven simple contains no stars. Therefore, the objection is resolved because the star-containing spheres are not simple, so the specificity of star location is expected.

Simultaneous Creation of Stars and Spheres
The author clarifies the philosophers' simultaneous creation of stars and spheres to refute the transfer objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why philosophers argue that stars and their spheres are created simultaneously, and how this prevents the possibility of star movement or transfer.

Core Idea
Stars and spheres are co-created, eliminating the need for motion.

The objection against the philosophers' view of star placement is met with a key distinction: the star and its sphere do not exist sequentially but simultaneously. This lesson unpacks that response, showing how it avoids logical problems like penetration and reunion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the author's structure: denial of a view, statement of the correct view, and conclusion.
2
Identify the two models
Contrast the sequential model (sphere first, then star inserted) with the simultaneous model (star and sphere come into existence together). Note why philosophers reject the sequential model.
The sequential model would require penetration and reunion, considered impossible.
3
Understand the implication
If creation is simultaneous, there is no time when the sphere exists without the star. Therefore, no question of 'why this star here rather than there' arises, and transfer is impossible.
Transfer would also necessitate penetration and reunion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في الفرق: أن يقال: إن القوم لا يقولون: إن الفلك حصل فيه ذلك التجويف، ثم حصلت الكواكب فيه، لأن هذا يقتضي حصول الخرق والإلتئام على الفلك، بل يقولون: حصول الكوكب مقارن لحصول الفلك، فما كان الفلك حاصلا قبل حصول ذلك الكوكب حتى يقال ليس حصول الكوكب ههنا أولى من حصوله في موضع آخر، ولما ثبت أن حصول الكوكب، وحصول الفلك معاً، فبعد ذلك يمتنع انتقال الكوكب من ذلك الموضع، لأن تجويزه يوجب تجويز الخرق والالتئام على الفلك، وأنه محال. فلهذا السبب بقي ذلك الكوكب في ذلك الوضع المعين من الفلك.
The second aspect of the distinction: It may be said that the people (philosophers) do not say that the sphere obtained that cavity, then the stars came to exist in it, because this would imply the occurrence of penetration and reunion in the sphere. Rather, they say: the coming to be of the star is simultaneous with the coming to be of the sphere. So the sphere was not existing before the coming to be of that star such that it could be said that the star's coming to be here is no more appropriate than its coming to be in another place. And since it is established that the star and the sphere come to be together, thereafter it is impossible for the star to move from that place, because allowing that would necessitate allowing penetration and reunion in the sphere, which is impossible. For this reason, that star remained in that specific position of the sphere.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This is the second response to the third objection about why stars occupy specific places. The author explains that philosophers do not claim that the sphere first existed as a hollow receptacle into which stars were later placed—that would imply penetration (خرق) and reunion (التئام) in the sphere, which they deem impossible. Instead, they hold that the star and its sphere come into existence simultaneously (مقارن). Since the sphere did not exist prior to the star, one cannot argue that the star's placement in one spot is more likely than another; the star and sphere are co-created. After that co-creation, the star cannot move from its place because moving would again involve penetration and reunion, which is impossible. Hence the star remains fixed in its determined position.

Key Terms
مقارن
الخرق والإلتئام
تجويف
انتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Stars are placed into pre-existing hollow spheres.
Correction: Philosophers hold stars and spheres are created simultaneously, so there is no prior cavity.

Checkpoint

B) The star exists first, then the sphere forms around it
C) They come into existence simultaneously
D) The star is independent of the sphere

Key Takeaway
The simultaneity of stars and spheres is the core philosophical explanation for why stars remain fixed in their positions.

Third Response: Sphere's Composition from a Soul or Intellect
The author attributes the sphere's specific composition to an originating soul or intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third response to the objection about celestial specificities: the sphere's composition is mediated by a soul or intellect, preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The sphere's specific structure is explained by a mediating soul or intellect, not directly by God.

This lesson presents the third response from the author to the problem of particularity in the heavens. The response proposes that the sphere's detailed composition originates from an intermediate entity—a soul or intellect—which can entertain multiple volitions based on different conditions, unlike the First Principle which is beyond change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Let the argument's structure become clear through the translation.
Notice the contrast between the intermediary and the First Principle.
2
Identify the two entities
Identify the soul/intellect and the First Principle. Note their different capacities: the intermediary can have multiple volitions; the First Principle does not accept changing attributes.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this response is important for maintaining God's transcendence while explaining celestial particularity.
This shows an intermediary causal chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: لا يبعد أن يقال: إن تركيب جرمية الفلك، إنما صدر عن نفس أو عقل، ويكون ذلك الشيء قابلا للإرادات المختلفة بحسب الشرائط المختلفة، أما المبدأ الأول فإنه لا يقبل الصفات المتغيرة، فظهر الفرق.
The third aspect: It is not far-fetched to say that the composition of the sphere's corporeality originated from a soul or an intellect, and that thing is receptive to different volitions according to different conditions. As for the First Principle, it does not accept changing attributes. Thus the difference becomes clear.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This is the third response to the objection about specific star placements. The author suggests that the constitution (تركيب) of the sphere's corporeality (جرمية) emanates from a soul (نفس) or an intellect (عقل). This intermediary entity is capable of diverse volitions (إرادات) depending on varying conditions (شرائط). In contrast, the First Principle (المبدأ الأول)—which is God—does not accept changing attributes (صفات متغيرة). Therefore, it is reasonable that the sphere has specific properties determined by the soul or intellect, not by the immutable First Principle. This provides a metaphysical explanation for the particularity of celestial phenomena.

Key Terms
النفس
العقل
المبدأ الأول
الإرادات المختلفة
الصفات المتغيرة
التركيب

Key Takeaway

Fourth Objection: Differing Density in a Single Complement
Sanusi refutes the fourth objection about differing density by denying equal conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the two sides of a single celestial complement can differ in density by denying the equality of conditions.

Core Idea
Density variation is not a problem because conditions are not equal.

The fourth objection claims that a single sphere (complement) can have sides of different thickness, which would imply an inconsistency. The author first recalls that earlier responses to a similar objection about star location also apply here. Then he presents a new argument: the objection only works if the two sides are equal in all relevant conditions for thickness, and this condition is denied. This lesson unpacks that refutation and its consequence of impossible overlap.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic once to get the flow, even though it ends mid-sentence. Note the key terms: الغلظ والرقة, المتمم, الشرائط المعتبرة.
The text is cut off at 'لزم تداخل' – the argument continues beyond.
2
Identify the two parts of the refutation
The author first mentions that previous answers apply here. Then he gives a new answer. Identify where the new answer begins ('ثم ههنا جواب آخر').
3
Understand the conditional premise
The new answer states that the objection is valid only if each side equals the other in the conditions for thickness. This is the key conditional that the author will deny.
The phrase 'إنما يتم لو كان...' signals the condition.
4
Grasp the consequence of denial
The author denies that condition and explains why: if the thin side were assumed thick, overlapping (تداخل) would occur. Consider what 'تداخل' means – interpenetration of layers, which is impossible.
The full argument is cut off, but the main idea is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض الرابع: وهو اختلاف جانبي المتمم الواحد في الغلظ والرقة، فالأجوبة المذكورة في اختصاص الكوكب بالجانب المعين من الفلك عائدة فيه. ثم ههنا جواب آخر وهو: أن هذا السؤال إنما يتم لو كان كل واحد من جانبي المتمم، مساوياً للجانب الآخر في الشرائط المعتبرة، في حصول الغلظ والرقة. وهذا ممنوع. وذلك لأن الجانب الرقيق لو فرضناه غليظاً لزم تداخل

And this is denied. That is because if we were to assume the thin side to be thick, overlapping would necessarily occur...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth objection concerning the differing density (الغلظ والرقة) of the two sides of the same sphere (المتمم الواحد). The author first notes that the previously given responses to the third objection (about star location) also apply here. Then he adds a new answer: The objection only holds if the two sides are equal in all relevant conditions (شرائط معتبرة) for thickness and thinness. This condition is denied. He briefly explains that if the thin side were assumed to be thick, it would lead to overlapping (تداخل) – likely meaning the layers would interfere or penetrate each other, which is impossible. The argument is cut off, but the main refutation is clear: the objection's premise of equal conditions is false.

المتمم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The differing density of a sphere's sides indicates an inconsistency or impossibility in the celestial model.
Correction: The inconsistency only arises if conditions are equal; since they are not, differing density is unproblematic.

B) If the two sides are equal in all relevant conditions
C) If the sphere has stars
D) If the sphere is simple

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection fails because it incorrectly assumes that the two sides of a complement are equal in all conditions necessary for density.

Cosimultaneous Creation of Planets and Sphere
Author presents the second argument that planets and sphere are coexistent, preventing migration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that planets and the celestial sphere are created simultaneously, and why this prevents planetary migration.

Core Idea
Planets and sphere are born together; migration would tear the sky.

This passage presents the second argument in a series responding to an objection about why planets occupy specific positions. The author explains that planets are not inserted into a pre-existing sphere but arise together with it. This simultaneity eliminates the possibility of motion, as moving a planet would require tearing the sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the argument flow.
Notice the key terms that appear: الخرق والإلتئام, التجويف, مقارن.
2
Distinguish the two opposing views
The author contrasts a false view (sphere first hollow, then planets inserted) with the correct view (simultaneous creation). Understand why the false view is rejected.
The rejection is based on the impossibility of الخرق والإلتئام (rupture and rejoining).
3
Understand the consequence of simultaneity
Because the sphere did not pre-exist the planet, there is no basis for asking 'why here and not elsewhere?'. The location is fixed from the start.
4
Connect to impossibility of transfer
Once co-created, any transfer of a planet would necessitate tearing the sphere. Since that is impossible, planets remain fixed in their positions.
This argument relies on the cosmological principle that celestial spheres cannot suffer rupture.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument defends the observed fixed positions of planets against objections based on contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في الفرق : أن يقال : إن القوم لا يقولون : إن الفلك حصل فيه ذلك التجويف ، ثم حصلت الكواكب فيه ، لأن هذا يقتضي حصول الخرق والإلتئام على الفلك ، بل يقولون : حصول الكوكب مقارن لحصول الفلك ، فما كان الفلك حاصلا قبل حصول ذلك الكوكب حتى يقال ليس حصول الكوكب ههنا أولى من حصوله في موضع آخر ، ولما ثبت أن حصول الكوكب ، وحصول الفلك معاً ، فبعد ذلك يمتنع انتقال الكوكب من ذلك الموضع ، لأن تجويزه يوجب تجويز الخرق والالتئام على الفلك ، وأنه محال . فلهذا السبب بقي ذلك الكوكب في ذلك الوضع المعين من الفلك.
The second aspect of the difference: It is said that those people do not claim that the sphere acquired that cavity and then the planets occurred in it, because this would necessitate the occurrence of rupture and rejoining in the sphere. Rather, they say: The occurrence of the planet is simultaneous with the occurrence of the sphere. So the sphere was not existing before the occurrence of that planet such that it could be said that the occurrence of the planet here is not more appropriate than its occurrence in another place. And since it is established that the occurrence of the planet and the occurrence of the sphere are together, then after that the planet's transfer from that location is impossible, because allowing that would entail allowing rupture and rejoining in the sphere, and that is impossible. For this reason, that planet remained in that specific position of the sphere.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (الوجه الثاني) in a series of responses to an objection about why planets occupy particular positions in the celestial sphere. The author explains that the proponents (القوم) do not hold that the sphere first existed as a hollow body and then planets were placed inside it, because that would require the sphere to undergo rupture (الخرق) and rejoining (الإلتئام), which is impossible. Instead, they assert that the planet and the sphere come into existence simultaneously (مقارن). Since the sphere did not pre-exist the planet, there is no basis for asking why the planet is in one location rather than another. Once both exist together, the planet cannot move from that location because any such movement would again require rupture and rejoining of the sphere. Thus, the planet remains fixed in its specific position. The argument hinges on the impossibility of fission (الخرق) in the celestial sphere, a key principle of Aristotelian cosmology accepted by these thinkers.

Key Terms
الخرق والإلتئام
التجويف
مقارن
الوضع المعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that the celestial sphere existed first as an empty hollow structure and then planets were placed inside it at specific locations.
Correction: The argument here asserts that planets and sphere come into existence simultaneously, so there is no prior empty sphere. The planet's location is fixed from the start, and any movement would require tearing the sphere, which is considered impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that the sphere first existed as a hollow cavity and then planets were placed inside?
A) Because the planets were created separately and later inserted.
B) Because this would require rupture and rejoining in the sphere, which is impossible.
C) Because the sphere is eternal and cannot have a cavity.
D) Because the planets are made of a different substance.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of celestial rupture implies that planets must be co-eternal with their spheres, fixing their positions.

Third Aspect: Celestial Composition from a Receptive Soul or Intellect
Author presents the third argument that celestial composition originates from a soul or intellect capable of varied volitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the composition of celestial bodies is attributed to a soul or intellect to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Celestial diversity points to a changeable intermediary, not to the unchanging First Principle.

In this passage, the author presents the third argument that distinguishes the celestial realm from the First Principle. Instead of attributing celestial variations directly to God, he posits a soul or intellect that is receptive to multiple volitions. This preserves the doctrine of God's immutability while explaining observable celestial phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's concise statement sink in.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that the soul/intellect is described as 'receptive to different volitions according to different conditions,' whereas the First Principle 'does not accept changing attributes.' This contrast is central.
3
Reflect on the theological motive
Ask yourself why the author insists on this intermediary. The answer lies in safeguarding divine transcendence—God cannot change, so celestial variations must come from a changeable cause.
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : لا يبعد أن يقال : إن تركيب جرمية الفلك ، إنما صدر عن نفس أو عقل ، ويكون ذلك الشيء قابلا للإرادات المختلفة بحسب الشرائط المختلفة ، أما المبدأ الأول فإنه لا يقبل الصفات المتغيرة فظهر الفرق.
The third aspect: It is not far-fetched to say that the composition of the celestial body originates from a soul or intellect, and that thing is receptive to different volitions according to different conditions. As for the First Principle, it does not receive changing attributes. Thus the difference appears.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage provides the third argument (الوجه الثالث) distinguishing celestial phenomena from the First Principle. The author suggests that the composition (تركيب) of the celestial body (جرمية الفلك) originates from a soul or intellect (نفس أو عقل), not directly from the First Principle. This intermediary entity can receive multiple volitions (إرادات مختلفة) depending on varying conditions (الشرائط المختلفة). In contrast, the First Principle (المبدأ الأول)—God—does not accept changing attributes (الصفات المتغيرة). This explains why celestial bodies can exhibit diversity (e.g., planetary positions) without attributing change to the Divine. The argument reinforces divine transcendence (تنزيه) by assigning variability to a created intermediary.

The Moon's Two Sides: Why They Differ
Author refutes the objection about the moon's varying thickness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the varying thickness of the moon's sides is not arbitrary but necessary due to differing conditions.

Core Idea
The moon's sides cannot be swapped without violating physical impossibility.

The fourth objection claims that the moon, as a single complement, should have uniform thickness. But observation shows one side thicker. How can this be explained? Imam al-Razi offers two answers: first, earlier arguments about planetary allocation apply; second, a new argument focusing on the impossibility of swapping thickness and thinness due to interpenetration or void.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. First, note that the author mentions previous answers still apply. Then focus on the new, distinct answer he provides.
The phrase 'ثم ههنا جواب آخر' signals the new argument.
2
Understand the conditional premise
The objection would hold only if both sides had equal conditions for thickness. Recognize that this is the point of contention.
3
See the two impossible scenarios
If the thin side were thick, bodies would interpenetrate; if the thick side were thin, a void would appear. Both are impossible. This proves the conditions differ.
Interpenetration (تداخل الأجسام) and void (الخلاء) are key concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض الرابع : وهو اختلاف جانبي المتمم الواحد في الغلظ والرقة فالأجوبة المذكورة في اختصاص الكوكب بالجانب المعين من الفلك عائدة فيه . ثم ههنا جواب آخر وهو : أن هذا السؤال إنما يتم لو كان كل واحد من جانبي المتمم ، مساوياً للجانب الآخر في الشرائط المعتبرة ، في حصول الغلظ والرقة . وهذا ممنوع . وذلك لأن الجانب الرقيق لو فرضناه غليظاً لزم تداخل الأجسام ، والجانب الغليظ لو فرضناه رقيقاً لزم حصول الخلاء ، وكل ذلك محال . فثبت : أن الشرائط المعتيرة في حصول الغلظ والرقة مختلفة بحسب اختلاف الجانبين.
As for the fourth objection: it is the difference between the two sides of the single complement [the moon] in thickness and thinness. The answers mentioned concerning the particular allocation of the planet to a specific side of the sphere apply to it. Then here is another answer: This objection holds only if each one of the two sides of the complement were equal to the other side in the requisite conditions for the occurrence of thickness and thinness. But this is denied. That is because if we supposed the thin side to be thick, interpenetration of bodies would follow, and if we supposed the thick side to be thin, the occurrence of void would follow, and all of that is impossible. So it is established that the requisite conditions for the occurrence of thickness and thinness differ according to the difference of the two sides.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This lesson addresses the fourth objection (النقض الرابع) concerning the moon (referred to as 'المتمم الواحد', the single complement) and the differing thickness (غلظ) and thinness (رقة) of its two sides. The author first notes that the earlier answers about planetary allocation to specific sides of the sphere can also be applied here. Then he provides a distinct answer: the objection would only pose a problem if both sides were equally eligible for thickness and thinness (i.e., if the conditions were symmetric). But that is not the case; the conditions differ. If we imagine the thin side becoming thick, that would force interpenetration of bodies (تداخل الأجسام)—an impossibility. Conversely, if the thick side became thin, a void (الخلاء) would result—also impossible. Therefore, the conditions for thickness and thinness differ between the two sides, and the observed variation is necessary, not arbitrary.

Key Terms
المتمم
الغلظ والرقة
تداخل الأجسام
الخلاء
الشرائط المعتبرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author say would happen if the thin side of the moon were supposed to be thick?
A) The moon would become spherical.
B) Interpenetration of bodies would occur.
C) The void would appear.
D) The planet would move to another side.

Key Takeaway
The moon's two sides are not uniform because differing conditions make thickness and thinness necessary, and swapping them leads to logical impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed two situations are interchangeable without examining the underlying conditions, leading to a mistake? What is one area of your life where you need to stop assuming symmetry and instead investigate the unique conditions God has placed? Commit to doing that this week.
?
The author simply denies an assumption ('هذا ممنوع'). Are there foundational beliefs in your faith that you accept without evidence, but which are actually necessary? How would you respond to someone who challenges them? Prepare a defense rooted in the impossibility of the alternatives.
?
If even the physical properties of the moon have necessary, non-arbitrary conditions set by God, how much more do your spiritual obligations have necessary conditions? Are you trying to 'swap' an act of worship or a moral duty for something easier, thinking it interchangeable, when in reality it would lead to spiritual impossibility? Examine yourself and repent.

Fifth Objection: Convex vs Concave Surfaces
Author refutes objection using conditions to explain differing surfaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single nature can produce different effects due to varying conditions, and why the objection from convex/concave surfaces does not undermine the unity of nature.

Core Idea
A single nature produces different effects because conditions vary.

The objection claims that convex and concave surfaces have many different qualities, arguing that a single nature cannot produce such diversity. In this passage, the author refutes this by explaining that one nature necessitates its effect differently depending on the conditions. He uses the example of a sphere's exterior and interior to illustrate that conditions determine the specific form the nature takes. This lesson clarifies the role of conditions in causal necessitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage aloud, then follow the English translation carefully. Notice the structure: the objection is presented first, then the author's response begins with 'نقول' (We say).
The Arabic uses concise philosophical terminology; the translation helps clarify the meaning.
2
Identify the conditions mentioned
The author gives a concrete example: the exterior (ظاهر) and interior (باطن) of a sphere. Notice how each accepts a different type of surface (convex wide vs concave small). These are the 'conditions' (الشرائط) that vary.
The word 'الشرائط' is plural of 'شرط' (condition). This is key to the argument.
3
Understand the principle
The author states that even if the nature (الطبيعة) is one, its necessitation (إيجابها) for its effect differs according to the conditions. This means the same nature can produce different effects when conditions differ.
This principle is central to many debates in Islamic theology about causality.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this defend the unity of nature? The answer is that the objection fails because it expects identical effects from a single nature, but the author shows that conditions mediate the effects, so diversity is expected, not problematic.
This lesson guards against the misconception that a unified cause must always produce identical effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض الخامس : وهو قولهم : السطح المحدب مخالف للسطح المقعر في الصفات الكثيرة . فنقول : الطبيعة وإن كانت واحدة إلا أن إيجابها لمعلولها ، يختلف بحسب اختلاف الشرائط . فظاهر الكرة ، لا يقبل إلا السطح المحدب الواسع ، وباطنها لا يقبل إلا السطح المقعر الصغير . ولما اختلفت الشرائط لم يبعد اختلاف الأثر .
As for the fifth objection: it is their saying: The convex surface differs from the concave surface in many qualities. We say: The nature, even if it is one, yet its necessitation for its effect differs according to the difference of conditions. For the exterior of the sphere accepts only the wide convex surface, and its interior accepts only the small concave surface. And since the conditions differ, it is not unlikely that the effect differs.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This lesson addresses the fifth objection (النقض الخامس): the claim that the convex surface (السطح المحدب) of a sphere and its concave surface (السطح المقعر) have many different qualities, which might be taken as evidence against a unified nature. The author responds by affirming that the nature (الطبيعة) is indeed one, but its causal necessitation (إيجابها لمعلولها) varies according to conditions (الشرائط). For instance, the exterior of a sphere can only have a wide convex surface, while the interior can only have a small concave surface. Since the conditions differ, it is entirely reasonable for the resulting effects (الأثر)—the surfaces—to differ. Thus, the objection fails to undermine the unity of nature.

Key Terms
السطح المحدب
السطح المقعر
الطبيعة
إيجابها
الشرائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single nature must always produce identical effects
Correction: Conditions mediate the effects, allowing diversity while nature remains one.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do the convex and concave surfaces of a sphere differ?
A) Because they have different natures.
B) Because the conditions for each surface are different.
C) Because the sphere is composed of multiple elements.
D) Because the surfaces are accidental and not essential.

Key Takeaway
One nature does not necessitate identical effects; conditions shape the specific outcome.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Where are you ignoring the different conditions Allah has placed in your life, leading you to expect identical outcomes? How can you trust that His wisdom works through diverse conditions without doubting His oneness?

The author uses a physical example to illustrate a deeper truth. Can you identify an area of your faith or life where you need to look at the 'conditions' rather than simply blaming the 'nature'? What would it mean for you to submit to Allah's wisdom in the diversity of His creation and commands?

Sixth Objection: Part in Depth vs Part on Surface
Author refutes the sixth objection by invoking the sphere's unity and the impossibility of rupture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why hypothetical parts of the celestial sphere cannot be interchanged despite sharing the same essence, according to the philosophers.

Core Idea
Shared essence does not imply interchangeability when the whole's form prevents separation.

In this lesson, we address the sixth objection which claims that a part in the depth of the celestial sphere and a part on its surface, sharing the same quiddity, could be swapped. The author refutes this by explaining the nature of the celestial sphere as a single continuum and the impossibility of rupture.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is stated and then answered.
2
Understand the philosophers' view
The author states that for the philosophers, the celestial sphere is a single continuum (جسم متصل واحد) and not composed of parts. Understand why this is fundamental.
3
Identify the role of external causes
External causes like illusion and varying attractions can give rise to conceptual parts (أجزاء بالفعل) only after the whole exists. Note that these parts are not actual separate entities.
4
Grasp why interchange is impossible
The form of the whole prevents the hypothetical parts from separating, because rupture (الخرق) in the spheres is impossible. This is the key reason for the interchangeability being impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض السادس : وهو قولهم : ( الجزء الحاصل في ثخن الفلك ، مشارك للجزء الحاصل في سطحه ، في تمام ماهيته ) فنقول : الجواب عنه أن يقال : هذا السؤال غير لازم ، على مذهب الحكماء . وذلك لأن عندهم جسم الفلك متصل واحد ، وهو غير مركب من الأجزاء أصلاً ، بل هو في نفسه شيء واحد، كما أنه عند الحس شيء واحد، إلا أنه قد يعرض للجسم المتصل أسباب خارجية فيحصل بسبب تلك الأسباب الخارجية أجزاء بالفعل كالوهم واختلاف المجاذبات، إلا أن على هذا التقدير فالجزء يفترض بعد حصول الكل ، لكن حصول صورة الكل لذلك الجسم الذي هو الكل ، يمنع من انفصال بعض تلك الأجزاء عن بعض، لما ثبت أن الخرق على الأفلاك : محال . فلهذا السبب امتنع أن يحصل الجزء المفترض في عمق الفلك في سطحه ، وبالعكس.
As for the sixth objection: it is their saying: 'The part occurring in the thickness of the sphere shares with the part occurring on its surface in its complete quiddity.' We say: The answer to it is to say: This objection is not necessary, according to the doctrine of the philosophers. This is because, according to them, the celestial body is a single continuum, and it is not composed of parts at all; rather, it is in itself one thing, just as it is one thing in sensation. However, there may occur to the continuous body external causes, and due to those external causes, actual parts come to be, like illusion and the difference of attractions. But on this assumption, the part is supposed after the occurrence of the whole. Yet the occurrence of the form of the whole for that body which is the whole prevents the separation of some of those parts from others, because it is established that rupture in the spheres is impossible. For this reason, it is impossible for the supposed part in the depth of the sphere to occur on its surface, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This lesson addresses the sixth objection (النقض السادس): the claim that a hypothetical part located in the thickness (ثخن) of the celestial sphere and a part on its surface share the same essence (ماهية), so they should be interchangeable. The author refutes this by referencing the doctrine of the philosophers (الحكماء). According to them, the celestial sphere is a single continuous body (جسم متصل واحد), not composed of discrete parts. It is one in itself and in sensory perception. However, under certain external causes (أسباب خارجية) such as illusion (الوهم) or varying attractions (اختلاف المجاذبات), parts may be conceptually distinguished (أجزاء بالفعل). But crucially, these parts are only supposed after the whole (بعد حصول الكل) already exists. The form of the whole (صورة الكل) prevents these hypothetical parts from separating—since rupture (الخرق) in the spheres is impossible. Therefore, the part in the depth cannot transfer to the surface, and vice versa. The impossibility of fission in celestial spheres blocks the interchangeability that the objection assumes.

Key Terms
ثخن الفلك
ماهية
جسم متصل
أجزاء بالفعل
الوهم
اختلاف المجاذبات
الخرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two hypothetical parts share the same essence, they can be interchanged.
Correction: The philosophers argue that the form of the whole and the impossibility of rupture prevent any actual swapping of parts, even if they share essence.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can't a hypothetical part in the depth of the sphere move to the surface?
A) Because the part is not really a part but only a concept.
B) Because the sphere is composed of many small parts that are interlocked.
C) Because the form of the whole prevents separation, as rupture in the sphere is impossible.
D) Because the part in the depth has a different essence from the surface part.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of rupture in the celestial sphere prevents the hypothetical interchange of parts, despite any shared essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated a part of your life—like a single sin or a single good deed—as separable from who you are as a whole, only to realize later that the form of your entire being prevents that isolation? How does this lesson's argument about the form of the whole resonate with your experience?
?
When you assume that two actions or beliefs are interchangeable because they share a similar essence, do you risk ignoring the unique context and unity that constrain them? Recall a specific situation and consider how the impossibility of 'rupture' in your own commitments might prevent you from swapping them carelessly.
?
This lesson shows that the celestial sphere remains one despite conceptual parts. In your relationship with Allah, have you fragmented your worship into disconnected parts, forgetting that the form of Tawheed should unify them? What would it mean for you to live as a single continuum of submission?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
One Nature, Different Conditions
Al-Razi answers the fifth objection about convex and concave surfaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi defends the unity of nature against the objection that convex and concave surfaces have different attributes.

Core Idea
One nature can produce different effects under different conditions.

Al-Razi faces an objection: if a sphere has a single nature, why do its convex and concave surfaces have many different attributes? He answers by introducing the concept of conditions (shara'it). This passage shows how a single cause can yield different results when conditions vary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection presented by the opponents: convex and concave surfaces differ in many attributes despite coming from the same spherical nature.
Notice that the objection assumes that a single nature should produce identical effects.
2
Understand Al-Razi's answer
Read Al-Razi's response: nature is one, but its causation differs according to the difference of conditions. He then gives the example of the sphere's exterior and interior.
The key insight is that conditions (shara'it) modify how a nature operates.
3
Reflect on the role of conditions
Consider how the exterior and interior of a sphere constitute different conditions. Ask yourself: what would happen if the conditions were the same?
This shows that attributing different effects to different conditions is rational and does not contradict the unity of nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض الخامس : وهو قولهم : السطح المحدب مخالف للسطح المقعر في الصفات الكثيرة . فنقول : الطبيعة وإن كانت واحدة إلا أن إيجابها لمعلولها ، يختلف بحسب اختلاف الشرائط . فظاهر الكرة ، لا يقبل إلا السطح [المحدب] الواسع ، وباطنها لا يقبل [إلا] السطح المقعر ، الصغير . ولما اختلفت الشرائط لم يبعد اختلاف الأثر .
As for the fifth objection: their saying that the convex surface differs from the concave surface in many attributes. We say: nature, even if it is one, its causation of its effect differs according to the difference of conditions. Thus the exterior of the sphere accepts only the wide convex surface, and its interior accepts only the small concave surface. And when the conditions differ, it is not unlikely that the effect differs.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. Al-Razi presents the fifth objection: the convex and concave surfaces of a sphere have many different attributes, yet they come from the same spherical nature. How can one nature produce two different surfaces? Al-Razi's answer is that the same nature can produce different effects when the conditions (shara'it) differ. The exterior of the sphere, due to its position and relation, necessarily gives rise to a convex surface that is wide, while the interior gives rise to a concave surface that is small. Since the conditions are different, it is not improbable that the effects differ. Thus the objection is answered by appealing to the variation of conditions.

Key Terms
السطح المحدب
السطح المقعر
الشرائط
الطبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single nature or cause must always produce the same effect regardless of conditions.
Correction: The same nature can produce different effects when conditions differ, as demonstrated by the convex and concave surfaces of a sphere.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi's answer to the fifth objection, why do the convex and concave surfaces differ?
A) Because the sphere has two different natures.
B) Because the conditions (shara'it) for each surface are different.
C) Because the concave surface is smaller than the convex.
D) Because the convex surface is accidental.

Key Takeaway
A single nature can produce different effects when the conditions under which it operates vary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you blamed your 'nature' for a failing, when in reality the conditions you placed yourself in were to blame? Will you now examine the conditions you choose rather than making excuses about who you are?
?
If a single nature can yield both convex and concave surfaces based on conditions, what does that mean for your potential? Are you limiting yourself because you accept that you are 'naturally' a certain way, ignoring the power of changing your circumstances?
?
Al-Razi points out that differing conditions make differing effects not unlikely. Where in your life have you assumed that a change in your character is impossible, when it is merely a change of condition that is needed? How will you change one condition this week to produce a different effect?
